The security guard offered his customary wave, the elevator brought him smoothly to the thirty-second floor, and his desk welcomed him with the stack of materials he'd organized Friday evening.
The investment portfolio he'd been analyzing required final adjustments before submission to the client. Jay worked through the calculations, double-checking his conclusions with the source documents. The spreadsheet error from the previous week had taught him valuable lessons about verification.
Ian arrived at eight, offering his customary greeting before disappearing into his office. Jay had grown accustomed to Ian's courtesy, though he felt an obvious distance in their interactions. Ian was fair and supportive when Jay needed guidance, but his manner indicated he was monitoring not just Jay's performance but his overall conduct in the office.
Jay didn't mind the scrutiny. He'd expected it, given his modest credentials. Earning Ian's complete trust would take time, and Jay was prepared to demonstrate his reliability through consistent competence.
On Wednesday, Jay made an unexpected discovery. The team’s lunch rotation had landed on Willow & Sage, a cosy bistro not far from the office that he had never noticed during his previous explorations of the neighborhood. The interior was warmly lit and comfortable, with plush sofas arranged around low tables and a menu that emphasized artisanal ingredients.
Jay ordered a sandwich made with herb-crusted bread and filled with vegetables he couldn't  identify but found delicious. The price was higher than his lunch budget allowed, but the quality justified the expense. More importantly, the bistro was less crowded than their normal destinations, creating space for conversation without the press of neighboring tables and competing noise.
"This place is great," Jay said, sitting on one of the armchairs with his sandwich. "I can't believe I walked past it so many times without noticing."
"It's been here for about a year," Amy explained. "The owner used to work at some fancy place downtown before opening this spot."
Jay took another bite, savouring the complex flavours and textures. The herb bread was dense but not heavy, offering a complexity that worked in harmony with the filling instead of overshadowing it. "It's really good. I might have to come back here on my own sometime."
His enthusiasm seemed to resonate with the group. Luc smiled approvingly, and even Kim looked up from his phone to agree that the food was good. Jay realized he'd found something valuable—a lunch spot that felt relaxing. Having a place in the neighbourhood that felt welcoming would make the daily lunch outings less stressful.
Thursday afternoon, Jay decided to explore the area around Northlight Financial Center more thoroughly. He'd been working there for almost three weeks but had seen little beyond the direct route between the office and various restaurants. The financial district revealed itself to be more varied than he'd initially realized, with small parks tucked between the imposing towers.
A short walk north from the office, Jay discovered a pocket park that seemed to exist in defiance of its surroundings. Trees provided shade over benches and walking paths, while a small fountain created a buffer against the constant traffic noise. Office workers occupied some of the benches, but the space wasn't crowded and felt peaceful.
Jay walked the perimeter of the park. He probably wouldn't have many opportunities to spend time here since his schedule was tightly structured around office hours and team lunches, but knowing the space existed felt reassuring. If he ever needed a few minutes to collect his thoughts or simply escape the office life, this place would be available.
Friday morning brought news that would reshape the team's dynamics for the foreseeable future. Ela arrived with updates about Max, the senior analyst who'd been on vacation.
"Max got some kind of tropical bug," Ela explained during their morning coffee break. "Nothing serious, but he's stuck in quarantine for another week, and then he wants to extend his trip to recuperate. He won't be back for at least three more weeks."
"Poor guy," Luc said, though his tone indicated limited sympathy. "Stuck on a tropical for an extra month. The hardships we endure."
Jay had heard Max mentioned frequently. Senior analyst, four years with the company, known for taking elaborate vacations and producing exceptional client reports. His extended absence had created additional work for everyone, particularly Ian, who had been covering Max's client relationships while managing his regular responsibilities.
"We're lucky Jay was hired when he was," Amy observed. "The workload would have been impossible to manage without the extra help."
"Definitely," Ela agreed. "Jay's been handling twice the normal workload for a new hire, and doing it well."
Jay felt a flush of pride at the recognition, though he tried not to let it show. His colleagues' approval mattered more than he'd expected. These people had welcomed him into their community and now openly acknowledged his contributions. 
In the afternoon, Luc came with the weekly invitation to after-work drinks. Jay had been expecting the question and had already prepared his response.
"I appreciate the invitation," Jay said, maintaining the apologetic tone that had worked for previous refusals. "I can't make it tonight, but if you're planning to go out next week, I'd like to join you then."
The answer clearly surprised the group. Luc's eyebrows rose slightly, and Amy looked pleased by the prospect of Jay's participation.
"Absolutely," Amy said. "We hit Murphy's or somewhere similar on Friday afternoons. Nothing fancy, just a chance to unwind after the week."
"We can't wait," Ela added. "It'll be fun to get to know you outside of work mode."
Jay managed what he hoped was an enthusiastic smile. "I'm looking forward to it."
The commitment felt both necessary and terrifying. Jay understood that continued refusal to participate in social activities would eventually mark him as uninterested in building relationships with his colleagues. But the prospect of spending an evening in a crowded bar created anxiety that he'd need an additional time to process.
Still, it was a manageable concession. One Friday evening every now and then seemed like a reasonable price to pay for workplace acceptance. If he could master the complex calculations and industry knowledge required for his job, he could probably learn to get through after-work socializing as well.
Scene 2
Offering input
Three weeks at Turner & Associates had taught Jay the patterns that governed office life. Mondays were focused, as if everyone was calibrating their approach to the week ahead. By Tuesday, the pace would intensify. Wednesday brought the week's heaviest workload. Thursday was for refinement and preparation. Friday offered the gentle deceleration toward weekend freedom.
As he started his fourth week, Jay had learned to match these rhythms, adjusting his own schedule to complement the team's collective momentum. He submitted reports early in the week when Ian had time for detailed review. He saved less important correspondence for Thursday afternoons when the office atmosphere was more relaxed. He prepared materials for the following week on Friday mornings.
The company structure also became clearer with each passing day. With just under a hundred employees total, the organization felt almost intimate. Jay's team held the central position, managing relationships with the firm's crown jewel accounts while three smaller teams handled the bread-and-butter contracts. Jay had already committed every client name to memory—a manageable feat given the list contained fewer than twenty entries. The top four clients received treatment bordering on reverence, and Jay had taken to studying their profiles each evening at his kitchen table, their key details arranged on his refrigerator like a shrine.
Ian remained distant but fair. He never asked anything of Jay that wasn’t strictly work related. Jay didn’t make his coffee or sort out any personal affairs. Their daily interactions followed predictable patterns—brief meetings to review assignments, quick consultations about client requirements, occasional corrections.
Jay kept observing Ian, how he handled client calls with unfailing composure, the way he managed tight deadlines with a calmness that never put undue pressure on the team. Everything about Ian demonstrated excellence Jay had only imagined possible. His previous bosses had been either erratic and demanding or inappropriate. Ian was everything these men weren’t: competent, respectful, skilled at his work.
The admiration felt dangerous somehow, too intense for a workplace relationship. Jay worked to keep his composure when Ian explained financial strategies, fought to suppress the eager questions that bubbled up during their meetings. He told himself this was simple respect, nothing more complicated than recognizing superior competence. But he knew it was more than that. It was happiness at being mentored by someone of Ian’s caliber. He felt lucky to have been given this opportunity.
On Thursday morning Ian appeared at Jay's desk with Amy, the senior analyst whose sharp intelligence intimidated most people in the office.
"We have a strategy session in fifteen minutes," Ian said. "Conference room B. I'd like you to join us."
Jay tried to hide his nervousness. "Should I bring anything specific?"
"The Wentworth portfolio files. Amy's been reviewing their risk exposure, and I want a fresh perspective on the client communications we've been handling."
"Have you reviewed the quarterly reports?" Amy asked.
"Yes. I've been tracking the correspondence about their concerns regarding the tech sector allocation."
"Good. That's exactly what we need to discuss." 
The conference room felt exclusive, from the thick carpet underfoot to the weighty glass decanters set out on a sideboard. Jay sat across from Amy and Ian at the head of a long table of dark, gleaming oak. Nervousness crept up his spine—the same feeling he'd experienced during college presentations, multiplied by the stakes of this environment.
Amy opened a thick folder. "Wentworth's been questioning our technology sector weighting for three months. Their returns have been solid, but they're spooked by market volatility."
Ian turned to a page of charts. "The numbers support our current strategy, but client confidence matters more than being right if they pull their investment. What's their specific concern?" 
Amy looked up from her notes. "They think we're overexposed. Twenty-three percent in tech when the market average is eighteen."
"But their risk tolerance profile said they wanted aggressive growth," Jay said, remembering the client files he read.
Ian paused, his pen halfway to the paper. "Go on."
Heat rose in Jay's face. Both of them were watching him now, waiting. "I just—when I was organizing their files, I noticed they specifically requested higher-return strategies. They seemed willing to accept volatility for growth potential."
"That was six months ago," Amy said. "Market conditions change. Client comfort levels change."
"Right, but..." Jay felt himself fumbling, his thoughts scattered under their scrutiny. "The correspondence I've been handling—they're not asking us to reduce risk. They're asking for reassurance about our decision-making process. They want to understand why we believe in the allocation, not necessarily for us to change it."
Ian set down his pen entirely. "Explain that."
"Their emails don't request portfolio adjustments. They ask questions about our research methodology, about how we evaluate tech companies versus traditional sectors. It reads like they want education, not modification."
Amy flipped through several pages. "He's right. Look at this—'Can you explain your analysis of cloud computing market projections?' Not 'Should we reduce our exposure to cloud computing companies?'"
"So they need confidence in our expertise, not a different strategy," Ian said.
Jay's pulse hammered in his ears. "Maybe. I could be wrong. I'm just going by the tone of their communications."
Ian made a note on his pad. "That's useful insight. Amy, what do you think about addressing their questions directly instead of proposing portfolio changes?"
"It makes sense. Less disruptive, and it builds the relationship and doesn't look like we made mistakes initially."
They spent another twenty minutes developing a response strategy. Jay contributed when asked but mostly listened, absorbing how they analyzed problems from multiple angles, how they balanced client psychology with financial logic.
When the meeting ended, Ian gathered his papers. "Good observation, Jay. Sometimes the obvious solution isn't the right one."
Amy packed up her folder. "Not bad for someone who's been here three weeks."
After they left, Jay remained in the conference room, staring out at the city skyline. His hands still trembled slightly from the adrenaline. He'd managed to contribute something worthwhile, hadn't completely embarrassed himself. Ian had acknowledged his input and treated his reasoning as legitimate.
The afternoon continued with standard tasks, but Jay kept returning to those moments in the conference room. The way Ian had listened to his explanation, had built on his suggestion instead of dismissing it. He'd worked for his previous boss for three years and never once felt that his ideas mattered. Ian had given him that feeling in a single meeting.
By the time he left work, Jay had replayed the conversation a dozen times. Part of him burned with shame at how he'd stumbled through his explanation. But a larger part felt something he hadn't experienced in years: the joy of being heard, of contributing meaningfully to something important. Ian had asked for his perspective and actually listened to the answer. In Jay's limited experience, that was extraordinary.
Scene 3
Going out
On Friday afternoon, talk of weekend plans filled the office. Jay had promised Luc he would join them this time. For weeks, he'd declined their invitations, citing other arrangements. The excuses were mostly true but incomplete. The real reason was simpler: he didn't drink much and didn't enjoy crowded bars.
But Luc had been persistent, and Amy had mentioned that the Friday tradition helped new employees feel included. Jay had finally agreed, but the commitment sat uneasily with him all week—he'd even spent some time Thursday evening practicing conversation topics in his bathroom mirror, then felt ridiculous about it.
The bar they chose throbbed with music and conversation. Exposed brick walls absorbed none of the sound, and the crowd pressed close around high tables. Jay arrived ten minutes late, hoping the others would already be deep in conversation and his entrance would go unnoticed.
"There he is!" Kim called out, raising a beer bottle. "We were starting to think you'd chickened out."
"Just had trouble finding the place," Jay said, which was partially true. He'd circled the building twice, gathering courage.
Kim pushed a beer toward him. "First round's on the company account.”
Jay accepted the bottle gratefully. Something to do with his hands. The beer was cold and tasted better than he'd expected, but it still sat untouched after the first few sips—the bitter flavor reminded him of college parties where he'd stood awkwardly against walls.
"So Amy's been here longest, right?" Jay asked during a lull in conversation.
"Eight years," Amy confirmed.
"Amy basically runs the analytical side of everything," Ela added. "Ian trusts her judgment completely."
Kim raised his glass. "To Amy, who makes the rest of us look competent."
The conversation flowed around him like water around a stone. Ela told stories about difficult clients who called at impossible hours. Amy shared gossip about acquisitions in their sector. Kim complained about outdated computer systems. Jay listened, laughed when appropriate, asked occasional questions.
"What about you, Jay?" Amy asked during a brief pause. "What did you do before Turner & Associates?"
"Administrative work. Nothing interesting."
"Come on," Kim pressed. "Everyone has stories from terrible jobs. Where was this?"
"A small consulting firm. Really, it wasn't worth mentioning."
Jay took another sip of his beer, hoping they'd move on. They did, but he caught Luc watching him.
The evening progressed casually. Sometimes the group clustered together, trading inside jokes and references Jay didn't understand. Other times conversations split into pairs, and Jay  talked with whoever happened to be nearest. The music made everything feel slightly surreal, like watching a movie of normal people having normal fun.
"You know who never comes to these things?" Ela said, sittling back on her chair after returning from the bar. "Zoe. Sweet girl, but she's practically invisible at the office."
"She's just shy," Amy said. "Like Jay here."
Jay felt heat rise in his face. "I'm not really shy. Just prefer smaller groups."
"Nothing wrong with that," Kim said. "Some people need more processing time."
"Speaking of people who never show up," Amy said, grinning at something over Jay's shoulder. "Anyone want to bet on whether we could get Ian to join us sometime?"
Jay's attention sharpened. "Does he not like these kinds of places?"
"Ian doesn't do informal gatherings," Amy said. "Too old-fashioned for after-work drinks."
"He's never come? Not even once?" Jay asked.
Kim and Luc exchanged a quick look that Jay missed entirely. "Nope. Not his style."
"Maybe he has other commitments," Jay said. "Family obligations or something."
"Ian doesn't have family obligations," Ela said with a laugh. "Trust me, his calendar is work, gym, sleep, repeat."
"He's married to the job," Kim added. "Which works out great for the rest of us since he's basically perfect at it."
Jay nodded, filing away this information. It seemed sad somehow, that someone as competent and respected as Ian would isolate himself so completely. Maybe success required that kind of sacrifice.
By nine-thirty, the noise and crowd had worn Jay down to exhaustion. His beer had grown warm  and his social energy felt completely depleted. He waited for a natural break in conversation.
"I should probably head home," he said, standing and reaching for his jacket. "Early morning tomorrow."
"It's Friday night!" Kim protested. "Saturday morning doesn't count as early."
"I know, but I'm not much of a night owl. Thanks for including me, though. This was nice."
"We'll do this again soon," Amy said. "Maybe somewhere quieter next time."
Luc stood as well. "Actually, I should get going too. Mind if I walk with you to the metro?"
They said their goodbyes and stepped into the cooler evening air. The contrast felt wonderful after the bar's stuffiness.
"Not your scene?" Luc asked as they walked.
"Not really. But I'm glad I came. Everyone's been very welcoming."
"The team's good people. Takes time to find your place with any group."
They walked in silence for a while. Luc seemed to understand that Jay needed time to decompress.
"Leo hired me under similar circumstances," Luc said eventually. "Gave me a chance when I really needed one. Sometimes the best opportunities come from unexpected places."
Jay glanced at him. "Similar circumstances?"
"Oh, you know. Right person at the right time. Lucky break."
"Well, I'm thankful he took a chance on me."
"Leo's good at seeing potential in people."
They reached the metro entrance. "Thanks for the company," Jay said. "And for all the help with office procedures these past weeks. I know I've asked a lot of questions."
"No problem. We all need guidance starting out."
They parted ways at the turnstiles, Luc heading north while Jay descended to the southbound platform. The train was nearly empty, the Friday night crowd having dispersed to their various destinations.
Jay found a seat and closed his eyes, letting the gentle rocking of the car ease the tension in his shoulders. The evening had been more draining than he'd expected, but not unpleasant. He'd learned things about his colleagues that would make Monday morning conversations easier. They'd accepted his early departure without making him feel guilty or antisocial.
Most importantly, he'd demonstrated that he could handle these situations. He didn't have to be the most social person in the room or the most entertaining conversationalist. He just had to show up, participate honestly, and be himself.
The detail about Ian never joining after-work gatherings felt important, though Jay couldn't articulate why. It reinforced his impression of Ian as the type to draw a clear line between his job and his private affairs. Jay respected that approach, even admired it. It seemed clean, uncomplicated.
By the time he reached his apartment, Jay felt the particular tiredness that came from successful social effort. He'd pushed himself outside his comfort zone and survived. Tomorrow he could retreat into solitude to recharge, but tonight he could feel proud.
Scene 4
Office culture
Jay spent the weekend in his apartment, doing absolutely nothing productive. The quiet felt restorative after Friday's social experiment. He made simple meals, organized his closet, and made no plans beyond basic maintenance. Wasting time felt earned after nearly a month of devoting all his free hours to work materials. By Sunday evening, he was ready to return to work.
On Monday, Jay sat at his desk knowing exactly how his day would unfold. He operated the office coffee maker without fumbling with the settings, selecting his preferred medium roast blend. He grabbed a package of artisanal trail mix with dried cherries and dark chocolate pieces from the employee kitchen. Four weeks ago, he'd packed sad sandwiches and plain fruits. Now he trusted the office provisions. The change from nervous newcomer to functioning team member had happened gradually, almost without his notice.
His desk had become truly his. The monitor was adjusted to the perfect height. His supplies were organized according to his preferences. The client files were arranged in the system that made sense to his workflow.
These changes felt monumental to him, even if they appeared trivial to everyone else. The casual abundance of quality food, the assumption that employees deserved good coffee, the unstated message that his comfort mattered—all of it represented a standard of life he'd only imagined.
The week progressed normally, updating client contact databases, preparing materials for  portfolio reviews, responding to correspondence about account maintenance. Jay moved through the tasks with growing confidence, no longer triple-checking every word before sending the email. 
On Thursday, Ian appeared at Jay's desk with a folder in hand. Jay expected a brief exchange—assignment delivery and quick clarification.
Instead, Ian paused after handing over the documents. "I wanted to mention that your work on the Wentworth’s case has been excellent. Your attention to the nuances in their concerns made a real difference in how we approached the situation."
Jay looked up, startled by the unexpected praise. "I just tried to read what they were actually asking for."
"Exactly. That kind of analysis is valuable. The client responded very positively to our revised approach."
"Thank you," Jay managed, feeling heat rise in his face.
Throughout the day, Ian made similar rounds. He paused at Amy's desk to acknowledge her risk assessment insights. He thanked Kim for resolving a technical issue ahead of schedule. He complimented Ela on her handling of a difficult client.
The pattern revealed something Jay hadn't fully appreciated before. Ian didn't just manage through assignments and deadlines. He noticed good work and took time to recognize it. The praise wasn't dramatic, but it was honest.
Jay watched these interactions with growing admiration. Ian's leadership style created an atmosphere where people wanted to excel, not because they feared criticism but because they valued his respect. The office culture that had impressed Jay from his first day stemmed largely from Ian's approach. This was true leadership, the kind Jay had read about but never experienced. Ian had built a team that functioned at the highest level.
The warm feeling persisted throughout the afternoon. The praise felt less personal now, but just as meaningful. Jay had been specifically noticed for quality he took pride in. His work met Ian's exacting standards. The clear expectations and recognition felt like paradise.
By late afternoon, Jay was organizing files when Leo appeared, perching casually on the edge of his desk.
"How's the first month treating you?"
"Very well. Better than I expected, honestly."
"Good to hear. Ian mentioned you're fitting in nicely."
Jay felt another flutter of excitement. Ian had discussed his performance with Leo. Positively, apparently.
"Everyone's been helpful. Especially Luc with the procedural questions."
"Luc's good people. So are you settling into the office? Finding your place with the team?"
"I think so. Still learning, but yes."
Leo chatted for a few more minutes about Jay's workload and comfort level, then wandered off toward Ian's office. After Leo departed, Jay realized he rarely saw him in the office during regular hours. Leo's appearances seemed sporadic, almost social. The contrast with everyone else's structured schedules struck him as odd.
When Luc passed by later, Jay seized the opportunity for clarification. "Can I ask you about Leo?"
"Sure."
"Is he here regularly? I don't see him most days."
Luc glanced around to ensure privacy, then lowered his tone. "Leo's situation is unique. His father owns the company, so Leo has a position but not traditional responsibilities. He consults on special projects, handles client relationships, but he's not a regular employee like the rest of us."
"I knew about his background, but I never considered the implications."
"Leo doesn't make a big deal of it. He's not pretentious about the family connection. But yeah, he's basically company royalty."
"Does he have a lot of influence here?"
"Absolutely. Ian respects his judgment, and ultimately Leo has his father's ear on major decisions."
Jay thought about Leo's relaxed demeanor, his friendly check-ins, the way he'd casually offered Jay this position. The equality Jay had assumed between them was an illusion. Leo was essentially a king in this environment, choosing to be approachable.
"Thanks for explaining that."
"No problem. Leo's good about not pulling rank, but it's useful to understand the actual hierarchy."
Jay gained new perspective on his place in the office ecosystem. Leo's interest in his welfare wasn't just collegial friendship but something closer to noblesse oblige. The realization didn't diminish his gratitude, but it added complexity to their interactions.
As the day ended, Jay thought back on the month that had passed. He'd found stability,  meaningful work and competent leadership. The team functioned smoothly, supporting each other without drama or politics.
Jay had spent years imagining what it would be like to truly enjoy his job. This was better than his projections—not just the absence of problems, but quality in every aspect of the work environment.
His admiration for Ian had only deepened with this understanding. Creating and maintaining this kind of workplace culture required skill and commitment to the people under your supervision. Ian managed to be demanding without being unreasonable. Jay felt like he belonged somewhere worth belonging to.
Scene 5
Compensation
Expand savings fragment
Jay was reviewing the day's schedule when his phone buzzed with a bank notification. Monthly salary transfer complete. He glanced at the screen, then stared at the direct deposit notification, certain he was misreading the numbers. He opened his banking app to confirm the amount, then closed it and opened it again. The figure remained unchanged.
The base salary was exactly what his contract had specified—nearly double his previous income. But the total deposit was almost triple his old paycheck. A bonus had been added to his monthly pay, far exceeding the modest additional compensation he'd half-expected.
He found Luc in the break room, refilling his coffee cup.
"Can I ask you something?" Jay kept his tone low. "About the paycheck structure?"
"Sure. What's up?"
"The bonus amount. Is this typical?"
Luc smiled. "First month, right? Yeah, we get monthly bonuses as standard. Then quarterly bonuses if the fund performs well, which it normally does."
"Every month?"
"Every month. Part of the compensation package. Didn't Leo explain that during your interview?"
"He mentioned bonuses were possible. I didn't realize they were guaranteed."
"Welcome to working somewhere that values talent properly." Luc raised his coffee cup in a mock toast. "Beats scrambling for overtime pay, doesn't it?"
During lunch, he excused himself early from the restaurant. "Need to handle some personal business," he explained, which was technically true. He spent the rest of his lunch break running calculations on his phone. Rent, utilities, groceries, transportation, expenses that had consumed his previous salary, now represented a fraction of his income. His old job had required budgeting for every purchase. This paycheck eliminated financial anxiety entirely.
He could afford to indulge without calculating the weekly impact. The artisanal sandwiches, the sushi place around the corner, the Mediterranean restaurant the team frequented—all of these became reasonable options.
That afternoon, Jay walked to Café Lemon, a fancy coffee shop close to his apartment. He'd discovered it during his first month of living in the area, drawn by the rich aroma of their house-roasted beans and the warm atmosphere created by mismatched vintage furniture. For years, he'd allowed himself at most two cappuccinos per month, savoring it like a small celebration.
He'd stopped coming a month ago when work lunches began consuming too much of his budget. The barista recognized him despite his absence.
"Haven't seen you in ages," she from behind the espresso machine. "The usual?"
"Please. And one of those almond croissants."
The pastry cost more than he’d previously been willing to spend, but the uneasiness that would have accompanied such a purchase was absent. He could afford this small pleasure without sacrificing necessities elsewhere.
Jay sat at his favorite table, the same spot where he'd filled out job applications after his first position ended badly. The cappuccino tasted exactly as he remembered, with the perfect balance of espresso and steamed milk.
The croissant was a revelation. Buttery layers dissolved on his tongue, the almond filling sweet but not cloying. He ate slowly, savoring both the flavor and the absence of guilt about the expense. Jay had forgotten how much he enjoyed this small comfort—the good coffee, the atmosphere, the brief escape from reality. For twenty minutes, he felt completely relaxed.
But even as he enjoyed the moment, practical considerations tempered his mood. His trial period lasted three months. Despite his positive performance reviews, nothing was guaranteed.  The generous compensation meant nothing if he couldn't maintain his position.
Back in his apartment, Jay transferred a big portion of his paycheck to his emergency savings account. The amount was larger than his entire previous monthly salary, but caution demanded preparation for potential unemployment. He'd learned not to assume job security, regardless of how well things appeared to be going. 
His emergency savings were meagre by some standards, just enough to cover three months of basic expenses, but he always made sure to keep it at that level, no matter what sacrifices he had to make. He couldn't be careless in this regard, and now, seeing the balance, it actually looked reassuring.
Still, enough remained in his checking account to get him through the next month with plenty left over. The relief was profound.
He thought about calling his mother to share the news, then decided against it. Their conversations had dwindled to obligatory exchanges on birthdays and holidays. She had her hands full with his stepfather and half-siblings, her new life in the house where Jay had never quite belonged. His small victories would seem insignificant compared to her daily concerns about mortgage payments and school events.
Besides, what would he say? That he'd found a job that paid well? That he was finally stable? The distance between them wasn't just geographic—it was the accumulated weight of years of growing apart, of accepting that some relationships couldn't survive the changes that time brought. His small town origins felt far removed from his current reality. The gap between his old life and new one was widening, and he wasn't sure he wanted to bridge it.
Jay opened his laptop and reviewed his calendar for the coming month. Client meetings, project deadlines, team collaborations. The work continued to challenge him, but his confidence had grown. Ian's recent praise had confirmed that his efforts were producing results.
One month complete. Two months remaining in his trial period.
He'd shown he could handle the daily responsibilities. Now he needed to demonstrate that his value to the company extended beyond basic competence. The generous compensation wasn't charity—it was an investment that expected returns.
Jay closed the laptop and prepared for bed, his mind already focused on tomorrow's assignments. The financial security was wonderful, but it was also a reminder of what he stood to lose if he became complacent.
He'd worked too hard to reach this point to risk it through overconfidence or relaxation of standards. The second month would be about building on the foundation he'd established, about proving that his first month's performance wasn't a temporary surge but a sustainable level of contribution.
Chapter 4: The ripples
Scene 1
Evaluation
Ian closed the file he was working on and took a moment to reflect. A month of working with Jay Hall had passed without issues. Jay did good work and drew no complaints from clients or colleagues. By any objective measure, the hire had been successful.
Jay arrived at seven-thirty each morning, prepared his workspace and dove into assignments without requiring supervision. His emails were well-crafted, his research accurate, his client interactions informative. He demonstrated analytical thinking, reading between the lines of client communications.
His awkwardness was obvious but harmless. He stumbled over words during meetings and turned pink when praised. He showed consistent deference and never presumed familiarity.
Distance was exactly what he'd intended to maintain. Jay had accepted the dynamic without question and even seemed content with their formal interactions. He made no attempts at personal conversation. Ian should have found this reassuring.
Instead, this very compliance unsettled Ian more than overt advances would have. He knew manipulation came in many forms. Patience could be strategy. The most effective predators understood the value of establishing trust before striking.
Ian was honest enough with himself to acknowledge the deeper problem: his attraction to Jay was undeniable. Those enormous gray eyes that looked at him with such obvious admiration triggered responses he'd spent years learning to suppress.
Physical attraction was manageable. Ian had learned to compartmentalize such responses, to acknowledge them privately while maintaining proper behaviour. He'd worked with attractive people before without difficulty.
But this was more complex. Ian respected Jay's work ethic, his dedication, his desire to learn and improve. The young man approached assignments with zeal that impressed veteran colleagues. He asked intelligent questions and accepted feedback gracefully. These qualities were alluring independent of physical appeal.
That combination—attraction and respect—was precisely what had destroyed Ian's career trajectory years ago. Sam Connelly had been both beautiful and brilliant, had demonstrated excellence that broke down Ian's defences. The memory of that catastrophic misjudgement remained vivid: the rationalization of inappropriate feelings, the spectacular consequences of trusting the wrong person.
Ian straightened in his chair as Leo appeared in his doorway.
"Afternoon check-in with the Ice King," Leo announced, sitting on the chair across from Ian's desk.
"Don't you have actual work to do?"
"I'm conducting important morale assessments." Leo's grin was unrepentant. "Speaking of which, how's the new assistant working out?"
"Adequately."
"He seems harmless, you know. Not everyone who works for you is running a secret agenda."
"Caution is appropriate."
"Paranoia might be more accurate. I've watched you build these walls higher every year. At some point, you have to trust somebody."
"I trust people who've earned it over time."
"And how much time is sufficient? Five years? Ten? Amy's been here almost a decade and you still maintain formal distance."
Ian didn't respond to the observation. Leo pulled a folder from his briefcase and placed it on the desk.
"Background check on Jay Hall. Professionally conducted, thoroughly documented. It’s as comprehensive as they come."
Ian opened the file and scanned the contents. Education records, employment history, credit report, criminal background check—all exactly as Jay had represented during his interview. No red flags, no inconsistencies, no cause for concern.
"Clean as a whistle," Leo said. "Everything checks out. Grew up in a small town, attended state university, worked for a consulting firm until recently. No financial problems, no legal issues, no questionable associations."
"Background checks only reveal documented problems."
"Ian. What exactly would convince you that someone isn't a threat? Because from where I sit, you've created an impossible standard. No one could meet requirements that change retroactively based on your anxiety."
Ian closed the file. "Experience has taught me that initial impressions can be deceiving."
"Experience has taught you to see enemies where none exist. I’m not trying to push you. I just worry you’ll end up making things harder on yourself.” 
Leo stayed a moment longer, as if weighing whether to add something else, then got up and straightened his jacket. “All right. I’ll let you get back to it.”
After Leo departed, Ian remained at his desk, reviewing not just Jay's performance but his own observations of other hires over the years. Turner & Associates attracted ambitious people, many of whom viewed proximity to senior management as an opportunity for advancement. Some had been obvious in their calculations—staying late to be noticed, volunteering for high-profile assignments.
Others had been more subtle. They'd demonstrated competence while slowly introducing personal elements into workplace interactions. A question about weekend plans here, a comment about shared experiences there, the gradual erosion of formal lines under the guise of developing working relationships.
Jay fit neither pattern. His behaviour was consistently withdrawn, almost shy. He completed assignments without seeking additional visibility and accepted praise without leveraging it for greater access. During team drinks, according to Luc's report, he'd been polite but reserved, leaving early with suitable excuses.
This very absence of calculation struck Ian as potentially more sophisticated than previous attempts. Jay's modesty could represent the most patient approach yet. Someone truly committed to a long-term strategy would understand the value of appearing harmless while positioning themselves for eventual exploitation.
A month was nothing in such calculations. If Jay was running a con, he was still in the foundation-building phase, establishing credibility, demonstrating reliability, earning the trust that would eventually be betrayed. The real test would come later, when he felt secure enough to begin exploring whether his target might be vulnerable to approach.
Ian had survived Sam's betrayal by learning never to underestimate anyone's capacity for deception. The stakes were too high—his career, his reputation, his rebuilt life—to gamble on assumptions about someone's character based on surface behaviour.
Ian opened his calendar and noted the date. Jay's three-month probationary period would end at the beginning of November. And Ian would maintain his vigilance. He would observe without seeming to watch and evaluate every interaction. He wouldn't compromise his life for a pair of gray eyes and an earnest smile.
Scene 2
Misstep
Jay knocked on Ian's office door with a finished report folder in his hands. He’d spent two full afternoons at home researching the comparative analysis Ian had requested, determined to make sure everything was perfect. The work had been challenging, more complex than his standard assignments, but he'd felt engaged with the material.
"Come in."
Ian looked up from his computer as Jay approached the desk. "The Greenwell Industries analysis?"
"Yes. I've included the five-year performance comparisons you requested, plus additional context on market conditions during each period."
Ian opened the folder and scanned the first page. His expression remained unreadable as he flipped through the documentation. "This looks comprehensive. Walk me through your methodology for the valuation adjustments."
Jay explained his approach, referencing specific sections of the report. Ian asked clarifying questions, probed his reasoning on certain calculations, tested his understanding of the underlying principles. The discussion felt productive, a substantive work conversation Jay had hoped to have more often.
Encouraged by Ian's engagement with his analysis, Jay ventured beyond the immediate assignment. "I noticed the Greenwell situation has similarities to the Falconridge Enterprises acquisition you handled in the past. The debt restructuring approach was very bold—was that driven primarily by regulatory considerations, or were there strategic advantages I'm not seeing?"
Ian's demeanour changed instantly. His posture stiffened and his eyes grew cold. The temperature in the room seemed to drop ten degrees.
"That's outside your current scope," Ian said, his tone sharp enough to cut. "Focus on your assigned projects."
Jay felt heat flood his face. "Of course. I apologize."
"The report is acceptable. You can go."
Jay retreated from the office, his heart hammering with embarrassment. He'd overstepped badly, had presumed an intimacy with Ian's work that clearly didn't exist. The dismissal had been outwardly polite, but curt enough to make the message absolutely clear. Outside your current scope. Focus on your assigned projects. 
A thinly veiled way of telling someone to stay in their lane and not presume to understand work beyond their level. Jay had allowed the supportive office atmosphere to make him forget the fundamental reality of his situation.
At his desk, Jay tried to return to his tasks, but concentrating was impossible. He replayed the conversation obsessively. The moment when interest had turnef into ice-cold rejection played on repeat in his mind.
He'd grown overconfident. The team's inclusiveness and Ian's recent acknowledgement of his good work had created a false sense of his position in the hierarchy. He was an assistant, barely a month into his probationary period, and he'd had the audacity to question strategic decisions made at levels far above his clearance.
The rest of the afternoon crawled by. When colleagues stopped by his desk for casual conversation, he kept his responses brief and work-focused. The camaraderie he'd begun to enjoy suddenly felt dangerous.
The next morning brought renewed determination to avoid similar mistakes. Jay arrived early and threw himself into assigned tasks. When Ian requested revisions to a client summary, Jay completed them without asking for clarification on borderline issues. When Amy mentioned a complex derivatives transaction, Jay listened politely but asked no follow-up questions.
During their morning briefing, Ian's manner seemed overly formal. His feedback on the due diligence report was delivered perfunctorily. Jay accepted each suggestion, avoiding any response that might seem presumptuous.
"Any questions about these revisions?" Ian asked after explaining the final changes.
Jay had several questions about the reasoning behind certain adjustments, but he suppressed them. "No, everything's clear."
"Good. Have the updated version ready by noon."
The conversation ended there. Ian returned to his computer, and Jay understood the dismissal.
Over the following days, Jay noticed Ian's behaviour had become even less available. Their interactions, always formal, turned positively glacial. Ian's responses to Jay's questions were curt, his acknowledgements of completed work cursory. The courtesy remained, but any hint of warmth had vanished.
Jay watched Ian's interactions with other team members, searching for signs that the change was general. But Ian's manner with Amy, Luc, and the others remained unchanged. He still paused to praise good work, still engaged in brief strategic discussions during team meetings. The difference was reserved for Jay alone.
Jay began second-guessing every word before he spoke. He prepared extensive notes for their brief meetings, rehearsing his comments to ensure he didn’t overstep. He stopped asking follow-up questions, even when confusion arose. Better to research answers independently than risk another humiliating rebuke.
The confidence he'd built over his first month eroded. He'd forgotten his place. Ian had made it clear that Jay was valued for his administrative competence, nothing more. Strategic thinking, curiosity about broader business decisions, intellectual engagement with complex problems—all of that lay beyond his station.
When he started this job, he’d promised himself he would stay unobtrusive, invisible. That was what he should have done all along, and what he intended to do from now on.
Luc noticed the change during a coffee break. "You've been quiet lately. Everything alright?"
"Fine. Just focused on work."
"Focused is good, but you seem tense."
Jay shrugged, unwilling to discuss his mistake. "Learning curve, I suppose."
"It gets easier. First few months are always the hardest."
Jay nodded, but privately wondered if he'd damaged something that couldn't be repaired. Ian's trust, once lost, might not return. The opportunity he'd been so grateful for could disappear if he continued making errors in judgement.
In the evenings, Jay sat in his apartment reviewing the day's interactions, searching for any sign that his probationary status might be in jeopardy. Ian's coldness could be temporary—a way of reinforcing the lines between them, and not a prelude to termination. But Jay couldn't be certain, and uncertainty felt corrosive.
He resolved to be more aware of his limitations. The work itself was still the best he ever had. He'd focus on those positives and remember his place. Ian was his supervisor, not his mentor. The sooner Jay internalized that distinction, the better their working relationship would be.
Meanwhile, in his office, Ian reviewed Jay's personnel file for the third time that week. The young man's question about the Falconridge Enterprises acquisition had been innocent enough on the surface—a logical connection based on legitimate business similarities. But it had probed into sensitive territory, into decisions that involved confidential client information and strategic considerations well above Jay's clearance level.
Sam had begun the same way. Casual curiosity about cases outside his purview. Ian had dismissed the pattern then as natural intellectual growth. He wouldn't make that mistake again.
Jay's recent withdrawal might be appropriate response to correction, or it might be calculated recalibration. Time would reveal which interpretation was accurate. 
Scene 3
Accepting his fate
The following week passed quietly. Jay arrived at his desk each morning in the same manner he had cultivated during his first month, but where once he had participated in the casual conversations that drifted between cubicles, now he offered only polite acknowledgements. When Luc made jokes during the lunch hour, Jay smiled but contributed nothing. When Ela shared newest gossip, he listened without comment.
"You've gone monastery on us," Kim observed on Wednesday, swiveling his chair to face Jay's workstation. "What happened to all those questions you used to ask?"
"I'm still learning," Jay replied, not looking up from his screen. "Just trying to focus more."
Kim waited for elaboration that never came, then returned to his monitors with a slight frown.
Jay had decided that his previous enthusiasm had been misplaced. The questions, the eagerness to understand complex strategies, the hopeful glances toward Ian's office—all of it had been inappropriate. He was administrative support, not an analyst. His role was to facilitate the work of others, not to insert himself into discussions that belonged to people with actual expertise. He had been playing a part he wasn't qualified for, and everyone had been too polite to correct him directly.
Ian's increasing coldness made perfect sense now. Of course the CFO had no interest in explaining acquisition strategies to someone hired to manage calendars and proofread documents.
On Thursday afternoon, Amy approached his desk with her laptop clutched against her chest. Dark circles shadowed her eyes, and her perfect posture had developed a slight slump.
"Jay, I need a huge favour," she said, dropping her laptop beside his keyboard. "This reconciliation report has to be perfect by tomorrow morning, but the numbers aren't adding up. I've been staring at it for hours and I can't find where the error is."
Jay examined the spreadsheet. Rows of figures stretched across dozens of pages, referencing multiple data sources. Work requiring infinite patience.
"I can take a look. How urgent is it?"
"Emergency urgent. The client presentation is first thing Friday, and if these numbers are wrong..." Amy rubbed her temples. "Max would normally handle this, but he's still out, and I'm drowning in the portfolio analyses."
"I'll find the problem," Jay promised.
Amy squeezed his shoulder gratefully. "You're a lifesaver. I owe you dinner."
Jay began working through the spreadsheet systematically, checking each calculation against its source documents. The numbers blurred together after the first hour, but he continued, marking each verified entry with a checkmark. Around him, the office gradually emptied. Luc stopped by his desk at six-thirty.
"Still here?"
"Just finishing something for Amy," Jay replied without looking up.
"Want me to wait? We could grab drinks."
"Thanks, but I'm good. Have a good evening."
Luc hesitated, then collected his jacket and left. By seven o'clock, only Jay and Ian remained on the floor. Jay could see Ian through the glass walls of his office, still working at his computer, but they didn't acknowledge each other beyond the brief nods they had exchanged earlier.
At nine-thirty, he found it, buried in a column of seemingly identical entries. A decimal point had been moved, creating a cascade of incorrect calculations throughout the dependent formulas. Jay corrected the cell and watched as the spreadsheet recalculated itself, numbers shifting into alignment. He ran the reconciliation again, checking and double-checking his work until he was certain the errors had been eliminated. Then he compiled a clean version of the report and sent it to the team with a brief note explaining what he had found and how he had fixed it.
The office was empty by the time he finished. Ian had left about an hour earlier, offering only a perfunctory farewell and no comment on Jay staying late. Jay gathered his things and walked to the elevator. He had done exactly what was asked of him, nothing more and nothing less. Tomorrow, the client would receive accurate data, and the firm would maintain its reputation for reliability.
The next morning Amy sent a brief email to the entire team: "Thanks to Jay Hall for staying late to fix the reconciliation report and catching the error that would have caused serious problems with the Broadmarsh account. Good work."
Jay read the message twice, warmth spreading through his stomach. Recognition felt good, even for work he considered basic responsibility. But the satisfaction faded quickly, replaced by self-doubt. Had he really done something noteworthy, or was Amy simply being kind? The error had been obvious once he found it. Anyone with basic spreadsheet skills could have made the same corrections.
He glanced toward Ian's office. He was deep in conversation with someone on the phone, his back turned to the main floor. Jay wondered if Ian had seen Amy's email, if he disapproved of Jay staying late without authorization.
"Nice work last night," Ela said, appearing beside his desk with her morning coffee. "Amy was panicking about that presentation."
"It wasn't difficult. Just time-consuming."
"Still. Not everyone would have stayed that late for someone else's project."
Jay forced a smile. "Happy to help."
The praise continued throughout the morning. Luc mentioned the email during lunch, Kim nodded approvingly when he passed Jay's desk, even Zoe offered a compliment. Each acknowledgement should have felt validating, but instead Jay grew more uncomfortable. The attention felt undeserved. He had simply done what anyone in his position should do—support the people doing the real work.
He spent the rest of the day organizing filing systems and updating contact databases. Work that required no expertise and created no opportunities for overreach. When someone needed documents copied, he volunteered immediately. When the printer jammed, he fixed it without being asked. When the conference room needed cleaning between client meetings, he handled it.
This was his proper role. Not the eager participant he had tried to be during his first weeks, not the aspiring analyst who asked too many questions. Just the person who made sure everything ran smoothly so others could focus on important work.
Ian had given him this opportunity–protection from the chaos and predatory behaviour that had defined his previous jobs, a chance to exist without fear. That was already more generosity than Jay had any right to expect. To want more, to hope for mentorship or advancement or even basic collegiality, would be presumptuous and ungrateful.
He glanced once more toward Ian's office. Ian's distant professionalism wasn't disapproval, Jay told himself. It was appropriate. A kindness, really. Ian understood that Jay belonged in the background, and he respected that reality instead of offering false encouragement.
Jay returned to his filing with renewed determination. He would prove himself worthy of this gift by asking for nothing more than what he had already received. In a world where powerful people routinely abused their positions, Ian had created a space where Jay could work safely and contribute meaningfully, even if that contribution remained largely invisible. 
Scene 4
Painful returns
There was a noticeable change in the office that Monday in late September, a liveliness that set it apart from most mornings. The elevator buzzed with conversation, colleagues clustered around the coffee station longer than usual.
"Max is back," Ela announced as she hung her jacket in the small closet. "Finally. I thought we were going to collapse under the workload."
Jay had heard the name mentioned countless times over the past month—Max Goodwin, senior analyst, the absent team member whose projects had been distributed among the remaining staff, and whose return date kept getting pushed back. From the way people spoke about him, Jay had imagined someone intimidating, a financial genius whose shoes no one could properly fill. The reality was entirely different.
Max exploded into the office at nine-fifteen, his laughter preceding him down the hallway. He was shorter than Jay had expected, stocky and sun-weathered, with graying hair and booming voice.
"This place looks like a morgue," he announced, dropping a oversized duffel bag beside Amy's desk. "What happened to the music I used to hear? Where's the energy?"
Amy stood to embrace him. "We've been drowning without you. The workload has been insane, and we've been splitting your portfolio between three people."
"If you hadn’t come back in time for the quarterly reports, we’d have been doomed," Ela added.
"Well, I'm back now. The island was incredible, but getting trapped there was a nightmare. Thought I'd never see you again." Max's eyes swept the office, cataloging faces. "Everyone looks exhausted. We need to fix that immediately."
Jay watched from his desk as Max worked his way through the team, dispensing hugs, backslaps, and rapid-fire questions about projects he'd left behind. People stood straighter, spoke louder, smiled more readily. Even Ian emerged from his office, and Jay witnessed something he had never seen before—Ian's face softening into what could almost be called warmth.
"Max," Ian said, extending his hand. "Good to have you back."
"Ian, you look terrible. Have you been sleeping?" Max gripped Ian's hand in both of his own. "I leave for two months and everyone falls apart. This is why I can never retire."
"The office hasn't been the same," Ian replied.
Max's attention eventually turned to the periphery, where Jay sat quietly organizing the morning's correspondence. "Hold on. Who's this?"
Jay's fingers stilled on his keyboard. Every head in the office turned toward him, and he felt his face begin to burn.
"That's Jay," Amy said. "Jay Hall. He's been helping us with administrative support."
Max strode over, his hand already extended. "Jay Hall! So you're Leo's new joke hire! Welcome to the team!"
The words hit Jay like cold water. The office fell silent—not the comfortable quiet of people returning to work, but the sharp silence of witnesses to something awkward. Jay shook Max's offered hand while his mind reeled.
"Thank you," he managed, his voice barely audible.
Max seemed oblivious to the tension he had created. "Leo's got a type, I'll give him that. But don't worry—if you've lasted this long, you must be doing something right. Most of his joke hires don't make it past the first month."
Jay's smile felt like it might crack his face. Around him, he could sense his colleagues' discomfort—Kim studying his computer screen intently, Ela suddenly finding her coffee fascinating, Amy's face tight with something that might have been sympathy or embarrassment.
"I should get back to work," Jay said, sinking into his chair.
"Of course, of course. We'll catch up properly later." Max clapped him on the shoulder with enough force to make Jay wince, then moved on to demand updates on the quarterly projections.
The rest of the morning passed in a haze. Jay processed emails, answered phone calls, and maintained a helpful demeanour, but Max's words echoed in his mind. Joke hire.
What did that mean? And why did everyone seem to know something he didn't?
Just before lunch, Luc appeared beside the printer where Jay was copying a stack of client reports. Luc glanced around the empty corridor, then moved closer.
"Hey. Don't let Max get to you. He's got no filter, especially when he's excited."
Jay fed another document into the machine, not trusting himself to speak.
"Look, I probably shouldn't tell you this, but..." Luc hesitated, then continued in an even lower voice. "I was a joke hire once, too. Leo has this thing—he likes to mess with people sometimes. It's not malicious, just... stupid rich boy games."
"What kind of games?" Jay asked, though he wasn't sure he wanted to know.
"It's complicated. Leo and Ian have this history, this dynamic. Sometimes Leo brings in people to... I don't know. Stir things up? Make some kind of point?" 
"Are you saying I was hired as a joke?"
"No. No, that's not—" Luc looked frustrated. "You were hired because we needed administrative support. But there might have been other motivations."
Jay stared at the printer, watching it slowly process each page. "What other motivations?"
"There was this scandal… Look, it doesn't matter. You work hard, Jay. Everyone sees that. Whatever Leo's reasons were for bringing you in, you've earned your place here through your own effort."
Luc's words should have been comforting, but they only raised more questions. What history did Leo and Ian share? And what did any of it have to do with Jay?
That evening, Jay sat in his apartment with his laptop open, staring at the search results for "Ian Hagen scandal." The articles were old, dating back several years, but the tabloid headlines were brutal: "Hagen's Office Romance Turns Ugly," "Corporate Spy Seduces A Heir." Jay clicked through them with growing horror, piecing together the story of Ian's relationship with a secretary, the corporate espionage that had nearly destroyed Ian's career, and the public humiliation that had followed.
But it was the newer articles that made Jay's stomach drop. Gossip pieces speculating about Ian's hiring practices, ugly insinuations about his preferences for attractive assistants.
The laptop screen blurred as Jay stared at the words. Was this what people saw when they looked at him? Was this what Max had meant by "Leo's got a type"? Was he nothing more than the latest in a series of pretty boys hired to amuse the wealthy and powerful?
The apartment felt too small, the walls pressing in on him. Jay walked to his window and looked out at the city lights, trying to make sense of what he had learned. Everything felt different now—every interaction, every kindness, every moment of courtesy. Had it all been part of some elaborate game?
But even as the betrayal cut through him, one thing remained stubbornly clear. Ian had never made him feel uncomfortable. Even at his coldest, Ian had treated Jay with respect and professionalism.
Jay returned to his laptop and read the articles again, more carefully this time. The scandal had been devastating. If Ian was now wary of relationships with subordinates, if he maintained distance from his staff, that made sense. It was self-protection.
And if Leo Turner was playing some kind of game with people's lives, using Jay as a pawn in whatever twisted dynamic he shared with Ian, then Jay was caught in the middle of something he didn't understand and couldn't control. But what could he do? Quit? Confront Leo? Demand answers from Ian?
Jay closed the laptop and sat in darkness. The safe harbour he thought he had found suddenly felt precarious and false. But where else could he go? Back to jobs where bosses saw him as prey? Back to the uncertainty and fear that had defined his working life?
He thought about Amy's smile when he had fixed her spreadsheet. Luc's friendship. Whatever games were being played at higher levels, his relationships with his colleagues felt real.
And Ian had given him something Jay had never experienced before: the feeling of safety, a workplace free from harassment, an environment where he could focus on work instead of fending off unwanted attention.
Even if it was all a lie, even if he was nothing more than entertainment for people who saw him as disposable, Jay wasn't sure he could give up the only security he had ever known.
Scene 5
Resignation
Tuesday morning was gray and unremarkable. Jay sat at his desk by seven-thirty, way before anyone else typically appeared. The office held a cathedral quiet that he had come to prefer—no conversations, no comments that might hold hidden meanings.
He opened the email and began sorting through the messages. Client requests, meeting confirmations, document revisions. Each task received the same attention, processed and completed without flourish or delay. The work filled the space where thoughts might otherwise intrude.
When his colleagues began filtering in around eight, Jay acknowledged their greetings with polite nods but offered nothing more. The morning pleasantries that had once felt welcoming now seemed like potential traps. Every interaction held the possibility of revealing how much he knew, how deeply the truth had cut.
"Morning, Jay. How was your evening?" Amy said.
"Fine, thank you."
She waited for elaboration that never came, then turned to her computer with a slight frown. Jay had noticed the pattern developing over the past week—people expected more from him, seemed surprised by his brevity, but no one pressed for details.
Max's booming laughter echoed from the conference room where he was catching up with Ian on the projects that had accumulated during his absence. The sound made Jay's shoulders tighten. He focused harder on the spreadsheet in front of him, double-checking figures that were already correct.
At ten-thirty, Luc approached his desk with a stack of documents that needed copying and binding for an afternoon presentation. "These need to be collated and spiral-bound."
"I'll have them ready by noon," Jay replied without looking up.
Luc hesitated. "You okay? You've been pretty quiet lately."
"I'm fine. Just focused on work."
"Right. Well, let me know if you need anything."
Jay nodded, already reaching for the papers. He walked to the copy room with purposeful steps, thankful for a task that required no human interaction. The machine's rhythmic churning provided white noise against the questions that constantly circled in his mind.
Why had Leo Turner hired him? What entertainment value did Jay provide for people who could afford to treat other human beings as amusing diversions? Was Ian complicit, or was he simply another victim of Leo's games?
The questions led nowhere productive, so Jay pushed them aside and focused on collating pages in the correct order. This was what mattered—the immediate task, the concrete requirement, the measurable result. Everything else was speculation and self-pity.
When he returned to his desk, Kim was troubleshooting a network connection issue that had affected three workstations. Jay set the bound documents on Luc's desk and returned to his own work without offering assistance. He had learned to recognize which problems required his help and which were beyond his capabilities. Network issues belonged to Kim's expertise.
Jay joined the team for lunch, slipping into the background. Back at his desk, he focused on reviewing the afternoon's calendar, noting which meetings would require conference room setup, which clients needed specific documentation prepared, which deadlines loomed closest. The mundane nature of the planning soothed him. No ambiguity, no hidden agendas, no need to guess at motivations.
Ela stopped by his desk on her way to a meeting. "Jay, I'm expecting a call around three. If it comes in while I'm in the presentation, can you take a message?"
"Of course."
"Thanks. You're a lifesaver."
The casual phrase made something twist in Jay's stomach. Lifesaver. Helper. Reliable support. All accurate descriptions, all appropriate for his role. But they all felt fake now. In that moment, he recognized his own replies as lines in a play he hadn’t even known he was performing.
He pushed the thought away and returned to his work. He couldn't afford these feelings. The job was great and safe. That was more than he had found anywhere else, and probably more than he would find again.
The afternoon crawled by. Documents were proofread, phone calls logged, appointments confirmed, files organized. Each accomplishment felt insignificant but necessary. Jay moved through his responsibilities like someone checking items off a shopping list, completely emotionally detached.
At five o'clock, most of the team began gathering their things and shutting down computers. As they prepared to leave, there was grumbling about the looming quarterly reports and talk of going to bed early to catch up on sleep before the workload peaked.
Jay remained at his desk, finishing a client database update that could easily wait until tomorrow. The extra work served no purpose beyond keeping him occupied, preventing the moment when he would have to return to his apartment and sit alone with his thoughts.
"You don't have to stay late," Amy said, gathering her purse and jacket. "The work will still be here tomorrow."
"Just finishing up," Jay replied without looking at her.
She paused by his desk. "Jay, is everything all right? You've seemed... quieter lately."
"I'm fine. Thank you for asking."
Amy looked at him for a moment, then nodded. "Have a good evening."
Jay waited until the office emptied before finally shutting down his computer. The elevator ride to the ground floor felt longer than usual, the mirrored walls reflecting his image from multiple angles. He avoided looking directly at his reflection, keeping his eyes fixed on the floor numbers as they descended.
The evening commute passed quickly. Jay found a seat by the window, letting the motion and noise wash over him without really registering either. His mind felt deliberately blank, a defense mechanism he had cultivated over the past week.
His apartment met him with silence, the kind that seemed to press in on him. Jay reheated dinner, ate it at the table, then sat at his couch with a stack of bills that needed organizing. The mundane task filled another hour, another stretch of time that didn't require self-reflection. He moved through the rest of his evening without thinking and went to bed early.
The next day, he returned to an office as tense as he felt. With September ending, the quarterly reports had begun, when everyone seemed to live in the office on takeout. Not much was expected of him beyond his duties, and he was at least relieved that, in the chaos, no one seemed to pay him any particular attention.
When Jay checked his bank account online later that day, he saw a balance larger than he’d ever had after two paychecks at his new company. The number should have made him happy, evidence of his successful transition to a better job. Instead, it felt like a transaction completed, services rendered and compensated according to their market value.
Jay stared at the screen for a long time, thinking about dignity and compromise, about the prices people paid for security. He had a roof over his head, food in his refrigerator, and work that challenged him. Thousands of people would consider his situation enviable.
If the cost was his self-respect, if the price was accepting his role as entertainment, then perhaps that was simply the nature of the transaction. He had traded his dignity for stability, his pride for a paycheck. The marketplace had determined his worth, and he had accepted the terms.
Jay closed the laptop and prepared for bed, mindlessly moving through the evening. Tomorrow would bring another day of obligations to fulfil. The work would continue regardless of his feelings about it.
In the darkness of his bedroom, he lay still, avoiding the thoughts that wanted to surface. He had made his choice. The only rational response was to live with the consequences and find whatever satisfaction he could in competent performance of his duties.
If it felt like slowly drowning, that was a price he was willing to pay, because even this was better than the alternative. He’d rather be the clown than someone’s prey.
Chapter 5: Finding his place
Scene 1
Glamour
As the first week of October came to a close, the frantic pace of quarterly reports finally began to ease. With the worst behind them, it was time for the next fixture on the company calendar—the quarterly party. 
The invitation to Turner & Associates’ Quarterly Celebration had arrived weeks in advance, printed on heavy cream cardstock with embossed gold lettering. The address was a rooftop venue in one of the city’s most prestigious towers, a place Jay had only ever seen in magazines. As a new employee, Jay had received his invitation along with his starter package, but with so many onboarding materials to go through, he hadn’t paid it proper attention. Only a recent reminder email from HR brought it back to his mind, leaving him with precious little time to prepare.
He'd spent three days researching appropriate attire, another two visiting menswear stores he'd never entered before. The midnight blue suit cost a small fortune, but he'd convinced himself it was a necessary investment. Appearance mattered in this environment, and blending in was essential.
Standing in the elevator ascending to the fifty floor, Jay adjusted his tie for the dozenth time. The suit fit well, but he felt like he was wearing a costume.
The elevator doors opened to reveal a space that belonged in a different world. Crystal chandeliers cast warm light over clusters of perfectly dressed guests. Servers in white jackets circulated with champagne. A jazz quartet played in one corner while a renowned local news anchor served as host.
Jay accepted a glass of champagne and tried to blend into his group. Amy looked stunning in a black cocktail dress, her serious demeanour softened by the elegant setting. Kim had traded his casual office attire for a sharp charcoal suit. Even Zoe, who rarely joined any social gatherings, was present in a flowing burgundy dress, engaged in animated conversation with someone from the accounting team.
"Great turnout this quarter," Luc said, appearing at Jay's elbow. "The fund's performance has everyone in good spirits."
"It's impressive."
"First company party?"
"This elaborate, yes."
"You'll get used to it. Turner & Associates knows how to celebrate success."
Jay struggled with the small talk that flowed around him. The conversation jumped from market analyses to industry gossip, topics that required either expertise he lacked or personal details he was reluctant to share.
"A toast," Amy announced, lifting her champagne flute. "To another successful quarter."
Everyone raised their glasses. Jay fumbled slightly with his, nearly spilling champagne on his expensive suit. "To success," he managed, his voice barely audible above the ambient noise.
As the evening progressed, the formal atmosphere gave way to mingling and networking. Jay watched his colleagues scatter throughout the room, seamlessly joining conversations with senior staff, and occasional clients and partners from other firms. The ease with which they moved through this social landscape highlighted his own awkwardness.
He positioned himself near the windows, hoping to appear contemplative. 
"Jay."
Jay turned to find Ian approaching, champagne glass in hand.
"Good evening, Ian. Beautiful venue."
"The partners chose well."
"The view is remarkable."
"Yes." Ian glanced toward a group of senior staff who were clearly expecting his return. "Enjoy the rest of the evening."
The exchange lasted perhaps fifteen seconds. Courtesy without a trace of warmth. Jay watched Ian walk away, joining a cluster of senior executives who welcomed him with obvious respect.
Throughout the evening, Jay observed Ian's interactions with others. He saw him laugh at something Leo said, offer congratulations to Amy on a recent project, engage in a long conversation with Ela. The contrast with their brief, formal exchange was unmistakable.
Jay told himself this was appropriate. Ian was treating him exactly as he should treat a junior employee at a company function. The distance was proof of Ian's integrity, not a personal slight. But the rationalization couldn't eliminate the sting.
"You look like you're analyzing the molecular structure of champagne," Luc appeared at his elbow. "Everything alright?"
"Fine. Just taking in the atmosphere."
"It's a lot the first time. I spent my first company event hiding in the bathroom."
Jay managed a smile. "That's reassuring."
"You're doing better than you think. These things get easier."
Jay nodded, but privately doubted the claim. He didn't belong in this rarefied environment, among people who moved through high society as if they'd been born to it.
After the night had stretched far enough, Jay made his escape as early as politeness allowed. He thanked the appropriate people, made the necessary excuses about early morning commitments, and slipped away before anyone could propose extending the festivities.
In the elevator descending to street level, Jay caught his reflection in the polished steel doors. The suit looked good on him—he could admit that much. But the man wearing it still looked like he was playing dress-up.
Outside, the city felt different. More real. Jay walked to the metro station, his expensive shoes clicking on the concrete, his jacket a barrier between him and the evening air.
This was his place: invisible, uninvolved, unthreatening. He'd wanted safety, and safety meant accepting limitations. The job was prestigious, the pay was excellent, the work was engaging. Those benefits came with costs, including the recognition that some spaces would always feel foreign to him.
Meanwhile, back at the celebration, Leo cornered Ian near the bar. "Jay looked great tonight. That suit was inspired."
"I hadn't noticed."
"Of course you hadn't. Though I should mention that several people did notice. Sal from risk management spent half the evening trying to engineer an introduction. And I'm pretty sure Jen from accounting was making eyes at him."
"His social interactions are none of my concern."
"No, of course not. But if you're going to fumble this badly, someone else is going to step in. The boy's got that lost romantic hero thing going on. Very appealing to a certain type."
Ian finished his champagne and set the glass on the bar. "There's nothing to fumble. He's an employee, nothing more."
"Right. And that's why you've been watching him all evening while pretending not to."
Ian had been watching, despite himself. He'd noticed Jay's obvious discomfort, the way he'd stood apart from groups, the politeness that couldn't mask his anxiety. The young man had looked beautiful in the midnight blue suit, his pale complexion and dark hair creating a striking contrast against the elegant fabric. He'd also looked completely out of his depth, like someone playing a role he'd never auditioned for.
The protective instinct that arose from that observation was dangerous. Ian recognized it as the same impulse that had blinded him to Sam's true nature—the desire to shelter someone who appeared vulnerable, to offer guidance and support to someone who seemed to need it.
"He's managing fine on his own," Ian said.
"Is he? Because from where I stood, he looked like he was drowning."
"Not my responsibility."
"Maybe not. But it could be your opportunity."
Ian walked away without responding, forcing himself to hold back. He refused to let himself be swayed by how fragile Jay looked, or by the hurt in his expression. Whatever Jay was going through, Ian needed to guard his own heart more than he needed to offer help.
Scene 2
Pity
Ian had prided himself on his powers of observation. Years of analysing market trends, reading client temperaments, and managing corporate politics had honed his ability to notice subtle changes in behaviour. But he had never applied that scrutiny to his own staff with quite the intensity he now directed toward Jay Hall.
The change had been immediate and unmistakable. Where Jay had once been quiet but engaged, he now seemed to exist in a state of cultivated invisibility. His work arrived earlier than before, so carefully executed it was clear he was desperate to avoid any criticism. During team meetings, Jay positioned himself at the edges, contributing only when directly asked.
