Most telling was his posture. Jay moved through the office like someone expecting punishment, shoulders slightly hunched, eyes fixed on whatever task occupied his immediate attention. He no longer smiled or asked tentative questions that had once punctuated their interactions. Instead, he responded to Ian's instructions with politeness that felt rehearsed.
Ian watched this change from behind his glass office walls, disturbed by how closely he was monitoring someone he had resolved to keep at a distance. The irony wasn't lost on him—in his determination to avoid the mistakes of his past, he had become hyper-aware of Jay's every movement.
Max's comment had been thoughtless but typical. Leo's hiring practices had always been subject to speculation and innuendo. But Jay's reaction had been unlike anything Ian had expected from someone with hidden motives. There had been no indignation, no attempt to defend himself or curry favor. Just a wounded silence.
For days, Ian wrestled with the implications. If Jay was truly the innocent he appeared to be, then allowing him to suffer under the weight of office gossip seemed needlessly cruel. But any direct intervention risked exposing how closely Ian had been watching, how much mental energy he had devoted to analyzing someone who was supposed to be merely administrative support.
On Thursday afternoon, Ian made his decision.
"Jay," he called from his office doorway. "Could you come in for a moment?"
Jay appeared within seconds, notepad in hand. "Yes?"
"Please, sit." Ian gestured to the chair across from his desk. "I have a new project for you."
Jay sat on the chair with the posture designed to take up minimal space. "Of course. What do you need?"
Ian pulled a thick folder from his desk drawer. "This is a client confidentiality audit for the Stormfront Group account. I need someone to review all documentation from the past eighteen months, cross-reference it with our disclosure protocols, and flag any potential compliance issues."
He watched Jay's face as he explained the scope of the work. The project was important and required discretion, but it was general enough that it didn’t demand extensive insider experience. It was also the sort of task Ian typically handled himself.
"The work is sensitive. Stormfront is one of our largest clients, and any compliance gaps could have serious consequences. I'll need detailed notes on your methodology and findings."
Jay accepted the folder. "When do you need this completed?"
"End of next week. And Jay? If you have questions about the protocols or need clarification on any of the documentation, don't hesitate to ask. This isn't a project you should struggle through alone."
Jay simply nodded. "I understand. Thank you for trusting me with this."
The idea of trust stopped Ian cold. He had to remind himself he wasn’t that naive. Still, his doubts wouldn’t cloud the fairness he aimed to show.
"Your work ethic made you the logical choice. That spreadsheet work you did for Amy last month showed exactly the skills this project requires."
Jay's fingers tightened slightly on the folder. "I'll make sure it's done properly."
"I'm confident you will."
After Jay left his office, Ian stared at the door that closed after him. The interaction had felt different—less guarded on his part, though he couldn't say why. Perhaps because Jay's resignation had been so complete, so lacking in any agenda beyond basic survival, that it had disarmed Ian's suspicions.
Over the following days, Ian glanced toward Jay's desk more frequently than work-related curiosity warranted. Jay worked through the documentation with the same diligence he brought to everything else, but there was something almost meditative about his focus. He spread the papers across his desk in categories, made notes in margins with beautiful handwriting, and periodically consulted the compliance manual Ian had provided.
Twice, Jay approached Ian’s office with questions about the finer points of the assignment—technical details he couldn’t deduce on his own, despite having reviewed the materials multiple times. Each time, he paused at the door, bracing himself for the possibility of a rebuke, even though his questions were perfectly reasonable. Instead, Ian always took the time to answer in full, going beyond a simple explanation of the rules to share the rationale behind each protocol. Jay listened with full attention, though some part of him stayed wary, half-expecting a reprimand.
"The disclosure requirements seem designed to protect the client's privacy while ensuring regulatory compliance," Jay observed during their second consultation. "It's more complex than I initially thought."
"Most people assume compliance work is just bureaucratic box-checking. But done properly, it's about balancing competing interests—transparency, privacy, legal obligations, business relationships."
Jay nodded thoughtfully. "I can see how easy it would be to miss something important if you approached it too perfunctorily."
When Jay submitted the completed audit the following Thursday, Ian set aside his other work to review it immediately. The analysis was well-organized, with clear explanations of methodology and detailed documentation of findings. Jay had identified three minor compliance gaps and his recommendations for addressing them were both practical and legally sound.
But it was the marginal notes that held Ian's attention longest. Jay had included small observations about document organization that indicated he understood not just the technical requirements but the broader business context. The work revealed a mind that could see patterns and connections beyond the immediate task.
Ian read through the report twice, then a third time, searching for... what? Evidence of deception? Signs of hidden agenda? Or simply confirmation that his growing sense of Jay's competence was justified?
Reality was more unsettling than either possibility. He was looking for clues about who Jay really was—not the administrative assistant who processed his correspondence, but the person behind the mask. The person who had been wounded by Max's comment and had responded not with anger but with deeper withdrawal.
Ian closed the folder and leaned back in his chair. He had given Jay an important assignment and received exceptional work in return. The gesture had been intended as a signal, recognition of value, an indication that Jay's contributions were noticed and appreciated. Whether Jay understood the message was another matter entirely.
Through his office wall, Ian could see Jay at his desk, working through the afternoon's tasks with the focus that had become his trademark. There was no indication that the special project had affected his mood or his perspective on his role. If anything, he seemed more withdrawn than before, as if success had only reinforced his determination to remain invisible.
Ian had offered what he could within the confines he had established for himself—meaningful work, respectful treatment. But watching Jay's solitary figure bent over his keyboard, Ian wondered if it was enough, or if some wounds ran too deep for such subtle gestures to heal.
The thought troubled him more than it should have. Jay Hall was an employee, nothing more. His emotional state was relevant only insofar as it affected his work performance. Ian had learned that lesson the hard way, and he had no intention of forgetting it again.
But the hollow look in Jay's eyes haunted him, and Ian hoped that somewhere beneath the resignation, some small part of Jay understood that he was valued for more than his appearance, more than whatever ploy had brought him to Turner & Associates in the first place.
Scene 3
Distrust
The awkwardness between Jay and Ian didn’t vanish, but it was suddenly eclipsed by a new, prestigious acquisition. Hartwell was a major firm, a client most teams only hoped to land once. Ian had brought them in personally, a move that raised eyebrows—he hadn’t handled client acquisition himself in years. That alone signaled the importance of the project, and the office rearranged around it accordingly.
For nearly two weeks, the team threw themselves into the prospect. Jay had watched from the periphery as they worked late into evenings, sensing the difference without fully understanding the stakes. Jay's role had been supporting—organizing documents, coordinating schedules, ensuring that presentations were printed without error. 
Jay's collegues consumed client research over lunches, and turned the office into a war room of financial projections and strategy sessions. Jay wasn’t invited to the meetings behind closed doors, but he could tell when they’d gone badly: Ian would emerge with his jaw tight, the lines at the corners of his eyes deepened.
Once, midway through the afternoon, Ian passed him on the way to the conference room and a faint smell of cigarettes followed him. It startled Jay,  reminding him that even Ian was showing strain.
The day the representatives arrived, the office atmosphere was tense, like the air before a storm. Jay had been asked to stay close, mostly to fetch files or bring in whatever documents Ian might need on short notice. He kept to the side of the conference room, trying not to draw attention. The conversation itself stayed mostly over his head, but even he could sense the undercurrents—moments when Ian’s tone cooled just enough to signal a line being drawn. When the meeting finally ended, the room emptied in silence. Jay hadn’t said a word the entire time. It felt as though he’d witnessed an invisible tug-of-war that left everyone a little drained.
Finally, on a Friday afternoon, the confirmation email arrived: Hartwell would sign with Turner & Associates. The office erupted in a spontaneous celebration. Amy applauded when Ian announced the news. Kim, who had spent a week troubleshooting client database integrations, grinned from ear to ear. Even Zoe emerged from her corner to join the impromptu gathering around Ian's office. 
"Conference room in ten minutes," Ian announced. "I want to debrief while everything's fresh." 
Jay had attended similar meetings before, taking notes and managing logistics while the real participants discussed strategy and lessons learned. He gathered his notepad and pen, prepared to occupy a position along the wall where he could document without intruding. The team sat around the polished conference table. Ian took his place at the head, but his formal demeanour had softened. 
"This was a team effort," Ian began. "Everyone contributed essential work. Amy, your market analysis identified the leverage points that made our proposal compelling. Kim, the technical integration you designed gave us a competitive edge that other firms couldn't match. Max, your relationship management kept Hartwell engaged through some difficult negotiations. Ela, you provided the international perspective that sealed their confidence in our global reach." 
Max leaned back in his chair leisurely. "It was a beautiful pitch. Though I have to say, Jay's pretty face didn't hurt our cause. Hartwell's senior staff definitely perked up when he walked into that presentation room." 
The comment landed heavily. A few people laughed an awkward, uncertain laughter that fills uncomfortable spaces. Jay kept his eyes fixed on his notepad, his pen moving in meaningless loops across the paper. 
"Jay did excellent work on this project," Luc said firmly. "Those client briefing materials were flawless." 
"Absolutely," Amy added. "The timeline coordination alone saved us days of confusion. Every meeting, every deadline, every document—perfectly organized."  
Jay felt warmth spread through his chest despite himself. These comments felt different from Max's brusqueness—specific, focused on actual contributions. For a moment, he allowed himself to believe that his work mattered, that he had earned his place through competence. 
"Jay's contribution has been invaluable. This project required coordination across multiple departments, and nothing fell through the cracks," Ian said.
The praise should have felt unambiguously good. Jay had worked hard, contributed meaningfully, and received recognition from people whose opinions he respected. But Max's comment echoed in his mind, poisoning it with doubt. Was the praise honest, or were his colleagues simply being kind? Did they see him as a valuable team member, or as someone who needed protection from jokes? 
The meeting continued with technical debriefs and process improvements, but Jay couldn't  concentrate. His thoughts oscillated between pride and suspicion, between the warm acknowledgement of his contributions and the cold reality of how others might perceive him. When the meeting concluded, people dispersed cheerfully. Jay packed his notes and prepared to return to his desk, but Luc caught his arm in the hallway. 
"Hey. Don't let Max get in your head. He can be an oaf sometimes. Your work on this project was impressive. Ian doesn't give compliments unless he means them." 
Jay managed a fragile smile. "Thank you. I appreciate that." 
"I'm serious. You've got good instincts for this work. Don't let anyone make you think otherwise." The words were kind, but they raised as many questions as they answered. Why did Luc feel the need to reassure him? How obvious was his uncertainty? And most unsettling of all—was Luc's kindness part of the same dynamic that had brought Jay to Turner & Associates in the first place? 
The rest of the afternoon went by with regular work—filing documents from the Hartwell project, preparing materials for Monday's meetings. The praise from the meeting replayed in Jay's mind alongside Max's objectification. The two perspectives felt irreconcilable, leaving him suspended between hope and cynicism. 
When five arrived, Jay gathered his belongings and joined the evening exodus. The metro ride home gave him time to process the day's events, but instead of clarity, he found only deeper confusion. Had he truly contributed something valuable, or were his colleagues being diplomatic? Was recognition possible for someone in his position, or would everything he accomplished be filtered through the lens of his appearance? 
Jay arrived at his apartment more unsettled than ever. After dinner, he stood before the bathroom mirror, studying his reflection. The face that looked back at him was unremarkable enough—pale skin, gray eyes, features that might charitably be called pleasant. But somehow this ordinary appearance had become the defining factor in how others perceived him, the lens through which all his interactions were interpreted. If he had been born with different features—plainer, more forgettable, less likely to inspire commentary—would his life have followed a different path? Would his first boss have seen him as a capable employee instead of prey? Would Leo Turner have hired him for his skills and not for whatever entertainment value he provided? Would Max see him as a colleague? 
Jay touched his reflection, his fingertips meeting cold glass. These features had never brought him anything but trouble. In school, they had made him a target for bullies. In his previous jobs, they had attracted unwanted attention that ultimately destroyed his sense of safety. Now they served as the foundation for jokes and speculation that undermined his every achievement. 
He thought about the praise he had received during the meeting.  Those comments had felt good in the moment, but doubt crept in anyway. How much of that recognition was real, and how much was simply colleagues being kind to someone they pitied? The question felt unanswerable, and Jay was tired of trying to solve it. 
He turned away from the mirror and prepared for bed. In the darkness of his bedroom, Jay lay still and tried to find joy in the day's accomplishments. But the victories felt bitter, overshadowed by the persistent sense that nothing he achieved would ever be entirely his own. 
Sleep came slowly, filled with dreams of conference rooms where praise turned to laughter, where mirrors showed not his face but the cold eyes of those who would never see him as an equal.
Scene 4
Apology
Repetitive beginning, the change was mentioned before
The days that followed the Hartwell acquisition returned to the previous rhythm while the contract was being finalized by the legal team. Jay performed his duties well, but the eager curiosity that had once characterized his work didn't return. He never lingered over interesting details or asked questions that might deepen his understanding. The enthusiastic student who had once asked questions about acquisition strategies had been replaced by someone who stayed strictly within the confines of his assigned role.
His colleagues noticed the change but seemed uncertain how to address it. Conversations around his desk continued normally, but people no longer expected him to participate beyond basic acknowledgments. The camaraderie that had been developing during his first month never recovered.
"Drinks after work?" Luc asked on Friday, the same invitation he had extended weekly since Jay's arrival. The question had become almost ceremonial—offered without real expectation, declined with predictable courtesy.
"Maybe some other time," Jay replied.
"Sure thing," Luc said, and that was the end of it. No one pressed anymore, no one expressed disappointment. They had learned to accept his polite rejections as final.
Jay told himself this was better. Cleaner. Less complicated than trying to manage social dynamics he didn't fully understand with people who might pity him more than they liked him. Work relationships should be kept on a formal level. But the isolation wore on him.
On Tuesday afternoon, while Jay was collating documents near the copy machine, Max appeared beside him with uncharacteristic hesitation. Instead of his booming confidence, Max whispered, glancing around to ensure they weren't overheard.
"Hey, Jay. Can I talk to you for a minute?"
Jay continued feeding papers into the machine, not looking up. "Of course."
Max fidgeted awkwardly, hands shoved deep in his pockets. "Look, about that whole joke hire thing I said when I got back. I've been thinking about it, and I realize I was an ass. I thought you already knew about Leo's... habits. It was just office nonsense, you know? Doesn't mean anything real."
Jay's hands stilled on the papers. "It's fine. Really."
"No, it's not fine. I made you feel like crap, and that wasn't my intention. Luc was in the same situation when he started—Leo brought him in for ridiculous reasons—and look at him now. He's one of our most valued team members. What matters is the work you do, and honestly? You're doing great work, man."
Jay forced himself to meet Max's eyes, manufacturing a smile. "I appreciate that. But really, no worries. It's all good."
"Is it though? Because I've been told you've been different since then. Quieter. And I can't help thinking that's my fault."
"I'm just focusing on work," Jay said, returning to his collating.
Max reached out and touched his shoulder briefly, the gesture surprisingly gentle from someone so physically exuberant. "Well, for what it's worth, I'm sorry. You didn't deserve that."
"Thanks," Jay said, and he meant it, even though the apology couldn't undo the knowledge that now lived permanently in his mind.
Max lingered for another moment, clearly wanting to say more, then seemed to recognize that he had done what he could. He squeezed Jay's shoulder once and walked away, leaving Jay alone with the copy machine and the weight of good intentions.
At lunch, Jay joined the team at their usual table in the nearby bistro. The invitation had become automatic, and his acceptance equally routine, but the dynamic felt different from his early weeks. He sat at the edge of the conversation, eating his sandwich while others discussed weekend plans.
"Did you see the email about the Marburg account restructuring?" Amy asked the table generally.
"Nightmare waiting to happen," Kim replied. "Their current portfolio allocation makes no sense."
"Could be an opportunity though," Ela added. "If we can demonstrate better performance metrics..."
Jay understood the technical references, could have contributed insights based on the documentation he had processed, but something held him back. Not lack of knowledge, but a bone-deep certainty that his perspective wasn't wanted, that his participation would at best be tolerated.
Luc caught his eye across the table and smiled—an expression of friendship that Jay forced himself to return. But even Luc's kindness felt complicated now, filtered through the knowledge that they shared a history as Leo's joke hires. Was Luc's friendship real, or was it the solidarity of the similarly positioned?
That evening, Jay sat in his apartment at his small dining table with a bowl of reheated curry, replaying the day’s events in his mind. Max's apology had been sincere, his colleagues' continued friendliness appeared honest, and his work performance remained strong. By any objective measure, his situation was stable and even positive.
But subjective experience told a different story. The ease he had felt during his first month—the sense of belonging somewhere, of building something meaningful—had been replaced by a performance. He played the role of competent administrative support, pleasant colleague, reliable team member. But underneath the facade, he felt like a fool.
His probation period would end in a week. The thought of getting a permanent position should have been elating. It would mean job security and validation of his capabilities. Instead, Jay hoped the decision might be taken out of his hands. If Turner & Associates decided not to offer him a contract, he would have an honorable exit from a situation that had become increasingly uncomfortable.
The thought surprised him with its appeal. To leave with his dignity intact, his work record unblemished, his relationships with colleagues cordial if distant. To escape the constant question of whether he was valued for his contributions or tolerated for reasons he couldn't control.
But even as the fantasy offered temporary comfort, practical reality intruded. Where would he go? Back to the job market that had already proven so hostile? Back to searching for opportunities while wondering which employers might have the wrong motivations for hiring him?
Jay cleared away his dinner dishes and prepared for bed. He was tired—not physically, but spiritually. Tired of wondering about motivations, tired of second-guessing every interaction, tired of the constant vigilance required to protect himself from disappointment.
Whatever decision awaited him, Jay would accept it with the same resignation he had cultivated over the past month. 
Scene 5
The contract
The calendar reminder appeared on Jay's screen at nine-fifteen Monday morning: "Performance Review - I. Hagen - 2:00 PM." Three months had passed since his first day at Turner & Associates, making him eligible for the standard probationary evaluation that would determine his permanent status with the company.
Jay stared at the notification with the same detachement he brought to most aspects of his work life these days. The review would ultimately be completely irrelevant to his sense of self-worth. He would receive feedback, accept whatever decision Ian made, and either leave or continue performing his duties.  
At two o'clock precisely, Jay knocked on Ian's office door and entered when summoned. Ian sat behind his desk with a folder open before him—Jay's personnel file, presumably, containing documentation of his performance over the past three months.  
"Please, sit," Ian said, gesturing to the chair across from his desk. His tone had none of the cool formality that usually characterized their interactions. Instead, he sounded almost... careful.  
Jay sat on the chair, notepad ready though he doubted he would need it. Performance reviews were typically one-way communications.  
"I want to begin by acknowledging the quality of your work," Ian started, consulting the papers before him. "Your performance has been consistently excellent. The compliance audit you completed exceeded expectations, and your coordination work on the Hartwell acquisition was integral to our success."  
Jay nodded politely. "Thank you."  
"Your colleagues have provided uniformly positive feedback. Amy specifically mentioned your reliability under pressure. Luc praised your analytical thinking. Even Kim, who rarely comments on administrative work, noted your technical accuracy."  
The praise should have felt meaningful, but it seemed to reach Jay through layers of cotton wool. He heard the words, understood their positive intent, but felt no corresponding emotional response.  
"That's very kind of them," Jay replied.  
Ian paused, watching Jay's face with an intensity that made him uncomfortable. "Jay, I want to address something directly. I know the transition to this office hasn't been entirely smooth. The... commentary from some team members about your hiring may have been difficult."  
The phrasing told Jay that Ian knew exactly what Max had said, understood the implications, and was attempting to address the damage without explicitly acknowledging it. A diplomatic approach that protected everyone's dignity while solving nothing.  
"I appreciate your concern, but I understand my role here. I'm grateful for the opportunity."  
"Jay, I want to be clear about something. You were hired because we needed competent administrative support. Your performance has exceeded those initial requirements. The work you've produced would be impressive from someone with twice your experience."
Jay met Ian's eyes briefly before looking away. "I try to do my best."
"You succeed. Consistently. Which brings me to the formal outcome of this review. I'm recommending you for permanent employment with Turner & Associates. The position comes with a ten percent salary increase, a standard three year contract and full benefits eligibility."
The offer should have been thrilling. Three months ago, permanent status at a prestigious firm would have felt like winning the lottery. Now it simply felt like an extension of his current circumstances—stable, but emotionally taxing.
"That's very generous. I accept."  
Ian frowned at the flat response. "Jay, you've earned this position. This isn't charity. Your work has been exemplary, and we want to retain that level of competence."  
"I understand. Thank you for the opportunity."
"I..." Ian hesitated, seeming to wrestle with something. "I realize I may have been overly formal in our previous interactions. If that contributed to any sense that your contributions weren't valued, I regret that impression."  
The words came as close to an apology as Ian was likely capable of offering. Jay recognized the gesture for what it was—an acknowledgment that Ian's coldness had been noticed and regretted. But knowing that Ian felt bad didn't make him feel better.  
"You've always been professional," Jay replied diplomatically. "I never expected anything else."
Ian's expression indicated he understood exactly what Jay wasn't saying. The conversation concluded with details about the contract, effective dates, and paperwork that needed completion. Throughout the discussion, Ian's tone remained warm, as if he were trying to compensate for months of deliberate distance.
When Jay returned to his desk, the news spread quickly through the office's informal communication network. Amy appeared first, offering congratulations and a brief hug that Jay accepted with appropriate gratitude. Luc followed, grinning broadly and promising that drinks were mandatory on Friday to celebrate the promotion.
"Turner & Associates permanent status," Ela announced dramatically. "You're officially one of us now. No escaping." 
Kim offered a smile and a brief "Good work" as he passed Jay's desk. Max, thankfully, was in meetings for most of the afternoon and missed the impromptu celebration that developed around the coffee station. 
"Friday drinks are non-negotiable," Luc insisted as the workday wound down. "Murphy's, six-thirty. We have traditions to maintain."
"Of course," Jay agreed. "I'll be there."  
But as his colleagues dispersed to their end-of-day activities, Jay felt no anticipation for the celebration. The permanent position was a practical victory, everything he should want, everything he had wanted just a few months ago.
Instead, he felt tired. The achievement was accepted with gratitude he didn't feel. Jay glanced toward Ian's office, where he was reviewing documents. The performance review had been fair, even generous. The awkward attempt at apology had been genuine, if limited by Ian's own emotional constraints.
Jay understood, intellectually, that he was valued for his contributions. His colleagues respected his work, Ian appreciated his competence, and the company wanted to retain his services. By any reasonable measure, he had succeeded in his goals.
But success felt indistinguishable from survival. He had earned his place by keeping his head down and performing his duties without complaint. The eager hope that had carried him through his first weeks had been systematically crushed, replaced by a resignation that protected him from further disappointment.  
Three months ago, permanent status at Turner & Associates would have felt like the beginning of a career, a foundation for growth and advancement. Now it seemed more like a comfortable prison. The contract would be signed, the salary increase would take effect, and Friday's celebration would be attended. Jay would continue to excel at his job, to exist in the space he had carved out for himself. Even if it felt like slowly rotting inside a respectable tomb.  
Emotional Stakes
Chapter 6: Regaining ground
Scene 1
Celebration
On Friday afternoon, the team gathered at the bar not far from their office that had become their unofficial headquarters for celebrations and commiserations. The walls had witnessed countless toasts to promotions and market victories over the years. To most of the team, the polished wood tables and craft cocktail menu felt like an extension of their desks.
For Jay, this marked only his second visit. He had avoided such gatherings during his probationary period, preferring the safety of his apartment to the uncertain dynamics of office socializing. Now, as the guest of honour celebrating his permanent employment, he sat at the end of a long table while his colleagues raised glasses in his direction.
Everyone had come, including Leo. Amy lifted her martini while Kim drank whiskey and Ela sipped wine between animated conversations. Max had ordered something colourful with too many garnishes, and Luc kept the mood light with jokes. Even Zoe had made the effort to attend, despite her general preference for quieter venues.
"To Jay. The first assistant who actually understands what Ian needs before he asks for it," Amy declared.
"That's because he reads the briefing materials instead of just skimming them," Kim added, raising his glass. "Revolutionary concept around here."
Laughter rippled around the table. Jay managed a smile, the kind he had perfected over recent weeks. The conversation moved on to client updates, the celebration becoming a comfortable backdrop to his own thoughts.
Everyone knew. They had all been aware from the beginning that Leo had hired him as another attempt to catch Ian's interest, another pretty face in a long line of failed experiments. While Jay had thrown himself into learning the intricacies of investment portfolios, convinced he was building something meaningful, his colleagues had been watching a performance play out.
Max appeared beside him, sliding into the chair that Zoe had vacated. The senior analyst's expression was puzzled, as if he still couldn't quite understand how his casual comment had landed so poorly.
"Jay, I wanted to explain about what I said when I got back." Max kept his tone conversational as he once again tried to smooth out the situation with his unfortunate comment. "The joke hire thing. I honestly didn't realize you weren't aware of the circumstances."
"It's fine, Max. Really."
"No, it's not. You looked like I'd punched you. Leo's always bringing in attractive assistants for Ian. It's been going on for years. Everyone just assumes new hires know the deal."
Jay focused on the condensation forming on his beer bottle. "I understand now."
"The thing is, none of them ever lasted more than a few weeks. They were either completely unqualified or caused some kind of drama. But you actually do the work. You understand what we do here. That's why you're staying."
"I appreciate you explaining."
Max waited for more, but Jay offered nothing else. After a moment, the older man squeezed his shoulder and returned to his conversation with Ela about upcoming client presentations.
Across the table, Luc caught Jay's eye and tilted his head in a silent question. When Jay responded with another measured smile, Luc looked unconvinced but didn't press. Instead, he smoothly redirected the conversation away from work entirely.
The lounge's entrance chimed, and the atmosphere changed.
Ian Hagen stepped inside. Conversations paused as heads turned. Ian's appearances at casual team events were so rare that it felt like a celebrity sighting. He wore an impeccable suit, though he had loosened his tie—a concession to the informal setting that somehow made him look more formidable instead.
"Ian!" Leo called out. "You actually came."
"Don't make it a regular expectation," Ian replied, accepting the chair that Amy quickly offered him.
"We're celebrating Jay's transition to permanent staff," Ela explained, though Ian clearly understood exactly why he was there.
"Congratulations," Ian said to Jay directly. The word was delivered with the same gravity he reserved for business announcements.
Jay felt heat rise in his cheeks under that direct attention. Ian made the gathering feel official. The fact that he had come at all sent a message that Jay struggled to interpret—was this mere workplace politeness or something more pointed?
The conversation resumed around them, but Ian remained on its periphery, contributing little beyond polite acknowledgements. Jay became conscious of every word he spoke, every reaction he displayed, wondering what Ian was observing and whether he was measuring up to some unspoken standard.
Ian's attendance felt deliberate. He had come here to make a point, though Jay couldn't decipher what that point might be. The man who had maintained distance for three months was now sitting six feet away, validating Jay's worth more deeply than formal contracts and performance reviews ever could.
After an hour, Ian caught Leo's eye and gave him a subtle nod toward the bar area. "Leo. A word."
Leo followed. They positioned themselves near the far end of the bar, far enough from the team's table that their voices wouldn't reach beyond the ambient noise.
"You need to fix this," Ian said, his voice sharpened with the steel that made subordinates straighten their spines.
"Fix what?"
"Don't play ignorant. Jay knows about your little scheme. Max told him he was a joke hire in front of half the office. He's been walking around like a ghost ever since."
"Shit." Leo's casual demeanour faltered. "When?"
"Weeks ago. Months of effort, and he finds out he was hired as entertainment."
"That's not—"
"That's exactly what it was." Ian cut him off smoothly. "And now he doesn't trust anything. His performance review, his permanent status, my assessment of his abilities. He thinks it's all part of some game."
"I didn't mean for him to find out like that. Max has the subtlety of a freight train."
"Max told the truth. You hired him hoping he'd catch my attention, just like all the others."
"Yes, but—"
"But nothing. You created this mess, Leo. The kid actually believed he'd found a place where his work mattered."
"He's good at the job. That has to count for something."
"It does. But try explaining that to someone who discovered he was the butt of an office joke."
Leo's his expression turned from defensive to regretful. "What do you want me to do?"
"Fix it."
"How?"
"You're the one who made him feel like a fool. You're the one who has to convince him he's not. Talk to him. Explain the situation properly. Make him understand that whatever your initial motivations were, his position is based on merit."
Leo nodded slowly. "You really think he'll listen?"
"I think he deserves better than office gossip and half-apologies." Ian turned to head back to the table, then paused. "And Leo? Next time you want to meddle in my personal life, try not to destroy someone else's confidence in the process."
"There won't be a next time."
"Good."
Leo watched Ian walk away, fighting to suppress the smile that threatened to break across his face. Ian was angry on Jay's behalf. The concern in his friend's voice, the way he'd framed Jay's wounded feelings—it all pointed to something Leo had been hoping to see.
Scene 2
Explanation
As the evening progressed toward midnight, the group began to fragment naturally. Amy and Zoe decided to share a taxi home, while Kim and Ela made plans to continue their night elsewhere. Ian made polite excuses about weekend responsibilities hours ago.
Jay saw his opportunity. He placed money on the table to cover his drinks and edged toward the exit while Luc was engaged in animated discussion with Max.
"Leaving already?"
Jay stopped at the threshold. Leo had materialized beside him, car keys already in hand.
"I should get home. It's been a long week."
"So it has. Come on, I'll drive you."
"That's not necessary. Metro runs all night."
"Metro is unreliable this late on Friday. My car's right outside."
Jay searched Leo's face for traces of the manipulation he now knew had shaped his entire employment. The charm that had once seemed generous now felt calculated. How many conversations had been strategic? How many moments of apparent kindness had served Leo's agenda?
"I appreciate the offer, but I prefer taking the train."
"Jay." Leo's voice had the same inflection he used when redirecting meetings or overruling junior staff suggestions. Not harsh, but unmistakably final. "It's midnight."
The practicality of the argument didn't mask its underlying nature. This wasn't a friendly gesture between colleagues—it was Leo Turner, son of the company owner, making a decision that Jay was expected to accept. The same dynamic that had brought Jay to Turner & Associates in the first place, wrapped in reasonable-sounding concern.
Jay wanted to refuse, to walk away and preserve what little autonomy he could claim. But Leo was already moving toward the door, as if he never doubted that his decisions would be followed. Jay felt powerless as he recognized the futility of resistance.
Leo's BMW waited at the curb, its pristine interior representing more wealth than Jay had ever been close to. The engine started silently, and they merged into the light midnight traffic.
"Address?" Leo asked.
Jay provided directions to his neighbourhood, then focused on the city passing outside his window. The streets felt different at this hour—emptier and stripped of their daytime pretenses.
Leo didn't attempt conversation during the twenty-minute drive, apparently content with the jazz playing through the sound system. Only when they reached Jay's building did he speak again.
"I'm coming up."
"Leo, I'm exhausted. Can we discuss whatever this is on Monday?"
"Five minutes." Leo was already out of the car. "There are things you need to understand."
Jay's apartment occupied the fourth floor of a charming prewar building that maintained its original character despite its age. The absence of a lift meant that residents had to climb a narrow but well-maintained staircase, with walls painted in beige that complemented the building's vintage charm. He unlocked his door and stepped aside, watching Leo inspect the surroundings. 
It was a small studio, furnished with carefully chosen pieces placed strategically to maximize the available space. A compact sofa faced a wooden coffee table. To the left, a tiny kitchenette featured white cabinets and a narrow counter, with a small dining table tucked into the corner. A tall bookshelf stood against the far wall, its shelves neatly organized with books, a few practical items and a small plant. The bed remained hidden behind a closet that created the illusion of separate rooms.
Despite the apartment's size constraints, everything was in excellent condition, creating a welcoming and uncluttered atmosphere.
"This is nice," Leo said, his eyes taking in the pristine arrangements. "Very comfortable."
Jay remained near the door, hoping Leo would recognize the hint and make this brief.
Instead, Leo sat on the couch as if he belonged there, his expensive suit a stark contrast against the well maintained but modest furnishings. His earlier casual aura had disappeared entirely.
"You know why I'm here."
"Not really."
"Max told me about your reaction to his comment. You've been different ever since—quieter, more withdrawn. You think you're some kind of a joke."
Jay moved to the kitchen area, filling a glass with water to give his hands something to do. "Aren't I?"
The directness of the question seemed to catch Leo off guard. He ran his fingers through his hair, disturbing its styling.
"Alright. You want complete honesty? Yes, appearance mattered when I hired you. The CFO's assistant is a client-facing position. You'll accompany Ian to meetings, handle client communications, represent the company. Being presentable isn't optional in this business."
"I see."
"But that wasn't the main factor. Ian is... particular. Finding the right assistant for him is incredibly difficult. He needs someone conscientious but also agreeable. Someone who won't push back when he's under pressure or try to manage his decisions for him."
Jay waited, unsure where this was heading.
"The problem is, in our industry, people with impressive credentials often come with equally impressive egos. They're stubborn, argumentative, convinced they know better than their boss. And I'm terrible at reading people during interviews. I make snap judgements that are wrong more often than right. Every assistant I've hired before fell into one of two categories. Either they couldn't handle the workload and crashed within weeks, or they were calculating. They saw Ian as a stepping stone, tried to use their position to advance their own careers or gain inside information. I kept picking people who were good at pretending to be agreeable, but they all had ulterior motives."
"So?"
"The joke hire comment was about my own terrible track record, not your qualifications. But you were different. Despite having modest credentials on paper, you're dedicated. You learned the role thoroughly and perform it better than any of your predecessors. You're exactly what Ian needed, even if I stumbled into hiring you for partially superficial reasons. It's more than I ever hoped to achieve."
Jay stared at his reflection in the kitchen window, processing this information. 
"I thought I'd found something real. A place where I belonged because of what I could contribute, not because of how I looked."
"You did find that place."
"Did I? Or am I just another experiment that happened to work out better than expected?"
Leo got up, smoothing his jacket. "That's something you'll have to decide for yourself. But consider this—Ian's never attended a celebration for any previous employee. He came tonight specifically to show that he takes you seriously."
Jay turned from the window. "What do you mean?"
"Ian doesn't do casual appearances. Everything he does has purpose." 
The door closed behind Leo, leaving Jay alone in his small apartment. He remained standing in his kitchen, contemplating the conversation and its implications. Leo's explanation had provided answers to questions that had been eating at him, but it had also raised new uncertainties about his place at Turner & Associates.
The safe haven he thought he had found might have been built on foundations he hadn't understood. But perhaps it had evolved into something more authentic.
Scene 3
Resolve
The whiskey burned its way down Ian's throat, as he sat alone in the muted darkness of his living room. The space around him spoke of wealth and taste—furniture selected by interior designers, art chosen for investment value. It was a home that could appear in architectural magazines, photographed for its clean lines and sophisticated palette. It was also a home where Ian spent as little time as possible. 
The silence pressed against him from all sides. No music, no television, no distractions from the thoughts that had been circling his mind since the previous evening. He had deliberately avoided turning on any lights beyond a single lamp in the corner, preferring the half-darkness that matched his mood. The city glittered beyond the windows like scattered diamonds, but he kept his back to the view. 
The liquor burned pleasantly as it slid down his throat, each sip a small punishment for his own behavior. He had overstepped. The realization sat uncomfortably in his chest, a foreign sensation that he couldn't dismiss. Thirteen years at Turner & Associates, and he had never once attended an informal team gathering. His absence from such events was so established that colleagues probably assumed he was physically incapable of casual socializing.  Yet last night, he had walked into Murphy's like it was the most natural thing in the world.  More than that, he had confronted Leo directly, pushing him to explain the circumstances of Jay's hiring. He had essentially demanded that his friend clean up a mess that was causing distress to a subordinate.  
The whiskey provided no answers, only the burn that had become a reliable companion over the years.  Ian tried to convince himself that his actions stemmed from simple fairness. Jay had been performing his duties competently for three months, only to discover that his colleagues viewed him as the latest iteration of Leo's  schemes. Any decent manager would want to correct such a misunderstanding, to ensure that merit was properly recognized.  But the explanation felt thin.  
Jay had never given him a single reason for suspicion. Three months of observation, of watching for tells and inconsistencies, and Jay had remained exactly what he appeared to be: awkward, reserved, committed to doing good work. There had been no signs of the calculated charm that Sam Connelly had wielded so effectively. 
Ian had wanted to catch Jay in some deception. He had been prepared for it, almost eager to confirm his suspicions and validate his defensive instincts. Instead, he had watched a young man throw himself into learning the intricacies of investment management with an enthusiasm that seemed entirely authentic.  
The background check Leo had conducted was already detailed, but Ian had commissioned his own investigation through a private firm that specialized in corporate security. The report had been exhaustive, tracing Jay's life from childhood through his employment history. Every reference had been contacted, every claim verified, every gap explained. 
The picture that emerged was of a man who had never quite found his place in the world. Teachers remembered him as diligent but socially isolated. University records showed solid academic performance but minimal extracurricular involvement.
His previous employers described him as reliable and conscientious, though two of the departures had been rather abrupt.  The details were illuminating beyond what formal documentation could capture. A manager from his first company had mentioned Jay's obvious discomfort around a supervisor who had blatantly sexually harassed him, and HR's dismissive response to his complaints. A colleague from his last job had described how Jay had quit after a coworker's persistent advances made him visibly uncomfortable.
The pattern was consistent: a young man who had learned early that the systems meant to protect him were unreliable, who had developed wariness as a survival mechanism. Ian had never seen anyone look as crushed as Jay had when Max's casual comment revealed the truth about his hiring. The expression of betrayal and humiliation couldn't have been manufactured.
Ian grappled with an uncomfortable realization. Despite his efforts to maintain emotional distance, despite his determination to avoid repeating past mistakes, he had been monitoring Jay with a persistence that showed he cared about the outcome.
More than cared. The admission sat uneasily in his mind, a violation of the defences he had constructed over the years.  He had been watching Jay not just for signs of deception, but for signs of well-being. He had noticed when Jay's initial enthusiasm began to fade, when his eager questions became more cautious, when his posture grew more defensive. Ian had told himself he was simply observing a potential threat, but it was more complicated.  
He had been concerned.  
The realization demanded action, though Ian wasn't sure what form that action should take. Jay deserved better than the impersonal supervision he had received. Ian could provide guidance without compromising himself. He could provide support without risking emotional entanglement. It was possible to be fair, supportive, and decent while maintaining the distance that protected them both.  
The whiskey had grown warm in his hand, its expensive taste wasted on his distracted palate. He set it aside and walked to the windows. Somewhere out there, Jay was probably struggling with his own questions about belonging and worth, questions that Ian could help answer through proper mentorship. Nothing intimate, nothing that would muddy the lines they both needed to maintain. Just guidance that Jay had been seeking from the beginning, that Ian should have been providing all along.  
Even if the ache in his chest pointed at motivations that went beyond obligation, Ian understood the practical realities. Jay's history made him vulnerable, which called for protection, not romantic advances. The difference in their social standing, their ages, the power imbalance between them—all of it created a situation where any personal interest would be inappropriate at best, harmful at worst. He wouldn't allow idle fantasies to compromise his judgment or Jay's well-being.  
But he could be the mentor that Jay had hoped to find when he first started at Turner & Associates. He could live up to the admiration he had seen in Jay's eyes during those early weeks, before disillusionment had replaced hope with resignation. 
The city lights stretched endlessly before him, each one representing dreams and ambitions and the endless human pursuit of something better. Ian had long ago stopped believing in such optimism for himself, but he could still recognize its value in others. He would do better. Not for the sake of connection or intimacy, but for the sake of his own integrity.
Ian returned to his chair and picked up his glass. There was work to be done, relationships to repair, and a young man's faith in mentorship to restore.  Ian could do that much, at least. Even if he couldn't—wouldn't—allow himself to want anything more.
Scene 4
Hope
On Monday morning, Jay was at his desk at seven-thirty. He organized his work and began tackling each task in order. But the office atmosphere was different, as if the weekend had reset more than just the calendar.
Ian arrived just before eight. Nothing in his demeanor hinted that Friday night had been anything out of the ordinary.
"Good morning," Ian said, pausing at Jay's desk. 
"Good morning."
"I wanted to follow up on last week's portfolio summary. The analysis was detailed and the recommendations sound. Well done."
Jay felt heat rise in his cheeks. "Thank you."
"I've scheduled a meeting with the client tomorrow morning. I'd like you to prepare supplementary materials—risk assessments and performance projections for the reallocations."
"Of course. I'll have everything ready by end of business today."
"If you have questions about the projections, don't hesitate to ask. The client can be particular about presentation format."
"I will. Thank you."
Ian moved on to his office, leaving Jay with the tasks of research and analysis. The morning passed uneventfully. Phone calls were answered, emails were processed, and the financial management continued without drama. 
"Morning, Jay," Max cheerfully called from the elevator. "How was your weekend?"
"Quiet. Productive." Jay looked up from his screen, offering a smile. "Yours?"
"Caught up on some reading. Nothing exciting." 
Max continued toward his office, leaving Jay with the distinct impression that the awkwardness between them had dissolved. It was, he realized, a simple interaction that had always characterized their workplace—straightforward, without hidden agendas.
Twenty minutes later, Luc appeared by Jay’s desk, holding his coffee mug.
“Heading to the kitchen for coffee. Want to come?”
Jay looked up and nodded. “Sure, I’ll join you.” 
"Excellent. I was hoping someone would save me from my own company."
Luc led the way down the short corridor, and Jay fell into step beside him. They reached the kitchen doorway together and were greeted by the aroma of fresh coffee as they stepped inside. Jay prepared his coffee while Luc leaned against the counter.
"You seem better today," Luc observed.
"Do I?"
"Less like you're waiting for something terrible to happen."
"Maybe I was."
"Understandable. New jobs can be stressful until you figure out how everything works."
The simplicity of the explanation struck Jay as accurate. He had been waiting for something terrible to happen—not because the job was new, but because experience had taught him that security was temporary, that kindness came with conditions he couldn't meet.
But looking around the kitchen, Jay wondered if he had been preparing for disasters that existed only in his imagination.
At noon, Amy appeared at his desk. "Lunch at Willow & Sage?"
"Sure."
The brisk walk from the office felt invigorating, and once they sat at a large table, the bistro seemed even cosier. 
“It’s like a freezer in my flat. Maintenance says they’ll get to it next week. Next week, my ass,” Ela complained, tugging her scarf loose but keeping it draped around her shoulders.
“You should move into my building,” Zoe said, flipping through the menu without looking up. “Heating’s reliable, and there’s a bakery downstairs. You’d live on croissants.”
“Tempting,” Ela admitted.
“Speaking of temptation—there’s a place I found last weekend. Handmade pasta, truffle oil on everything. You’d love it, Amy. The plating alone is art,” Zoe continued.
“I’m in,” Ela said immediately. “As long as I can feel my fingers while I’m eating.”
The conversation flowed around personal topics. No one put Jay on the spot or demanded his participation, but space was left for him to contribute when he chose to. When Amy asked about his thoughts on a recent market development, he offered his analysis without feeling like he was being tested. 
The food was good as always—rich and well-prepared. Jay ate with appetite, unlike the robotic chewing that had characterized most of his meals lately.
"I hadn’t realized how much I missed being here," he said to Amy as they walked back to the office.
Jay approached the afternoon tasks with mild enthusiasm. The portfolio projections required careful formatting, a detailed assignment that he had always enjoyed.
When he finished the supplementary materials an hour before the official end of business, he felt pride in his analysis. The risk assessments were comprehensive and the presentation format was polished.
"Everything ready for tomorrow?" Ian asked, appearing at his desk as Jay was organizing the final documents.
"All set. I included alternative scenarios for the more volatile holdings."
Ian accepted the folder and spent several minutes reviewing the contents.
"This is excellent work," Ian said finally. "Exactly what the client will need to make informed decisions."
"Thank you."
Jay felt warmth unfurl in his chest, a sensation he had almost forgotten was possible. Not just relief at avoiding criticism, but satisfaction at work well done and properly recognized.
Nothing had changed—his responsibilities were identical, his colleagues were the same people they had been on Friday. But the lens through which he viewed these constant elements had changed. Max's cheerful greeting was simply friendliness, not a test of his ability to fit in. Luc's invitation to the kitchen was casual camaraderie, not a judgement of his social skills. 
Ian’s demeanour was strictly businesslike, but it was never unkind.
Jay had been so prepared for mistreatment that he had interpreted ordinary interactions as potentially hostile. The revelation was both embarrassing and liberating.
Maybe he’d let himself be overly dramatic, allowing the humiliating circumstances of his hiring tint everything that came after. In reality, no one else seemed to care, his colleagues treating him like any other new hire. The only unpleasant thing that had happened to him was Max's initial thoughtless comment, and Max had apologized once he realized his mistake.
Perhaps he’d misread the rest—the perceived coldness, the sense of being on the periphery—and the office was simply what it seemed: civil, capable people.
The test of this new perspective came unexpectedly on Tuseday morning when Ian stopped by his desk with a request that made Jay's stomach drop.
"I have a client meeting at two o'clock. Mayfield wants to review their portfolio allocation. I'd like you to attend and take notes."
Jay felt his composure falter. "You want me in the meeting?"
"Yes. It will be good experience for you to observe how client relationships are managed. Bring your laptop."
After Ian returned to his office, Jay spent the morning in barely controlled panic. He had never attended a client meeting before—at least, not in any meaningful sense. The Hartwell negotiation technically counted, but he’d spent most of it in the background fetching documents and trying not to be noticed. This would be different. He’d be sitting at the table, expected to follow along.
While he’d had minor interactions with clients, answering phones, replying to emails, occasionally greeting them at reception or escorting them to conference rooms, he had never actually been part of a meeting as a participant, face-to-face with the wealthy investors who comprised Turner & Associates’ clientele. What if he said something inappropriate?
At one-fifty, Jay gathered his laptop and headed for the conference room. Ian was already there, reviewing documents, when Jay entered.
"Take a seat near the back. You're here to observe and record."
Mayfield representatives arrived promptly—three middle-aged executives in expensive suits who greeted Ian warmly. Jay remained silent in his corner, trying to project competence while his pulse hammered in his ears.
The meeting proceeded exactly as advertised: a review of investment performance, strategic adjustments, and market projections. Jay dutifully recorded the key points, relieved that the discussion followed predictable patterns that made note-taking manageable. Ian fielded questions and provided detailed explanations without once glancing in Jay's direction.
When the clients left forty-five minutes later, Jay felt drained but proud. He had managed to sit through his first high-level client meeting without embarrassing himself or Ian.
"Could you prepare a brief summary of the key action items we discussed?" Ian asked once they were alone.
Jay spent the next hour crafting a concise overview of the meeting's outcomes, double-checking his notes to ensure accuracy. When he delivered the summary to Ian's office, he watched nervously as Ian read through it.
"This is exactly what I needed. Clear and well-organized. Thank you."
As Jay prepared to leave for the day, cleaning up his desk and reviewing tomorrow's schedule, he felt something he hadn't expected to feel: cautious optimism. Not the desperate hope that had characterized his early weeks, but a timid belief that he might actually belong here after all.
Scene 5
Trust
The days since Monday had slipped by, each one pushing Jay a little further from the shock of learning the truth of his hiring. He remained subdued, though the tight knot of anxiety in his chest had loosened. Thursday unfolded in the same way as any other workday, but by three-thirty that afternoon, the atmosphere changed.
Jay heard the commotion before he saw it—urgent phone calls, rapid conversations, the loud sound of keyboards. Amy rushed past his desk with a stack of files, her normally composed expression strained.
"What's happening?" Jay asked Luc, who was pulling documents from a filing cabinet with unusual speed.
"Fairfield Industries. Their board meeting got moved up to tomorrow morning, and they need our full portfolio analysis tonight." Luc grabbed another folder. "Ian's coordinating the response."
Jay recognized the name immediately. Fairfield Industries was one of their most important clients—one that Ian had brought in himself nearly a decade ago and still managed personally. Everyone in the office understood what that meant: every report, every analysis for Fairfield had to be flawless. There was no margin for error.
Jay watched Ian emerge from his office, moving through the chaos with the same calm he always displayed. He spoke briefly to Max, then Amy, systematically assigning tasks. The office erupted into motion, dominated by a sudden surge of restlessness unlike anything Jay had seen there.
"Jay." Ian's voice cut through the activity. Jay looked up to find Ian standing beside his desk. "I need you to stay late tonight. We have documentation to compile for the Fairfield presentation. Are you available?"
The request was direct and businesslike. Jay felt a flutter of something—not quite nervousness, but heightened awareness. This was the first time Ian had personally asked him to handle something beyond ordinary tasks.
"Of course," Jay said. "What do you need me to do?"
"Start with the quarterly reports from the last twelve months. Pull the relevant sections and organize them chronologically. I'll have the specific parameters ready in twenty minutes."
Ian returned to his office, leaving Jay to gather the necessary files. Around him, the office buzzed with urgency. Even Kim worked without complaint, double-checking the secure connection for Fairfield and monitoring the data transfer to make sure nothing went wrong at the last minute. Jay got caught up in the momentum, relieved to have clear direction and a concrete role to play.
The next three hours were consumed by paperwork and numbers. Jay compiled reports and organized materials according to Ian's specifications. The work was demanding, leaving no room for his self-consciousness. When other colleagues finished their portions and departed, Jay barely noticed. He was absorbed in the task, determined to complete it properly.
At half past ten, he encountered a problem. A file contained a reference to a subsidiary acquisition that didn't match the timeline in the reports. Jay stared at the discrepancy, uncertain whether he'd made an error or if the documents themselves were inconsistent. He checked his work twice, but the numbers refused to align.
The office had grown quiet. Only a few lights remained on, casting long shadows across empty desks. Jay could see Ian working in his office, illuminated by the glow of his computer screen. The memory of their past awkward interaction—his ill-timed question about the acquisition, Ian's swift dismissal—made him hesitate. But the deadline loomed, and he couldn't resolve the discrepancy alone.
Jay approached Ian's office and knocked on the open door. Ian looked up from his screen.
"I'm having trouble with the timeline," Jay said. "There's a discrepancy in the acquisition dates that I can't reconcile."
Ian saved his work and turned to face him completely. "Show me."
Jay laid out the documents on Ian's desk, pointing to the conflicting information. Ian examined the papers with a focused expression.
"This subsidiary was acquired through a shell company," Ian explained, pulling up additional files on his computer. "The official filing date doesn't reflect the actual transaction date. See here—" He pointed to a specific line in the contract. "The effective date is backdated to align with the fiscal quarter. That's why the timeline appears inconsistent."
Ian walked him through the reasoning. The explanation was detailed, delivered without the slightest hint of impatience. Ian answered Jay's follow-up questions, ensuring Jay understood not just the situation but the full history.
"Does that resolve the issue?" Ian asked.
"Yes, completely. Thank you."
Ian returned to his computer, and Jay gathered the documents. The interaction had been exactly what it appeared to be—a manager helping an employee solve a problem. No hidden irritation, just competent guidance.
They worked side by side for another hour, alone in the office. Jay finished his portion of the documentation and began preparing the final presentation folder. Ian reviewed each section, making minor adjustments and additions.
At eleven-fourty, Ian closed his laptop and got up. "That should be sufficient for tomorrow's meeting."
Jay gathered his things, feeling exhausted but also satisfied from work completed properly. As he reached for his coat, Ian spoke again.
"Good work tonight."
The acknowledgement was brief. Jay nodded his thanks and headed for the elevator. Outside, night had already fallen, leaving just sparse headlights on the road and the occasional passerby.
Jay walked to the metro station. The evening replayed in his mind—not the crisis or the long hours, but the moment when he'd needed help and received it. Ian's response had been exactly what a good manager should provide, professional and supportive.
The realization dawned on him gradually as he waited for the train. He had been living with a tension he hadn’t fully acknowledged—the expectation of disappointment, the anticipation of rejection. But tonight wad different. When he'd needed guidance, he'd received it. When he'd completed his work, it had been acknowledged.
As the train pulled into the station, Jay caught himself thinking something he hadn't dared to consider in weeks. "Maybe I don't have to keep flinching. Maybe, in this one small corner of the world, I can actually just do my job."
Chapter 7: A sense of security
Scene 1
Optimism
Expand mother's call about birthdays?
The next morning, the office had the worn-out yet relieved atmosphere that follows a crisis handled well. The Fairfield Industries presentation had gone flawlessly, and by noon, word spread that the client had approved the portfolio recommendations without a single revision.
Amy came in beaming. “No revisions. Not a single one,” she announced, holding up her phone for emphasis. 
Kim didn’t bother hiding his joy, but he still muttered, “Let’s not get used to it.”
Ian appeared in the middle of the open space at one-thirty, addressing the office with his characteristic economy of words. "The Fairfield matter is concluded successfully. Everyone did exceptional work under pressure. Take the rest of the day off."  
The announcement sparked a ripple of surprised murmurs. Jay watched his colleagues gather their things with the bemused smiles of people granted an unexpected gift. Within twenty minutes, the office had emptied except for Ian, who remained at his desk, apparently immune to his own directive.
Jay packed his laptop and left as well. Outside, the afternoon sun felt warmer than expected for late autumn. He walked to the metro, arriving at his apartment at two o'clock to find himself in the unusual position of having nothing urgent to accomplish. He changed into casual clothes and considered his options. The thought of spending the afternoon indoors felt wasteful. He stood in the hallway for a moment, unsure of what to do with himself, until his eyes landed on a book he'd bought long ago and never opened. It felt like the right time. He slipped it into his bag and made his way to Café Lemon.
Since it was still early, the café wasn’t very busy. Only a few patrons occupied the tables, creating a homely atmosphere. He ordered a cardamom latte and an almond croissant, sitting at his preferred table near the window. The barista delivered his order with a friendly nod. 
Jay opened his book to the first page and began to read. The afternoon passed in a manner he'd almost forgotten was possible. The novel occupied his attention, the coffee remained warm in his hands, and the street outside provided a gentle backdrop of activity. Jay felt no need to monitor his surroundings. He simply existed in the moment, reading about space travels and sipping his drink.
Somewhere between the second and third chapters, his thoughts drifted to the previous evening. The crisis had revealed things Ian he always knew to be true but had never thought much about—competence under pressure and reliability.
When Jay had needed help, Ian had provided it. It had been given freely, without making Jay feel diminished for asking.  Jay realized he'd maintained a defensive posture for so long that he'd forgotten what it felt like to simply trust someone's consistency. Ian might be overbearing at times, but he was also fundamentally dependable. When he said he would do something, he did it. When he offered guidance, he meant it. The reliability felt almost foreign after years of dealing with workplace environments built on hidden agendas.
The recognition required swallowing some wounded pride. Jay had spent weeks interpreting Ian's reserve as judgement. But perhaps the distance had been maintained not out of disdain but out of prudent management. Looking back, Jay couldn't identify a single instance where Ian had been unfair or unkind. Strict, certainly. Demanding, without question. But never cruel. 
Despite everything that had happened—the circumstances of his hiring, the awkward early weeks, his own defensive reactions—it felt remarkable to work for someone he could actually rely on.
Jay finished the croissant and ordered another latte, returning to his book, considering the afternoon well spent. The clientele changed from temperate coffee drinkers to a chattier evening crowd. He read three more chapters before the light began to fade, feeling peaceful. 
Jay returned home that evening refreshed, and when Saturday morning came, he decided to tackle the overdue obligation he’d been avoiding for weeks. He made coffee in his small kitchen and dialed his mother's number. The conversation he'd been postponing felt necessary now, grounded in a reality that finally seemed stable enough to share.  
"Jay! I was wondering when you'd call. How are you managing in the city?"  
"I'm doing well, actually. I found a new job a few months ago. It's working out better than I expected."  
"What kind of work?"  
"Administrative assistant at an investment firm. It's more prestigious than my last position, better environment." Jay kept the details deliberately vague, unwilling to explain the complications of his hiring or the background of Turner & Associates. His mother wouldn't understand the significance, and the phone call felt inappropriate for complex explanations.  
"That sounds promising. Are you earning enough to manage your expenses?"  
"Yes, the salary is fair. I'm adjusting well."
They talked for another ten minutes about practical matters—his health, the weather, his mother's work. Jay answered her questions without elaborating, providing just enough information to satisfy her concern without inviting deeper inquiry. When she mentioned his stepfather's promotion and his half-sister's school achievements, he offered congratulations and asked polite follow-up questions.
The conversation ended with the same reserve that had marked their relationship ever since he’d moved to the city. Not cold, but not intimate either. They still cared about each other's well-being, but no longer confided in one another, content to exchange only the necessary pleasantries.
Jay’s mother had given birth to him when she was barely eighteen, and she never spoke about his father. When Jay was ten, she married his stepfather and two years later, his stepbrother was born. In the years that followed, she had two more children—a daughter and another son, the youngest sixteen years younger than Jay. He had never been close with any of them. By the time his siblings were born, he was already a teenager, and he always felt set apart from his mother’s new family. They never treated him unkindly, but he stood out—more guest than true member. As soon as he finished high school, Jay moved away for college and never returned, his visits home growing less frequent with each passing year. Now, he rarely visited more than once a year.
After hanging up, Jay realized why he'd delayed the call for so long. The situation at Turner & Associates had felt too uncertain, too fragile to treat as real. But his perspective had changed recently. The job felt solid now. Maybe not entirely comfortable, but secure.
He spent the rest of Saturday reading, cleaning his apartment, and preparing for the week ahead. By Sunday evening, he managed to finish the book he’d started on Friday, feeling quite happy with the small achievement. The weekend passed leisurely, giving him time to clear his head and look at his life with a fresh perspective. The prospect of returning to work on Monday seemed almost welcoming. Not exciting, but no longer grim. He had a place there, earned through his own efforts and maintained by his own reliability. The cautious optimism that had emerged over the past week remained, tempered by experience, but genuine.
Scene 2
Invitation
At nine o'clock on Monday, Jay's computer chimed with an email that disrupted the week's predictable arc. The subject line read "Celebration Dinner—Hartwell Success" and the sender was Ian himself.
Jay opened the message to find a formal invitation to Friday evening's dinner at The Palladium, an upscale restaurant he'd heard mentioned in conversations. The email explained that the Hartwell contract represented the largest single acquisition in Turner & Associates' recent history, warranting celebration with both company leadership and the entire team. Both Mr. Turner Senior and Leo would attend, along with all staff members who had contributed to the success.
Jay read the invitation twice, feeling the tightness in his stomach that always accompanied high-stakes social situations. He'd attended one company function since joining Turner & Associates—a quarterly gathering that had left him acutely aware of his position at the bottom of the office hierarchy. The prospect of another formal event, with senior leadership present, felt equally daunting. 
He minimized the email and opened a new browser tab, searching for information about The Palladium. The restaurant's website featured elegant photographs of crystal glassware and artfully arranged plates. The dress code was listed as "business formal," which at least provided clear guidance. He thought of the blue suit hanging in his closet—purchased specifically for the last company function and worn only once. At least he wouldn't feel completely out of place.
The morning passed on mundane tasks, but Jay periodically returned to the dinner invitation. Around him, his colleagues discussed the upcoming event with evident enthusiasm. Amy mentioned making reservations at a nearby salon for Friday afternoon. Kim wondered aloud whether the wine selection would justify the evening's inevitable length. 
At lunch, the team gathered at Captain's Table, a seafood restaurant close the office that was Ela's favourite. Jay ordered something simple, contributing minimally to the conversation.  
"Has everyone seen the dinner invitation?" Amy asked, cutting into her salmon. "The Palladium is supposed to be exceptional." 
"I've eaten there twice," Max said. "The food is remarkable, but the atmosphere is rather stuffy. All white tablecloths and hushed conversations."  
"Perfect for impressing the senior Turner," Ela added.
Jay fidgeted in his seat, trying to appear casual. "I have to admit, I'm not entirely comfortable with these upscale places. I always feel like I'm going to use the wrong fork or something."  
The admission drew understanding nods from several colleagues. Luc grinned and pointed his own fork at Max. "You don't have anything to worry about. Max will inevitably make some completely inappropriate comment about the waitstaff, and everyone will be too busy trying not to laugh to notice anyone else's table manners."  
"I resent that characterization," Max replied with an exaggerated sigh. "My comments are always perfectly appropriate for the situation."  
"Right. Like when you asked the event planner if the floral arrangements were supposed to look like they’d come from a supermarket," Kim said.
Max waved dismissively. "That was a legitimate question."  
Even Jay laughed at the exchange. In hindsight, Max’s blunt remarks about his appearance seemed less mortifying than he’d remembered—more a product of Max’s lack of tact than anything personal. Max never singled anyone out, his offhanded comments were just part of how he handled social situations, careless but never truly mean-spirited.
“See?” Luc said, nodding toward Jay’s smile. “With Max around, all you have to do is stand behind him and let him soak up the attention. No one’s going to notice you with him in the room.”
“Unbelievable. My honesty is a rare commodity in this world, and all I get is grief.” Max protested.
The conversation drifted into stories of awkward company dinners, and Ela immediately jumped in. “Do you remember that holiday dinner at Rossi’s, when Allen was still around?”
Kim snorted. “How could I forget? He was three glasses in and started ranting about corporate culture—right in front of Ian.”
Ela grinned. “Didn’t he call Ian ‘the most uptight man in a fifty-kilometre radius’?”
Kim mimicked the voice: “‘If you ever want to learn how to strangle the joy out of a room, just watch Ian at a social event.’” Kim shook his head, still amused. “The whole table went silent. I thought Ian was going to snap.”
Jay glanced at them, interested. “What did Ian do?”
Ela’s grin widened. “He just looked at Allen and said, dead serious, ‘My radius is at least a hundred kilometres.’”
Kim broke in, laughing. “Everyone lost it. Allen couldn’t even come up with a comeback. I think that’s when their feud started.”
Ela rolled her eyes. “Yeah, and then Allen went to work for the competition six months later.”
Jay listened, surprised. He hadn’t known that side of Ian existed. For a moment, he tried to picture it—the unflappable boss making a perfectly-timed joke. The image seemed almost alien.
The relaxed atmosphere around the table reflected their recent success—everyone, even Jay, seemed pleased with their work and comfortable with the recognition it had earned.
When they returned to the office, Jay felt some of his anxiety diminish. The dinner remained intimidating, but it no longer felt like a solitary ordeal. He would be surrounded by colleagues he'd grown to understand, attending an event that celebrated work he'd actually contributed to completing.
