That evening, Jay sat in his apartment, considering his wardrobe. He checked his good suit and polished his shoes, then sorted through his shirts and ties, trying to decide which combination looked most suitable for the occasion. He hesitated over each choice, wondering if they were good enough. 
For a moment, he wondered if he should buy another suit. He could afford it now, at least. But after a moment’s thought, he decided against it. The one he had was more than fine and perfectly adequate for the venue. He wasn’t trying to impress anyone—just get through the evening. He set everything in order and tried to put the dinner out of his mind.
The nervousness remained, but it felt manageable now. He'd attended similar events before. The dinner wasn't a test of his worthiness. It was just another obligation, made slightly more bearable by the knowledge that his colleagues would be equally uncomfortable with the formal atmosphere. Max would indeed make inappropriate comments, Amy would handle the social dynamics with ease, and Jay would simply need to dress appropriately and avoid drawing attention to himself. 
Scene 3
Dinner
The following week was more frantic than usual. The Hartwell contract was finalized, injecting new momentum into every project. The new client required immediate attention, and Ian competently coordinated the expanded workload.
The first formal briefing concerning Hartwell happened on Tuesday morning. Ian stood at the head of the conference table, outlining the portfolio requirements.
"The integration timeline is aggressive," Ian said, consulting his notes. "Amy, I need the full  market analysis completed by Thursday. Max, handle the domestic projections. Kim, your systems integration assessment is due Wednesday afternoon."
He continued down the list, assigning tasks to the whole team. When Ian reached his name, Jay felt a flutter of nervousness.
"Jay, coordinate the documentation review with Amy and ensure all materials are properly formatted for client delivery. Questions?"
Jay shook his head. Around the table, his colleagues asked practical questions about deadlines. The meeting concluded with the workload divided among the team members, while Ian remained at the helm of the project, personally handling the most crucial aspects.
As the week progressed, conversation increasingly turned to Friday's dinner. The women in the office engaged in animated discussions about attire, makeup and hair styling. Jay listened to these conversations with growing unease, acutely aware of his inexperience with formal dining protocols.
Thursday afternoon, as Jay struggled to concentrate over routine paperwork, Luc appeared at his desk
"You look like you're contemplating a difficult mathematical problem," Luc observed.
"I'm trying to figure out how to avoid embarrassing myself tomorrow night. I've never eaten at a place like The Palladium before. I keep imagining myself knocking over wine glasses."
"Ah, the classic anxiety of the overly fancy restaurant. What specifically are you worried about? The sixteen different forks or the wine selection that requires a PhD in viticulture?"
"All of it, honestly. I don't want to do something obviously wrong in front of Turner Senior."
"Here's the secret," Luc said, lowering his voice conspiratorially. "Nobody actually cares about the rules as much as you think they do. Just follow what everyone else does. Don't drink from the finger bowl. If someone offers wine, say yes and take small sips. If you're uncertain about anything, watch Ian—he handles these situations flawlessly."
"What about conversation? I never know what to say at these things."
"Ask questions about other people's work. Everyone enjoys talking about their expertise, and it keeps the focus off you."
Jay laughed despite his anxiety. "You make it sound almost manageable."
"That's because it is. Remember, Turner Senior is intimidating but predictable. He'll spend most of the evening discussing market conditions. Just nod thoughtfully, don't get drunk, and you'll be fine. The worst that can happen is you accidentally use the salad fork for the main course, and honestly, Max will probably do something far more embarrassing within the first hour."
"I appreciate the reassurance. Even if it comes at Max's expense."
"Max can handle it. Besides, he'd probably be offended if we didn't expect him to provide entertainment."
The practical advice helped more than Jay expected. As Thursday evening approached, he felt his preparation was as complete as it could be. The suit was pristine, his shoes polished, and Luc's guidance had provided a basic framework for managing the evening's social requirements.
Friday evening was unseasonably warm for late autumn. Jay took a taxi to The Palladium, arriving precisely at the designated time. The restaurant exceeded his expectations in elegance. Instead of the vast, impersonal space he'd imagined, it featured a series of smaller dining rooms. 
Their party had been seated in a private room that felt more like an exclusive club than a public restaurant. Jay counted twelve people: the seven regular team members, Ian, Leo, Turner Senior, and two senior partners he recognized but had never spoken to. 
Turner Senior commanded attention without effort—a man in his seventies who dominated the room despite his modest stature. Jay had seen him perhaps three times in the office corridors, always at a distance, always surrounded by an aura of authority.
"Jay, you're here," Ian said, rising briefly as Jay approached. "Take the seat between Luc and Kim."
Luc gave a thumbs-up, and Jay took his designated chair, relieved to be positioned between his colleagues. Everyone looked glamorous—women in smart dresses, men in well-fitted designer suits. Jay felt distinctly under-dressed by comparison, acutely aware of the ordinary cut of his jacket. It didn’t help that he caught Ian’s eyes on him more than once, though he couldn’t read anything in Ian’s expression. Meanwhile Kim launched into a commentary about the wine list.
"Look at these prices," Kim muttered, showing Jay the leather-bound menu. "Forty dollars for a glass of wine that probably costs them eight."
"Value assessment again?" Luc asked with a grin.
"Market analysis," Kim corrected. "I'm curious about markup strategies."
The meal began with cocktails and appetizers. Jay sampled the wine selection with appreciation, thankful for Luc's earlier advice about following Ian's lead. The conversation around the table flowed with ease. 
"The scallops are amazing," Amy commented.
"Everything here is exceptional," Max replied. "Including the prices. Though I suppose successful ventures deserve appropriate celebration."
Turner Senior seemed interested in the team's work on the Hartwell contract. Ian guided the discussion with skill, highlighting each team member's contributions while deflecting Turner Senior's more probing questions.
"The documentation process was rigorous," Ian said, addressing Turner Senior's inquiry about quality control. "Jay coordinated the review process and ensured all materials met client specifications."
Jay felt a moment of panic as Turner Senior's attention moved to him, but Ian smoothly transitioned to discussing Amy's market analysis, drawing focus away before Jay needed to respond. The conversation moved past Jay, but he felt the warmth of being acknowledged. Ian had included him naturally, without putting him on the spot.
The pattern continued throughout the evening. When Turner Senior asked pointed questions about project timelines, Ian provided comprehensive answers. When the conversation veered toward office politics or personnel decisions, Ian redirected toward technical achievements. When Turner Senior began discussing regulatory changes, Ian guided the conversation to include everyone's perspective.
"The new compliance requirements will affect our entire client base," Turner Senior said, his tone growing more serious. "Particularly the smaller portfolios that lack dedicated legal resources."
"That's where our advisory services become crucial," Ian replied. "We can provide the expertise they need without the overhead of full-time staff."
"Assuming we can scale appropriately," Turner Senior said. "Increased demand means increased responsibility. Are we prepared for that level of growth?"
"We have the core expertise. The challenge is ensuring consistent quality as we expand our client base."
As the main course arrived, Jay realized he enjoyed the evening more than he'd expected. The food was exceptional—a perfectly prepared salmon with some sort of elaborate sauce. The wine was undeniably excellent. 
"So Jay," Luc said, turning to him, "what's your take on fine dining? Living up to the hype?"
"The food is amazing. I can see why places like this have waiting lists. Though I have to admit, I was expecting something more intimidating."
"Intimidating how?" Ela asked.
"Stuffy waiters and complicated etiquette. This feels almost... comfortable."
As the evening progressed, the conversation ranged from work achievements to harmless gossip about competing firms. Jay contributed occasional comments—nothing profound, but small observations that earned nods of agreement from his colleagues.
"The international markets have been particularly volatile," Amy was saying, addressing a question from one of the senior partners. "Our projections account for the uncertainty, and client confidence remains strong."
"That's partly due to the presentation quality," Leo added, raising his glass slightly. "This team knows how to make complex data accessible to decision-makers."
Jay sipped his wine and listened to the discussion. The atmosphere was more relaxed than he'd anticipated. Jay realized he hadn't felt excluded or out of place since arriving. Ian's management of the conversation had been masterful. The team dynamic worked even in this setting, with light jokes that spoke to real camaraderie.
"I have to say," Turner Senior announced as dessert arrived, "this acquisition represents strategic thinking that built this company. Well done, all of you."
The acknowledgement sparked a round of pleased murmurs and raised glasses. As the evening wound down and colleagues began gathering their coats, Jay marveled at how different the night had been from his expectations. The formal setting had been intimidating, but the people had remained friendly, and Ian's leadership had created a protective atmosphere that allowed everyone to have a good time.
"Good evening, everyone," Ian said as they prepared to leave. "Excellent work, as always."
Jay collected his coat and followed his colleagues toward the exit, feeling the satisfaction of a successful evening. The anxiety that had plagued him for days had proven largely unfounded.
Scene 4
Planning
Jay approached the office with a different perspective on Monday morning. The weekend had provided time to process Friday's dinner, and he'd returned to work with something he hadn't expected: confidence in his place at Turner & Associates.
The standard start of the week briefing convened at nine-thirty in the the local conference room. Jay took a seat and opened his notebook, but noticed he no longer felt the tension that had characterized these meetings for months.
"The Hartwell integration is proceeding on schedule," Ian began, consulting his notes. "Ela, how are the international market adjustments progressing?"
"Ahead of timeline," Ela replied. "Clients had been responsive to our recommendations."
Ian nodded and continued through the agenda. When he reached Jay's assignments, the interaction felt easier than it had in previous weeks.
"Jay, the documentation review you completed last week was well done. Continue with the same approach for the Hoist Materials portfolio. Questions?"
"No questions. I'll have the preliminary review completed by Wednesday afternoon."
"Good."
The meeting continued, but Jay noticed Ian's attention during the exchange. Not scrutiny, exactly, but a kind of evaluating awareness. The attention didn't feel intrusive or suspicious—more like a manager monitoring an employee's development.
After the meeting, Jay returned to his desk with a clearer understanding of something that had been developing over the past weeks. Ian's leadership style wasn't about friendship or personal warmth. It was about creating an environment where everyone could perform their best work. The correction Jay had received months ago—the one that had felt so devastating at the time—had simply been feedback delivered without unnecessary cushioning.
Jay had interpreted Ian's directness as personal rejection because he'd been hypersensitive to any sign of disapproval. In those first weeks, Ian’s distance had seemed deliberate and unyielding. Looking back now, Jay realized it was simply the natural lack of rapport between a manager and an unfamiliar employee. Ian treated everyone with the same standard—he didn’t coddle anyone, but he also didn’t single anyone out. It was foolish, Jay thought, to have read so much into it.
"You look thoughtful," Luc observed, appearing at Jay's desk with a cup of coffee. "Deep philosophical contemplation or just Monday morning confusion?"
"A bit of both, probably. I was just thinking about management styles."
"Ian's approach, you mean? He's definitely not the warm and fuzzy type, but he's probably the most competent manager I've ever worked for."
"That's exactly what I was thinking. He doesn't waste time on unnecessary social niceties, but he's completely reliable."
"And he protects his team. Did you notice how he handled Turner Senior's questions on Friday? Every time the old man started probing too deeply, Ian redirected the conversation."
Jay had noticed, though he hadn't fully appreciated the skill involved. "He made it look effortless."
"That's what good management looks like. You don't always see it happening, but you benefit from it constantly."
The conversation was interrupted by Kim's arrival, holding a stack of documents and wearing an expression of mild exasperation.
"Have you received the holiday party emails? Christmas party on December fifteenth, end-of-year gala on January twenty-third. My social calendar is now completely full."
"I got them this morning," Amy said, joining the impromptu gathering. "The gala is always elaborate. Last year's theme was 'Winter Elegance' and the decorations were stunning."
"What's the difference between the Christmas party and the gala?" Jay asked.
"The Christmas party is for staff," Ela explained, appearing with her own coffee. "More casual, though still formal dress. The gala includes major clients and industry contacts. It's the most important networking event of the year."
"And the food is amazing," Max added, joining the conversation circle that had formed around Jay's desk. "The gala budget is apparently out of this world."
"Are you planning to attend both events?" Amy asked Jay directly.
"I hadn't really considered not attending," Jay replied. "Though I suppose I should start thinking about what to wear to a gala."
"Last time was just formal—this one is strictly black tie," Luc said. "And if you survived The Palladium, you can handle anything the gala throws at you."
The group continued discussing holiday plans, and Jay contributed to the conversation without the self-monitoring that had characterized the previous weeks. When Kim made a sarcastic comment about mandatory fun, Jay laughed. When Ela asked about his Christmas plans, he admitted he hadn't decided yet.
"I'm considering whether to visit my family," Jay said. "Last year was somewhat difficult, and I'm not sure I want to repeat the experience."
"Family gatherings can be complicated," Amy said with understanding. "Sometimes the obligations don't match the reality."
"Exactly. I'm torn between doing what I'm supposed to do and doing what might actually be good for me."
"What would be good for you?" Luc asked.
"Honestly? I'm not sure. Part of me thinks spending Christmas alone would be depressing, but another part thinks it might be peaceful."
"There's no shame in choosing peace," Max said. "Family dynamics don't improve just because it's Christmas."
The conversation continued for another few minutes before everyone dispersed to their respective tasks. Jay returned to his work with the pleasant awareness that he'd participated in normal office small talk without self-consciousness.
As the day progressed, Jay thought about the holiday invitations. The Christmas party and gala didn't seemed intimidating. He understood the office dynamics well enough to handle future events with confidence. More importantly, he felt more like a legitimate member of the team, no longer being  an outsider struggling to fit in.
The question of Christmas remained unresolved, but even that decision felt less fraught than in previous years. He knew he now had a valid excuse to skip the trip if he really didn’t feel up to it, and that possibility gave him a measure of peace. The pressure to choose between family obligation and his own comfort had eased, replaced by the reassurance that he wasn’t trapped by expectation.
From his office, Ian observed the gathering around Jay's. The laughter indicated that the damage from the earlier misunderstandings had been largely repaired. Jay's integration into the team had been slower than Ian had hoped, but it appeared to be genuine now. The distance he'd maintained had been a necessary protection, but it had also caused unintended harm. Jay's gradual recovery suggested resilience, and Ian found comfort in that.
Scene 5
Exhaustion
The last two weeks of November blurred into one unending stretch of work. Ian arrived before most of the team and left after midnight more than once, spreadsheets glowing against the dark glass of his office windows. The Hartwell integration demanded more oversight than any project in recent memory, but Ian welcomed the pressure. It was a relief to drown out unwanted thoughts with impossible deadlines and numbers that didn’t care about human failings.
The acquisition had been Ian's initiative, a decision that surprised even Leo when Ian presented the proposal during their weekly leadership meeting. Most new client acquisitions came through established networks or referrals, but Ian had identified Hartwell as a strategic opportunity and pursued them directly.
"This is somewhat unusual for you," Leo had observed, reviewing the client profile. "You typically prefer managing existing relationships."
"The portfolio potential is significant," Ian had replied, which was true but incomplete. The complete truth was more complicated: Ian needed a distraction that would demand his full attention and eliminate the growing problem of his focus on Jay Hall.
The realization had crept up on him gradually. At first, Ian had attributed his heightened awareness to caution—the natural vigilance that came from having been deceived before. But as weeks passed and Jay was consistently reliable, Ian's attention had turned from suspicious monitoring to something more troubling. Jay's combination of competence and vulnerability, his earnest dedication to work, his obvious gratitude for basic courtesy—all of it appealed to Ian more than he wanted to acknowledge.
Leo had been right about Jay's innocence. The corporate spy theory had been paranoid projection, a way of avoiding the more uncomfortable truth that Ian simply found his assistant compelling. The solution was obvious: redirect his attention to something that would consume his focus entirely.
Hartwell provided exactly that opportunity. The acquisition required intensive analysis and comprehensive portfolio development that would demand sixteen-hour days and weekend work. Ian threw himself into the project, hoping that exhaustion would cure his inconvenient interest in Jay.
The strategy worked, mostly. Ian arrived at the office before seven each morning and rarely left before nine at night. He brought work home, spreading documents across his dining table and working until his eyes burned with fatigue. Weekends became extensions of the workweek, spent hunched over laptops and financial reports.
The pace was unsustainable, but it was also effective. Ian's thoughts, previously prone to wandering toward Jay during quiet moments, remained focused on portfolio optimization. When Jay appeared in his office with questions, Ian addressed the matter at hand without the unwelcome personal awareness.
For Jay, the change was subtle at first. He always arrived early, before the office lights fully brightened, but day after day, Ian was already there, reviewing documents as if he was chasing a clock only he could see.
Luc noticed as well. “First weeks with a new client always mean chaos,” he told Jay, passing by his desk one morning with a sympathetic shrug. “It’ll calm down. Ian just sets a pace no one else can keep up with.”
Jay nodded, but couldn’t shake a sense of unease as the days wore on. He watched Ian move through each day relentlessly, the briefest pauses for coffee or conversation even rarer than usual. Ian’s responses became almost mechanical, as if he’d excised all but the most essential parts of himself for the sake of the assignment.
The team adapted well to the increased workload, but Ian still monitored their stress levels, redistributing tasks when necessary and ensuring no one felt overwhelmed by the accelerated timeline. What he didn't monitor was his own condition, which deteriorated steadily as November drew to a close.
By the end of the month, Ian's reflection in the bathroom mirror showed the accumulated effects of weeks without adequate rest. He looked a little more disheveled than usual—his tie was slightly askew, his hair less tidy, and there were dark circles under his eyes.
Jay noticed the change during their Monday morning briefing. Ian's presentation remained sound and his assignment distribution remained fair, but his manner indicated he operated on insufficient sleep and excessive caffeine. The observation made Jay uncomfortable, because Ian appeared to be pushing himself beyond reasonable limits.
"The Hartwell integration timeline remains aggressive," Ian was saying, consulting notes that seemed more extensive than usual. "We need the final review ready by Friday afternoon."
Jay nodded, making notes about the deadlines for the specific projects. His colleagues asked additional questions, and Ian answered each inquiry in detail, but Jay noticed the slight hesitation before responses.
After the meeting, Jay returned to his desk with a growing sense of concern. Ian's dedication was admirable, but the pace seemed to exhaust him. Jay had no basis for commenting on his supervisor's work habits, but he could at least ensure that Ian's daily management burden remained as light as possible.
Over the following days, Jay anticipated administrative needs before they became problems. He prepared meeting materials in advance and handled correspondence without requiring Ian's input. When colleagues needed guidance on project coordination, Jay provided it when possible, reserving Ian's time only for decisions that required senior management attention.
The effort was largely invisible, designed to eliminate small irritations. Jay streamlined processes and ensured that Ian's schedule remained manageable despite the increased workload. It was a modest contribution, but one that might allow Ian to focus on strategic matters without distraction.
Ian noticed the improved efficiency without initially connecting it to Jay's deliberate efforts. Documents appeared properly organized and routine tasks seemed to require less attention than usual. It wasn't until Thursday afternoon, when Jay appeared with a comprehensive status report on the Hartwell documentation process, that Ian recognized the pattern.
"This is very detailed," Ian said, reviewing the report.
"I wanted to make sure you had everything you needed for the client review."
The statement was matter-of-fact, but Ian detected the underlying intention. Jay was attempting to lighten his workload. The approach was tactful, offering support without commenting on Ian's condition.
"Thank you. This will be helpful."
Jay nodded and returned to his desk, leaving Ian to consider the dedication with which his assistant had been managing the increased demands. The support was exactly what Ian needed—unobtrusive but effective, competent without being presumptuous.
Ian's attempt to distract himself from inappropriate interest in Jay had only resulted in even greater appreciation for him. 
Chapter 8: The festive season
Scene 1
Decorations
December came on with a biting cold, catching the city by surprise. After months of unseasonably warm weather, winter finally announced itself with sharp winds and frost that decorated office windows with delicate patterns. Along with the drop in temperature, a festive atmosphere filled the building, and by the second week of December, the lobby displayed an enormous Christmas tree that commanded attention from everyone entering the Northlight Financial Center.
At Turner & Associates, the crisis pace of the Hartwell integration had finally subsided. The initial chaos of bringing on a major new client turned manageable, with tasks distributed among the team. Ian's insane days had returned to more reasonable proportions, and the exhaustion that had characterized his appearance throughout November had lifted.
Jay noticed the change immediately. Ian's morning briefings proceeded without the underlying fatigue that had marked recent weeks. His clothes resumed their typical perfection, and the slight hesitation that had crept into his responses disappeared. The relief Jay felt at seeing Ian return to normal surprised him.
"The Christmas tree debate begins today," Luc announced, appearing at Jay's desk with theatrical solemnity. "Brace yourself for the annual display of office democracy at its most chaotic."
"Christmas tree debate?" Jay asked, looking up from his screen.
"Office tradition," Kim explained, joining the conversation. "Every year, administration sets up the lobby tree, and every year, we decorate our own tree in the office area. And every year, we spend an hour arguing about colour schemes before Ian intervenes with executive authority."
"It's actually quite entertaining," Ela added, pulling three large storage boxes from the supply closet. "Amy has strong opinions about aesthetic cohesion, Max believes in festive chaos, and everyone else just wants to hang ornaments without extended philosophical discussions."
Jay watched with curiosity as his colleagues gathered around the boxes, which overflowed with decorations accumulated over years of celebrations. The two metre pine tree stood in the corner, undecorated but filling the space with the scent of evergreen. Office Christmas trees had been nonexistent at his previous workplace, where holiday acknowledgement consisted of a single poinsettia in the reception area and a brief mention in the company newsletter.
"This year, I'm advocating for a sophisticated monochromatic approach," Amy announced, unpacking silver ornaments. "Silver and white, perhaps with touches of gold for warmth."
"Absolutely not," Max declared, pulling out a collection that appeared to represent every colour in the spectrum. "Christmas trees should be joyful. The more colours, the better."
"That's not joyful, that's overwhelming," Amy protested. "There's beauty in restraint."
"There's boredom in restraint," Max countered, holding up a particularly garish red and green ornament. "This is what Christmas should look like."
Luc turned to Jay with an expression of amused resignation. "This happens every year. They'll argue until Ian arrives and makes a decision that pleases no one and somehow works perfectly."
Jay watched the good-natured bickering with amusement. Amy arranged ornaments by colour and size, Max deliberately mixed patterns and textures.
“It’s not a hostage negotiation, Amy,” Kim said. “And Max, I’m not sure chaos counts as a design philosophy.”
"What's your preference?" Ela asked Jay. "Are you team sophisticated minimalism or team festive chaos?"
"I've never really had strong opinions about Christmas decorations. I enjoy whatever other people created."
"A diplomatic answer," Luc said approvingly. "Perfect for avoiding the crossfire."
The debate continued for another ten minutes, with Amy and Max presenting increasingly elaborate arguments for their respective approaches. Jay enjoyed the theatrical nature of their disagreement, recognizing it as a silly conflict that only emerged among people who liked each other.
"What seems to be the annual crisis?" Ian emerged from his office, surveying the scene with an expression of patient resignation.
"Colour scheme dispute," Amy and Max said simultaneously, then glared at each other.
Ian approached the boxes of decorations, examining the contents. "Crystal and teal," he said after a moment's consideration. "Purple accents, silver details. It's festive and sophisticated without being sterile."
Amy and Max exchanged looks of mutual dissatisfaction, but neither protested. The compromise had the advantage of disappointing both parties equally.
"Crystal and teal it is," Amy said.
"I suppose that's acceptable," Max agreed grudgingly.
The team, freed from decision-making paralysis, began enthusiastic decorating. Jay got paired with Ian, hanging strings of silver garland while his colleagues distributed ornaments. The work was surprisingly intimate, requiring tight cooperation.
"Hand me that section," Ian said, indicating a length of garland that had tangled around Jay's feet.
Jay passed the decoration, their fingers brushing briefly in the exchange. The contact was entirely accidental, but it left Jay with an unexpected awareness of Ian's proximity. They adjusted the garland until it draped properly across the tree's branches.
"This is nice," Jay said, stepping back to examine their work. "I'd forgotten how much I enjoy Christmas decorating."
"You don't celebrate at home?" Ian asked, fixing a silver ornament.
"I haven't had space for decorations. And since I live alone, it never seemed worth the effort."
Ian glanced at him with an expression Jay couldn't interpret. "Will you be traveling to see family this year?"
"I'm still deciding. Last year the atmosphere was pretty tense, and I'm not sure I want to repeat the experience."
They continued working, adding crystal baubles and straightening the lights. Around them, the office was filled with the cheerful noise of collaborative effort. Luc and Kim debated the placement of purple accents, Amy ensured the colour distribution remained balanced, and Max added silver details with slightly more enthusiasm than his earlier protests had suggested.
Jay got caught up in the holiday spirit. The bickering of his colleagues, the joy of creating something beautiful together—all of it felt like a Christmas tradition he'd always imagined.
"There," Ian said, adjusting the final section of garland. "That should be properly balanced."
Jay looked at the tree, now turned by their collective work into something truly festive. The crystal ornaments caught the office lights, the teal and purple accents provided just enough colour to avoid monotony, and the silver details added elegance.
"It looks perfect," Jay said, meaning it completely. "Much better than I expected from a compromise solution."
"Compromise solutions often work better than anyone anticipates," Ian replied.
As the decorating concluded, Jay felt content. The office was warm and welcoming and even the mundane work tasks seemed more pleasant in the festive atmosphere.
As Jay prepared to leave the office in the afternoon, he considered something he'd never contemplated before: buying Christmas decorations for his own apartment. With his first full paycheck as a permanent employee deposited in his account—even larger than his previous salary—he finally had  the financial security to indulge in unnecessary but pleasant expenses.
The idea of having his own small Christmas tree, decorated according to his own preferences, felt increasingly appealing, even if he didn't really have space for it. Jay was beginning to understand what it meant to create comfort instead of merely enduring circumstances.
Scene 2
Christmas preparations
On Friday, just after work, Jay stood before the display window of an upscale tailor, studying the elegant evening wear arranged behind the glass. The mannequins wore impeccable black-tie attire—tuxedos with silk lapels, white shirts and bow ties.
"Good afternoon," the salesman said as Jay entered the shop. "How can I help you today?"
"I need a tuxedo for a company function," Jay replied, still adjusting to the idea that he belonged in such an establishment. "Something appropriate for a formal gala."
The salesman appraised him with a practised eye. "Excellent. When is the event?"
"January twenty fourth."
"Perfect timing. We'll have everything ready well before then. A proper fitting will take about four weeks—we pride ourselves on meticulous tailoring."
The salesman led him through the process, taking measurements and discussing fabric options. The final price would have horrified him six months ago, but now he nodded and provided his credit card information without the knot of anxiety in his stomach.
"We'll call you when everything is ready," the salesman said, handing him a receipt. "You'll look magnificent."
The compliment left Jay uneasy. He gave awkward thanks and hurried out of the shop.
His next stop was the small garden centre. The scent of evergreens hit him immediately—fresh, clean, and somehow hopeful. He wandered through the rows of trees, most of them far too large for his modest apartment.
"Looking for something specific?" asked a woman in a canvas apron.
"Something small," Jay said. "I have limited space."
She led him to a section of tabletop trees, each one perfectly proportioned despite their diminutive size. "These are lovely for apartments. They smell just as good as the big ones."
Jay selected a tree that reached barely fifty centimetres tall with full and symmetrical branches. The woman helped him choose a simple stand and wrapped the tree in protective netting.
"You'll want to get it in water soon," she advised. "And don't put it near any heat sources."
Jay thanked her and hauled his purchase to the nearby department store, where he spent an hour selecting ornaments. He chose crystal balls, silver garland that reminded him of the office decorations, and a string of warm white lights. The selections was deliberate—each piece contributing to a specific vision he hadn't realized he'd been forming.
Back in his apartment, Jay cleared space on the wide windowsill—one of the few spots that could accommodate the tree. He pushed aside a stack of books, then set the tree in place. After filling the stand with water, he adjusted the tree until it stood straight, makinh the best use of the limited space.
The lights went on first, their warm glow transforming the modest space immediately. Jay wound them around the branches, ensuring even coverage. The crystal ornaments came next, each one catching the light in different ways. Finally, he draped the silver garland, stepping back periodically to assess the overall effect.
The finished tree was tiny but radiant. It filled the corner with gentle light and the fresh scent of pine, making the entire apartment feel different—more like home.
Jay sat on his couch and admired his work. The tree represented something he'd never allowed himself before, decorating simply because he wanted to, not because it served any practical purpose.
Jay finally picked up the phone to call his mother. He’d decided to stay home for Christmas this year, and the thought of breaking the news had been weighing on him for days. As he waited for her to answer, he steeled himself for the conversation he’d been dreading.
"Hi, Mom."
"Jay! How are you, honey? I was just thinking about Christmas plans."
"Actually, that's what I wanted to talk to you about. I don't think I can make it home this year."
"Oh," his mother said finally. "Is everything all right?"
"Everything's fine. Better than fine, actually. But this new job has me working on a major project, and I can't take the time off right now."
"I see." Her disappointment was evident despite her attempt to hide it. "Well, work is important. I understand."
"I'm sorry, Mom. I really am."
"Don't apologize for doing well, sweetheart. I'm proud of you. It's just... this is only the second time you've missed Christmas."
Jay remembered the first time—his final year of college, when everything had felt impossible and he'd been too ashamed to explain why he couldn't afford the trip home. This felt entirely different.
"I know. I'll make it up to you."
"You don't need to make anything up. I just miss you."
"I miss you too."
They talked for a few more minutes about safe topics—the weather, his half-siblings' school activities. When they hung up, Jay felt both guilt and relief at getting out of family obligations. But tonight, the guilt felt manageable. He wasn't avoiding Christmas because he was struggling. He was choosing to spend it differently because he finally could.
The next morning, Jay walked to the shopping district with a specific mission. He'd decided to send gifts to his family. The prospect felt strange but pleasant—another indulgence he could now afford.
For his mother, he chose a bottle of perfume—bold and elegant. For his stepfather, a good bottle of whiskey, the sort that wouldn’t gather dust on the shelf. The choices seemed obvious and safe.
He hesitated a little longer over gifts for his siblings. For Ema, fifteen and already passionate about makeup, he picked out a set of quality brushes and a neutral eyeshadow palette—classic, nothing too trendy, but miles above the drugstore sets she probably used. For Tim, the oldest at seventeen and rarely indoors, he decided on a military grade multitool, sturdy enough for camping trips and after-school projects. For Ari, twelve and still fascinated by building things, he picked a building block set—the kind that promised a dozen different vehicles in one box.
He knew the gifts were more expensive than strictly necessary, but he didn’t mind. It wasn’t just about showing he was doing well now—though that was part of it. He wanted their holidays to be a little brighter, even if they’d all grown apart. Affection didn’t require closeness, sometimes it was just the effort to give something nice.
The sales clerk wrapped the gifts in festive paper. Jay planned to arrange delivery later, ensuring the packages would arrive in time.
Walking home with bags full of gifts, loaded down like a donkey, Jay realized the persistent discomfort that had shadowed him for months—the anxiety about money, about his place in the world, about whether he deserved good things—had mostly disappeared.
He couldn't pinpoint exactly when it had happened. Maybe it was the successful completion of the client crisis, or simply the accumulation of small victories that had built his confidence. Maybe it was the approaching holiday season, which had always held a special magic for him despite his complicated relationship with his family. Or maybe, Jay thought as he climbed the stairs to his apartment, life was finally looking up.
He unlocked his door and stepped inside, greeted by the gentle glow of his Christmas tree. The sight filled him with actual happiness. He made coffee and sat by the window, watching the city move below while his tree cast dancing shadows on the walls. The evergreen scent had grown stronger overnight, filling the apartment with the promise of celebration.
Scene 3
Christmas party
In the week that followed, the office entered into the frantic pace of the season—deadlines looming, inboxes swelling, but a sense of focus taking hold. Holiday anticipation crept in at the margins, just enough to soften the usual year-end rush.
Finally, on a Friday evening, the pace broke for the annual Christmas party. The restaurant buzzed with conversation and the clink of glasses as Turner & Associates' staff mingled around elegantly arranged standing tables. Jay adjusted his suit jacket, the same one he'd worn to every formal occasion, and surveyed the space. Chandeliers cast a gentle glow on winter greenery, and tables throughout the room were set with trays of food and drinks, inviting everyone to serve themselves.
"Jay!" Amy called from across the room, waving him over to where she stood with Kim and Zoe. "Come try the salmon canapés—they're incredible."
Jay made his way through the crowd, grabbing a glass of eggnog on his way. The restaurant had been completely reserved for the company, creating an intimate atmosphere.
"This place is amazing," Zoe said, gesturing around the room. "Turner Senior really outdid himself this year."
"He does every year," Kim replied, sampling a pastry. "Remember last year's party at that rooftop venue? I thought Ela was going to freeze to death."
"I survived," Ela said, appearing beside them with a plate of hors d'oeuvres. "Though I learned my lesson about outdoor December parties."
They chatted about the food, the decorations, and the year's successes. Jay sipped his eggnog, feeling the warmth of the alcohol ease him further into the evening. The opulent surroundings didn’t faze him anymore. He was relaxed, part of the group, and the sense of being an outsider had faded.
"Attention, everyone!" Turner Senior's voice reached across the room as he took position near the restaurant's central fireplace. The crowd gradually quieted and turned toward him.
"I want to thank you all for another exceptional year. When I started this company thirty years ago, I dreamed of building something that would stand the test of time. You've all exceeded those dreams."
He spoke about specific achievements, mentioning several team members by name, and outlined the company's impressive year-end projections. Jay felt pride as Turner Senior praised the team's ability to handle complex client situations and maintain their reputation for excellence.
"Now, before we continue enjoying this excellent meal, I have something for each of you." Turner Senior gestured to a table laden with beautifully wrapped baskets. "A small token of appreciation for your hard work and dedication."
The gifts were extraordinary—wicker baskets lined with silk and filled with imported chocolates, premium wines, artisanal cheeses, and various confectionery. Tucked into each basket was an envelope containing spa vouchers.
"Two full days at Serenity Springs Resort," Amy read from her voucher. "This is incredible."
Jay opened his own envelope and stared at the elegant certificate. The spa package was designed for couples, with treatments and accommodations clearly intended for two people. He felt heat rise in his face as he realized he had no one to bring.
"Who are you taking?" Zoe asked, showing off her own voucher.
"I'm not sure yet," Jay replied, hoping his voice sounded casual.
"I'm dragging my husband," Amy said. "He'll complain about the whole thing, but he'll secretly love it."
The conversation moved on to holiday plans as everyone returned to their food and drinks. Jay stood at the table with Ian, Max, Luc, and Ela, listening as they discussed their upcoming time off.
"My wife's parents booked us a house in the mountains," Max said, cutting into a piece of beef tenderloin. "A week of skiing and hot chocolate. I can't wait."
"Sounds exhausting," Luc replied with a grin. "I'm planning to sleep until noon every day and binge-watch terrible movies."
"I'm visiting my sister," Ela added. "She just had a baby, so I'll be playing aunt for a week."
"What about you, Jay?" Max asked. "Any exciting plans?"
"I'm staying home, actually. I need some rest after the hectic time I've had getting used to the new job."
"That sounds perfect," Luc said. "Sometimes the best vacation is no vacation at all."
The conversation moved to work coverage during the holiday period. It became clear that roughly half the team would be taking time off, leaving a smaller group to handle any urgent matters.
"So it'll be just us," Ela said, looking around the table. "Ian, Jay, Luc, and me. I hope everyone else works hard to finish all the major tasks before they disappear."
"We always manage," Ian replied. "The holiday period is usually quieter anyway."
Jay was curious about Ian's own plans but didn't dare ask directly. Ian hadn't mentioned any travel or family gatherings, and Jay wondered if the topic was off-limits.
As the evening progressed, the atmosphere grew more relaxed. Groups formed and reformed as people moved between tables, sharing stories and laughing over the excellent food. Jay no longer felt like the nervous newcomer worried about saying the wrong thing.
The crowd began to thin as the evening wound down. Some employees had families to return to, others had made dinner plans elsewhere. Jay was preparing to leave when Leo appeared at his elbow.
"Jay, good to see you enjoying yourself. I trust you're prepared for the holiday work schedule?"
"Of course," Jay replied curtly.
"Excellent. You'll need to take good care of Ian during that time. He always works through the holidays—never really had any family plans, you know. Estranged from his relatives and all that. I'm glad you'll be there to keep him company."
Jay felt a flash of irritation at Leo's presumptuous tone. "I'm sure Ian can take care of himself. He managed perfectly well before I arrived."
Leo raised an eyebrow at Jay's sharp response. "Just looking out for a friend, Jay. No need to get defensive."
"I'm not defensive," Jay replied, though he could hear the edge in his own voice. "I'm just not particularly interested in your insights about office dynamics."
Leo smiled. "Fair enough. Have a good evening, Jay."
Jay still hadn't forgiven Leo, and the casual way Leo discussed Ian's personal life bothered him. But beneath his irritation, Jay felt concern for Ian.
The image of Ian working alone through the holidays, with no family to visit and no plans beyond the office, struck Jay as profoundly sad. He thought of his own small apartment with its tiny Christmas tree, and wondered what Ian's holidays looked like.
Jay collected his gift basket and said his goodbyes, thanking Turner Senior for the generous evening. As he walked to the metro, he thought not about his own holiday plans, but about Ian's solitary Christmas.
The spa voucher felt heavy in his pocket, a reminder of experiences designed for sharing that he would likely never use. But more than that, it reminded him that he wasn't the only one who would be spending the holidays alone.
Scene 4
Holidays
The week following the party brought a surge of pre-holiday urgency. Everyone rushed to complete projects and tie up loose ends before the office closed for Christmas. Jay stayed late most nights, helping process year-end reports and ensuring client communications were current. Work on the annual reports had started in mid-December and was expected to continue through the first half of January, with everyone putting in overtime in a tense atmosphere.
"This is insane," Luc muttered, sorting through a stack of documents. "Why does everyone suddenly remember they need things done right before Christmas?"
"Same reason people shop on Christmas Eve," Ela replied, not looking up from her computer. "Poor planning and magical thinking."
Jay had spent his lunch break the previous day at the post office, shipping packages to his family. He'd divided the contents of his gift basket—the spa voucher went to his mother and stepfather, the chocolates and artisanal cookies to his half-siblings, while he kept the wine and aged cheeses for himself. The gesture felt right, a way to share his good fortune without the complicated emotions of an actual visit.
By Wednesday afternoon, the office atmosphere turned celebratory. People packed up their desks and exchanged final Christmas wishes before heading home to their families.
"Amy, have a wonderful time with your parents," Ian said, approaching her desk. "Give them my regards."
Amy stood and surprised him with a quick hug. "Merry Christmas, Ian. Try to enjoy yourself."
Ian accepted the embrace with good grace, though Jay noticed the slight stiffness in his posture. He moved through the office systematically, offering handshakes and holiday wishes to each team member.
"Max, enjoy the skiing. Don't break anything important."
"Luc, get some rest. You've earned it."
"Ela, safe travels. I'm sure your sister will appreciate the help."
Jay watched with growing anticipation, his pulse quickening as Ian approached his desk. When Ian finally reached him, Jay got up and extended his hand.
"Merry Christmas, Jay. Thank you for your excellent work this year."
Their hands met in a firm handshake, and Jay felt an electric current run through the contact. Ian's grip was sure, his skin warm against Jay's palm. For a moment that stretched longer than it should have, neither man moved to break the connection.
"Thank you," Jay replied, his voice slightly breathless. "For everything. For giving me this opportunity."
Ian's eyes held his for a beat longer than necessary before he released Jay's hand. "You've more than earned your place here."
Jay felt heat rise in his face at the praise. "I'll see you soon," he said, gathering his things.
"Indeed. Enjoy your holiday, Jay."
The metro ride home felt dreamlike. Jay replayed the handshake over and over, the memory of Ian's touch lingering on his skin. He told himself he was reading too much into a simple gesture, but the flutter in his chest suggested otherwise.
Christmas was gray. Jay had slept late, luxuriating in the freedom from alarm clocks and schedules. He made coffee and sat by his small tree, watching the city wake up through his apartment window.
The cheese platter he assembled for lunch was a work of art—aged cheddar, creamy brie, and sharp blue cheese arranged on a wooden board with crackers and grapes. The wine he'd selected was rich and complex, far better than anything he'd ever purchased for himself.
"This is living," he said aloud, raising his glass to the empty apartment.
His phone rang as he was preparing dinner—a simple but elegant meal of herb-crusted salmon, roasted vegetables, and rice pilaf.
"Jay!" his mother's voice was bright with excitement. "I had to call and thank you for the gifts. You really shouldn't have."
"It's nothing, Mom. Just some things from work."
"The spa voucher is incredible. Your stepfather keeps complaining about it, but I caught him reading the brochure three times."
Jay laughed. "Tell him it's a company gift and I'm way too busy to use it myself. He'll have to suffer through a weekend of massages and gourmet meals."
"The kids love their presents too. Ema's been doing her eyes nonstop, and Ari has already assembled two of the vehicles."
They talked for another twenty minutes, his mother updating him on family matters. When they hung up, Jay felt a surge of affection free of the tension that plagued his relationship with his family lately.
The evening passed pleasantly. Jay opened another bottle of wine, savored his meal, and watched a Christmas movie on his laptop. The apartment felt cozy and warm, his small tree glowing in the corner.
He thought briefly about Ian, wondering how he was spending the evening, but pushed the thought away. Whatever Ian's holiday looked like, it wasn't Jay's concern.
Meanwhile, across the city, Ian sat in his pristine home office, reviewing reports that could easily have waited until January. His penthouse was elegant and spotless, unmarked by any hint of seasonal decoration. The only acknowledgement of the holiday was the expensive bottle of scotch he'd opened.
He worked  through financial projections and client assessments, using the task to quiet his thoughts. The office would be closed for two days, but Ian had never seen much point in artificial celebrations.
Christmas had lost its meaning for him years ago, when his family's rejection had stripped away the warmth and tradition he'd once associated with the season. Now it was simply another date on the calendar, notable only for its inconvenience to business operations.
He paused in his work to refill his glass, thinking briefly about Jay's enthusiastic "see you soon" from the day before. The young man's obvious excitement about returning to work was both touching and puzzling. Ian couldn't remember the last time he'd looked forward to anything with such anticipation.
The memory of their handshake surfaced—Jay's palm warm against his, the brief moment of connection that had lasted a heartbeat too long. Ian forced the thought away and returned to his reports.
He worked until well past midnight, as he always did on Christmas Eve. The silence of his penthouse felt safe, unmarked by the messy emotions that holidays seemed to bring others.
As he finally prepared for bed, Ian caught sight of his reflection in the bathroom mirror. The man looking back at him appeared tired, someone who had forgotten how to feel the simple joy that had once made Christmas magical.
He doubted he would ever feel that kind of happiness again, the uncomplicated pleasure of anticipation and celebration. Those feelings belonged to a different version of himself, one that had disappeared along with his family's acceptance and his capacity for trust.
But somewhere in the city, Jay Hall was falling asleep with a smile on his face, content and already looking forward to returning to work. The contrast between their experiences would have struck Ian as poignant, if he'd allowed himself to consider it.
Instead, he simply turned off the lights and prepared for another day of productive solitude.
Scene 5
Dream
Ian dreamed of warmth.
Jay's body pressed against his on the leather couch in his office, the younger man's gray eyes wide with wonder and an adoration that made Ian's chest tighten. Jay's hands trembled as they touched Ian's face, fingers mapping the angular planes of his features.
"I can't believe this is real," Jay whispered. "I can't believe you want me."
Ian cupped Jay's face, thumbs stroking across his pale cheeks. "Always," he murmured, drawing him closer. "From the moment you walked into my office."
Their lips met in a kiss that was tender and hesitant, Jay's mouth opening under his with a small sound of surprise. Ian kissed him slowly, savouring the sweetness, the way Jay melted against him with complete trust. Jay pulled Ian closer with desperate need.
"Please," Jay breathed against his lips. "I've wanted this for so long."
Ian's mouth moved to Jay's throat, pressing gentle kisses to the sensitive skin there. Jay's pulse raced under his lips, and Ian unbuttoned the top two buttons of Jay's shirt, revealing the pale column of his neck. Jay arched beneath him, trembling with want.
"Ian," Jay gasped, his voice breaking. "I'm so happy. I never thought... I never imagined you could feel this way about me."
Ian held him like something precious, something that might disappear if he gripped too tightly. Jay's skin was warm and smooth under his hands, and the sounds he made drove Ian to madness. This was everything he'd forbidden himself to want, everything he'd pushed away—
Ian's eyes snapped open.
His bedroom was dark and cold, the dream dissolving like smoke. His heart hammered against his ribs, and his breathing came in sharp, uneven gasps. The phantom sensation of Jay's mouth lingered on his lips, the memory of his weight in Ian's arms so vivid it felt like a physical loss.
Ian sat up, running his hands through his hair. The profound emptiness that followed the dream overwhelmed him. For a moment, he'd been happy—truly, completely happy—and now reality brutally crashed back.
He was alone. He was always alone.
Ian checked his phone. Three in the morning. The apartment felt suffocating, filled with the ghost of something he could never have.
He thought about how long it had been since he'd allowed anyone close. Years. He'd convinced himself that solitude was strength, that independence was safety. But apparently isolation was exacting its own price.
Jay's face rose in his mind—those wide gray eyes, the curve of his mouth. He was so beautiful that it made Ian's chest ache, sweet and honest and utterly untouchable.
Ian forced himself to stop. These thoughts were dangerous, useless. He couldn't start harbouring such fantasies about Jay. That path led only to pain—for both of them.
He grabbed his phone and scrolled to Leo's number. Leo had orchestrated this entire situation, bringing Jay into Ian's life with his meddling. Ian had tolerated it, but now he needed to express his displeasure properly.
The phone rang eleven times before Leo answered, his voice thick with sleep.
"Ian? Christ, what time is it? Is everything okay?"
"Tennis tomorrow. Nine o'clock."
"What? Ian, it's three in the morning. Are you drunk?"
"Nine o'clock," Ian repeated, his tone brooking no argument. "Don't be late."
"Are you out of your mind? It's the middle of the night, and it's December. The courts will be freezing."
"I don't care. I'll see you at nine."
Ian hung up before Leo could protest further.
The tennis courts were nearly empty on a gray December morning. Leo arrived looking disheveled and deeply annoyed, clutching a travel mug of coffee.
"This is insane," Leo muttered, stretching halfheartedly. "It's freezing out here. We're going to get frostbite."
Ian was already warming up. "You'll survive."
"What's gotten into you? You call me at three in the morning demanding tennis, and now you look like you want to murder someone."
"Serve," Ian commanded, taking his position.
Leo served weakly, still grumbling about the early hour. Ian returned the ball with viciousness, sending it flying past Leo's racket.
"Jesus, Ian! What was that?"
"Fifteen-love."
The next serve met the same fate. And the next. Within minutes, Leo was running frantically around the court, diving for shots that Ian accurately placed. Ian showed no mercy, driving Leo from corner to corner.
"This is torture," Leo gasped after losing the first set six games to love. "You're playing like you're trying to kill me."
"Second set," Ian replied, spinning his racket.
"Can we at least take a break? I'm dying here."
"We're playing."
Leo bent over his knees, breathing heavily. "This isn't tennis. This is some kind of sadistic punishment. What did I do?"
Ian's return shot nearly took Leo's head off. "You know what you did."
"I honestly don't! If this is about work, we can discuss it like civilized people over coffee."
Ian's next shot forced Leo to sprint to the baseline, where he stumbled and nearly fell. "This isn't about work."
"Then what?" Leo retrieved the ball and trudged back to the service line. "Did I forget your birthday? Insult your mother? Sleep with your imaginary boyfriend?" Leo paused, racket halfway through his serving motion. "Is it about Jay?"
Ian's look could have frozen water.
"He's doing great work, everyone likes him, he's fitting in perfectly. What's the problem?"
Ian's return shot landed inches from Leo's feet. "The problem is your meddling."
"My meddling?" Leo's voice rose to a squeak. "You mean hiring him? You approved that!"
"I approved hiring an assistant. I didn't approve hiring..." Ian paused, searching for words that wouldn't reveal too much. "Someone like him."
"Someone like what? Competent? Hardworking? Good-looking?"
Ian's racket connected with the ball so hard it sounded like a gunshot. Leo dove sideways, barely avoiding being hit.
"Are you trying to kill me?"
"Keep playing."
They continued for another hour, Leo growing increasingly bedraggled while Ian maintained his ruthless pace. By the time they finished, Leo looked like he'd been through a blender.
"Three hours," Leo gasped, collapsing onto a bench. "Three hours of this torture. I can't feel my legs."
Ian toweled off his face, his expression still severe. "Perhaps you'll think twice before your next scheme."
"What scheme? I hired you an assistant! A good assistant! He's perfect for the job!"
"That's the problem."
Leo stared at him for a long moment, understanding dawning in his eyes. "Oh. Oh, Ian. You like him. Actually like him. And it's freaking you out."
Ian's expression went glacial. "Don't be ridiculous."
"That's why you're punishing me with arctic tennis at the crack of dawn. You're attracted to your assistant and you're blaming me for it."
"I'm not attracted to anyone."
"Right. And that's why you just spent three hours trying to murder me with a tennis ball."
"This conversation is over."
"Ian, wait." Leo struggled to his feet. "Look, I know you're scared. After Sam, I understand why you don't trust—"
"Don't." Ian's voice was deadly quiet. "Don't mention Sam. And don't pretend you understand anything about my situation."
"I'm just saying, maybe it's time to consider that not everyone is out to hurt you."
Ian shouldered his bag and walked toward the exit. "Thank you for the tennis match, Leo. It was exactly what I needed."
"Ian!"
But Ian was already gone, leaving Leo standing alone on the frozen court, with bruised ego and aching muscles.
As Ian drove home, he felt marginally better. The physical exertion had burned off some of his restless energy, and Leo's suffering had provided a satisfying outlet for his frustration. But the underlying problem remained. Jay would be back at work tomorrow, with his earnest eyes and his gentle smile, and Ian would have to pretend that everything was exactly as it should be.
Chapter 9: New Year
Scene 1
After the holidays
The office felt different after the holidays, with most of the desks empty and the usual bustle absent. Jay arrived earlier than necessary, energized and eager.
"Good morning," Jay said as he passed Ian's office, surprised that his boss was already at his desk.
Ian looked up from his computer. "Good morning, Jay. I trust you had a pleasant holiday?"
"Very pleasant, thank you. Quiet and restful." Jay hesitated in the doorway. "How was your Christmas?"
"Productive." Ian's tone offered no invitation for further inquiry. "I've prepared a list of priority items for this week. With our reduced staff, we'll need to focus on essential tasks."
"Of course. I'm ready."
Jay sat at his desk with renewed purpose. The break had done him good. He felt clear-headed, approaching each task with focus.
Only a skeleton crew remained during the week between Christmas and New Year's, and even that had been reduced when Ela called in sick with the flu that morning, her voice hoarse and apologetic over the phone.  
"Don't worry about it," Ian had told her. "Rest and recover properly. We'll manage."
Luc arrived an hour later, looking considerably less energetic. "This place is like a ghost town."
"Ela called in sick this morning—caught the flu." Jay replied. 
"So it's just the three of us? This should be interesting."
The morning passed smoothly despite their reduced numbers. Jay worked through client correspondence while Ian handled the more complex enquiries. 
Luc emerged from the open space looking slightly overwhelmed. "Thank God you're both here. I've got three client reports that need to be finished by Friday, and Ela was supposed to handle most of them."
"I can take on the market analysis," Jay offered immediately. "I've been studying the proper formats."  
Ian watched Jay's eager expression and felt a tightness in his chest. The young man's recovery was remarkable—he'd regained that bright, determined look that had initially caught Ian's attention. But Ian's own reaction to that enthusiasm now felt dangerous.  
"That would be great," Ian said. "I'll review the parameters with you."  
Jay worked diligently, asking questions and tackling complex data into clear reports. Ian fought the urge to watch him too closely.
Every instinct screamed at Ian to maintain distance. The dream from the night before had left him wary, hyperaware of every casual interaction. But he also recognized that Jay's confidence remained fragile despite his improved mood. One careless gesture could undo months of progress.  So Ian forced himself to be patient and accommodating, answering Jay's questions thoroughly. The effort was exhausting—like walking a tightrope while pretending everything was normal.
By lunchtime, it became clear that the workload would be manageable but demanding. Jay volunteered to take on additional responsibilities, preparing reports that would typically be distributed among the full team.
By late afternoon, Luc had finished his assignments and was preparing to leave. "I'm heading out," he announced, gathering his things. "My girlfriend's cooking dinner, and I don't want to be late."
"Have a good evening," Ian replied, not looking up from his computer.
"Don't work too hard, you two. The world won't end if something waits until tomorrow."
As Luc's footsteps faded down the corridor, Jay glanced at the remaining work on his desk. His analysis was nearly complete, but Ian still had several client summaries to review.
"I can stay late and help with those," Jay said, gesturing toward Ian's stack of documents. "It's not like I have anywhere else to be."
"That's not necessary," Ian replied quickly. "I can manage it."
"Please, let me help. I want to contribute."  
Ian looked at Jay's earnest expression and felt his resolve waver. He could see the importance this held for Jay—the chance to prove himself useful, to be trusted with meaningful work. Refusing would likely hurt Jay's feelings.  
"All right," Ian said finally.
The next few hours passed quickly. They worked in silence, Jay at his desk while Ian remained in his office. The building grew quieter as everyone else left and the cleaning crew finished their rounds.
Jay had stayed late before, but almost always with other people present. The current situation felt different—since no one else was there, it felt more intimate. The warm glow of the Christmas lights in the corridor cast everything in a softer light.
Jay worked through a stack of client summaries, but he couldn't shake the awareness of Ian across the room. They were alone together like never before—no colleagues nearby, no interruptions from the office activity.
"Jay." Ian's voice made him look up. "Could you review these projections? I want to ensure the calculations are accurate before I submit them."
Jay accepted the documents, their fingers brushing briefly as the papers changed hands. The contact lasted only a moment, but Jay felt his skin burn at the touch.
"Of course," he said, hoping his voice sounded steadier than he felt.
As Jay worked through the projections, he was acutely aware of Ian returning to his desk, the sound of his chair, the rustle of papers being organized. The office felt simultaneously vast and cramped, as if they were occupying a private space carved out from the larger corporate world.
Jay discovered something unexpected as the evening progressed: he trusted Ian. Not just as a supervisor, but as a man. The realization struck him as he worked through the data—he felt entirely safe with Ian, protected by his integrity.
Under normal circumstances, being alone in an office with a senior manager would have made him anxious. His previous experience had taught him to be wary of men in positions of power, to expect the worst from such situations. But with Ian, that fear simply didn't exist. Jay couldn't explain the certainty he felt about Ian's character—they didn't know each other well, they had shared few personal conversations. Yet Jay trusted him implicitly.
The trust was absolute and unquestioning, a feeling Jay hadn't experienced with anyone in years. Ian could ask him to do anything, and Jay would comply without hesitation, confident that the request would be reasonable and appropriate.
This certainty should have been comforting, but instead it made Jay's pulse quicken. He trusted Ian not to take advantage, not to make him uncomfortable, not to use his position inappropriately. It was a level of faith Jay hadn't experienced since starting his career. But the intimacy of the moment also made his skin tingle. The quiet office, the warm lighting, the sense of being alone together—it created an atmosphere that felt charged, even though Jay knew nothing would happen.
Jay forced himself to focus on the documents, but his awareness of Ian remained constant. Through the windows, the city lights twinkled like earthbound stars, and Jay felt as though they were suspended in a moment outside of normal time.
He glanced up to find Ian watching him with an unreadable expression. For a heartbeat, neither man looked away, then Ian returned to his work, and Jay did the same. But the air in the office vibrated with tension that Jay couldn't identify.
Scene 2
Before New Year
As the week progressed, Jay threw himself into his work with determination, driven by a desire to make Ian's workload as manageable as possible. After nearly five months at Turner & Associates, he'd developed some expertise in the company's basic operations and client needs.
His evenings at home had been spent studying client portfolios and industry standards, and the investment was paying off. Jay could now manage simple reports and handle standard client correspondence with minimal supervision. He was reserving only the most crucial assignments for Ian.
"You're a godsend," Luc said on Wednesday morning, watching Jay organize the day's priorities. "Without you, I'd probably be sleeping in the office."
Ian observed Jay's initiative, not just in organization but in the matters that drove their business. His reports were clear and his instincts increasingly sound.
Jay stayed late every evening, working through tasks that would have otherwise fallen to Ian. One evening, as the office began to empty out, Ian paused by Jay’s desk.
“It’s the holiday season, Jay. You should head home and get some rest."
"When I get home, I study the work materials anyway," Jay said, not looking up from his computer. "It doesn't matter to me if I'm in the office a bit longer."
Ian shook his head in resignation. He recognized the pattern—the complete absorption in work that left no room for personal life. He didn't want Jay to become like him, a monk married to the job, but he was hardly in a position to criticize.
The lunch routine changed organically. With most of the team gone, Jay and Luc abandoned the group outings to restaurants. The weather was brutal, and neither felt motivated to venture outside for extended periods.
"I grabbed sandwiches from that bakery nearby," Jay announced, holding up a paper bag. "Turkey club and roast beef. Take your pick."
"Lifesaver," Luc replied, accepting the turkey club. "I was dreading another frozen walk to find food."
They ate in the office kitchen, a space Jay had rarely used for eating before. Ian appeared at his usual lunch hour, retrieving a container from the refrigerator and heating it in the microwave.
"Mind if I join you?" Ian asked, though he was already sitting down at the small table.
"Of course," Jay said, but he felt suddenly self-conscious about his lunch.
The conversation was stilted at first. Ian was private even during breaks, never discussing personal matters or revealing much about his life outside the office. But Jay enjoyed seeing this different side of his boss—less formal, more human.
"What does Ian eat?" Jay asked on Luc, realizing he'd never paid attention to his boss's meal habits.
"Ian has a personal meal service. Everything prepared according to optimal macros and delivered fresh."
Jay looked up, surprised. “Seriously? I thought he just skipped meals.”
Luc laughed. "Does he look like someone who skips meals? He's a gym rat. Working out is basically the only thing he does besides work. Very disciplined about it."
Jay took in the information with interest. He'd never thought about Ian's life outside the office, but now he watchem him more carefully. It was entirely possible that he was actually quite muscular beneath those tailored clothes. The broad shoulders, the way he held himself—it all supported Luc's observation.
Jay also identified the sleek containers in the office refrigerator. Each meal was portioned exactly, the contents clearly optimized for nutrition.
As New Year's Eve approached, the three of them worked closer than ever. Several last-minute client emergencies tested their coordination, but they handled each situation smoothly. Jay felt his confidence soaring with each successful resolution.
The work was meaningful, bringing him satisfaction unlike anything he'd experienced before. He began to understand why some people became workaholics—there was something deeply fulfilling about performing duties to the highest standard and seeing tangible results.
Jay looked toward Ian's office and felt a new understanding of his boss. Perhaps Ian felt this same fulfillment, this sense of purpose that made long hours feel worthwhile.
The last day before New Year was notably calmer. Client correspondence had slowed to a trickle, and their major assignments were complete. The entire financial district switched into celebratory mode.
Around four o'clock, Ian surprised both Jay and Luc by appearing at their desks.
"Would you join me in my office?"
They followed him, exchanging curious glances. Ian moved to the credenza where he kept a selection of glasses and a bottle of whiskey Jay had never noticed before.
"I thought we should toast the year," Ian said, pouring three generous measures. "It's been particularly successful."
Luc accepted his glass eagerly, but Jay hesitated. "I'm not sure this is entirely appropriate—"
Ian laughed, a sound that softened his entire demeanor. "We're done with our work, and we'll all be leaving momentarily. Even I won't stay late today. If it were truly improper, I wouldn't keep alcohol and glasses in my office in the first place."
Jay accepted his glass, still uncertain but charmed by Ian’s unexpected warmth. He had never seen Ian laugh this way—not the polite smile, but genuine joy. He had a feeling this moment would stay with him for a long time.
"To a successful year," Ian said, raising his glass.
"To surviving the holidays," Luc added with a grin.
They drank, and Luc immediately looked impressed. "This is excellent whiskey."
"It's actually my best bottle," Ian admitted.  
Luc went pale. "How best are we talking?"  
"Best enough that you should savor it, not gulp it."  
Jay sipped his whiskey and immediately made a face. "I never understood hard liquor."
Both men laughed at his expression, and Jay felt a warm thread of connection despite his distaste for the drink. He persevered, drinking stubbornly while continuing to grimace.
"It's an acquired taste," Ian said. "Wine is more forgiving for beginners."
"What do you usually drink?" Jay asked, emboldened by the alcohol and the relaxed atmosphere.
"Scotch, mostly. Sometimes bourbon. I prefer aged spirits—they have more complexity."
Ian explained the differences between various whiskeys, his passion for the subject evident in his detailed descriptions. Jay listened intently, fascinated by this glimpse into Ian's personal preferences.
The conversation continued for another twenty minutes, all three men feeling closer after the intense week they'd shared. Even Ian was more at ease, the initial tension from his dream having subsided. He'd regained confidence in his ability to compartmentalize his attraction.
Jay radiated happiness. The innocence in his expression touched something in Ian's chest, a warmth he didn't dare examine too closely.
When their drinks were finished, Ian got up, offering a brief farewell. "Happy New Year, Jay," Ian said, extending his hand. "Thank you for everything this week."
Jay shook his hand, looking a little embarrassed. “Happy New Year, Ian. I’m glad I could be useful.”
Jay left the office that evening feeling fulfilled and secure. Despite everything, this had been a good year. Challenging and not free from drama, but ultimately strengthening.
He felt truly blessed, and even his initial anger at Leo had faded. The man's meddling had actually resulted in something wonderful.
On impulse, Jay sent a message to Leo's company phone: "Happy New Year. You are forgiven."
Across town at an upscale party, Leo read the message and grinned. Combined with Ian's recent tennis-related tantrum, things were clearly progressing in the right direction.
Jay spent New Year's Eve alone in his apartment, watching fireworks bloom across the city skyline at midnight. He felt good—genuinely, deeply good.
In his own apartment, Ian stood at his window watching the same fireworks. He felt a similar contentment, a sense that the year had brought positive change.
Both men thought about each other as the new year began, and for once, these thoughts were warm and hopeful, not complicated by doubt or fear.
Scene 3
Bonus
The first week of January brought Turner & Associates back to life at full throttle, the year-start chaos intensified by the dual demands of annual and quarterly reports. Ela was still home recovering from the flu, Kim was on vacation for another week, and the smaller team meant no one had time to ease into the new year. Jay arrived earlier than usual on his first day back, only to find Ian already in his office with the door ajar, a half-empty coffee cup beside him and several files open on his desk.
"Good morning,” Jay said as he passed.  
Ian glanced up from a document. “Good morning. I trust you had a good holiday?”  
“I did, thank you. I hope yours was restful.”  
“It was,” Ian replied, before turning back to his work.
Jay's tasks felt comfortable now, second nature after months of practice. At eleven-thirty, his computer chimed with a new email. The subject line read: "Year-End Performance and Bonus Announcement." Jay opened it immediately.
"Team,
I want to congratulate everyone on an exceptional year. Turner & Associates has achieved record-breaking performance across all sectors, exceeding our targets by significant margins. This success is a result of your dedication, expertise, and collaborative efforts.
Year-end bonuses have been processed and will appear in your accounts shortly. These amounts reflect not only our financial success but also your individual contributions to our achievements.
I look forward to building on this momentum in the coming year.
Best regards, Ian Hagen Chief Financial Officer"
Jay read the message, then checked his phone for bank notifications. Nothing yet. He wondered what constituted a bonus at a firm like Turner & Associates. His previous job had given modest holiday bonuses—enough for a nice dinner or a small gift at best. Other firms had weeks of waiting, sometimes months, for accounting to finalize numbers and senior partners to approve distributions. It felt like that could only be explained by Ian’s standards, applied to himself before anyone else.
He tried to focus on his work, but kept checking his phone every few minutes. Around noon, the notification finally arrived.
Jay stared at the screen, dumbfounded. The number couldn't be right. Eighty thousand. For a second, he thought it had to be eight thousand, something generous but still plausible. He counted the digits again: one, two, three, four, five. Five digits. More money than he'd ever seen in his account at once.
He stood abruptly, then sat back down. This amount could change everything. In a couple of months, he could make a down payment on an apartment—not just any apartment, but a decent one in a good neighbourhood. The possibility felt surreal.
Jay walked quickly to Luc's desk, where his colleague was typing with unusual enthusiasm.
"Luc, could I ask you something?"
Luc looked up, grinning. "Let me guess. You got your bonus notification and think there's been a mistake."
"Well... yes. There has to be an error."
