"No error. Welcome to the real benefits of working in big finance. When the year is good, the bonus is mind-blowing. And this year was spectacular."
Jay glanced around the office. Max was on the phone, gesturing excitedly as he spoke. "I'm thinking the Maldives this time," he was saying. "Maybe that resort with the overwater bungalows." Zoe sat at her desk with a grin, apparently running numbers for apartment searches.
Amy walked past with a stack of files, looking pleased. "Congratulations, Jay," she said. "First bonus at Turner & Associates?"
"Yes, and apparently last, because I think I'm having a heart attack."
She laughed. "You'll get used to it. Though I hope you never take it for granted."
"I don't think that's possible," Jay said.
"So what's the plan? New apartment? Fancy car? Month-long vacation?" Luc asked.
"I honestly don't know. I never expected anything like this."
"Well, you earned it. Ian doesn't give bonuses out of charity."
Jay felt a warm flush of pride. "It still feels unreal."
"Enjoy it," Luc said. "You've been working hard. You deserve to celebrate."
Jay returned to his desk, still processing the morning's revelation. He opened his laptop and began researching apartment listings, then stopped himself. Moving felt premature. This windfall could disappear as quickly as it had appeared. Markets fluctuated, jobs ended, and he'd learned not to trust good fortune too completely.
But he could make some smaller improvements. His wardrobe, for instance. He'd been wearing the same rotation of modest clothes for months, carefully maintaining them to extend their life. His colleagues dressed impeccably, and while no one had ever commented on his appearance, he sometimes felt self-conscious about the gap in quality.
Jay made a decision. He would upgrade his work wardrobe—nothing extravagant, but a definite improvement. Three new suits, properly tailored, that would help him feel more confident.
After lunch, he researched tailors in the city centre. He found a well-reviewed boutique within walking distance that specialized in business attire. It wasn’t a place that built suits from scratch. Most of their pieces were ready-made, adjusted to fit each client, but that suited him just fine. It was a world apart from the department-store racks he’d relied on so far. 
The fittings could be done in a single visit, with alterations ready in about a week. The prices were reasonable, especially considering his unexpected financial cushion. He decided not to stay late that day and to visit it immediately, suspecting the workload in the coming days would be even more unforgiving, and doubting he would have the time.
The boutique occupied a narrow storefront. Inside, the atmosphere was quiet, with racks of suits in rich fabrics and a small seating area near the fitting rooms.
A middle-aged man approached as Jay entered. "Good afternoon. How can I help you?"
"I'm looking for three business suits," Jay said. "Nothing too flashy, but well-made."
The man nodded approvingly. "Excellent choice. Quality pieces that will serve you well. Let me show you some options."
For the next hour, Jay tried on suits in navy, gray, and a subtle pinstripe. The tailor took measurements, discussing fabric choices and fit preferences. Jay selected conservative styles that would complement his existing wardrobe while elevating his overall appearance.
"These will be ready in ten days," the tailor said as Jay completed his payment. "You'll find the improvement in confidence is worth the investment."
Walking to the metro, he felt good. Finally he wouldn't worry about standing out for the wrong reasons. The suits wouldn't make him equal to his colleagues' backgrounds or education, but they would eliminate one source of self-consciousness. The new year had begun with unexpected generosity, and Jay was determined to make the most of it.
Scene 4
Friday evening reflections
The days that followed were relentless. New projects launched alongside the backlog from the break, and with both the quarterly and annual reports due, there was little room to breathe. Ian was at his desk before Jay arrived each morning and still there after he left in the evenings. Jay’s own workload felt heavier than December’s, each task flowing into the next without pause. Lunch hours vanished, everyone ate at their desks, picking at takeout while answering emails. The printers ran constantly, conference calls overlapped.
Eventually Ela returned from her sick leave with dramatic stories about her recovery and Kim came back from his extended vacation with a deep tan and a collection of choice observations about tropical tourist destinations. The office felt complete again.
Despite the workload, the end-of-year gala dominated conversations throughout the building. Jay overheard snippets of planning discussions everywhere—in the elevator, during coffee breaks, over lunch. The venue this year was apparently the Grand Hotel, with its famous ballroom. Amy mentioned shopping for a new dress, while Max debated between his black tuxedo and charcoal formal wear.
"Have you picked up your tuxedo yet?" Zoe asked Jay during a brief break between meetings.
"Not yet," Jay said. "I should probably collect it this weekend."
The prospect of attending such an elegant gathering still felt surreal, but his growing confidence made it seem less intimidating than it once would have.
By the end of the week, the quarterly reports were finished. The pace eased marginally, though the annual reports and early-year planning kept everyone busy. Still, Monday brought the return of the team’s lunch outings. The weather was bitter, but the change of scenery was welcome. The pasta place, the sushi bar, the bistro with its onion soup, and Jay’s favourite Willow & Sage were all back in rotation.
"The weather's brutal," Luc said during lunch, warming his hands around a cup of coffee. "I'm ready for spring already."
"At least it's not snowing," Amy replied. "Remember last January? We were practically trapped in the office for two weeks."
"That was actually productive," Max said. "Nothing like a blizzard to eliminate distractions."
Jay listened to the banter, appreciating how naturally he'd become part of these conversations. The awkwardness of his early weeks felt like a distant memory. 
Friday afternoon brought a sense of completion as the week's projects wrapped up. Jay finished organizing Ian's calendar for the following week and compiled the status reports that would be distributed Monday morning. The office gradually emptied as people prepared for the weekend.
"Hey Jay," Luc said, stopping by his desk as he gathered his coat. "Feel like grabbing drinks? There's a new place downtown that's supposed to have decent craft beer."
Jay looked up from his computer, considering the invitation. Luc had been asking regularly, and Jay had consistently declined with polite but vague excuses.
"Thanks, but I'll pass," Jay said. "I appreciate the invitation though."
"Come on, it's Friday. Live a little."
Jay paused, then decided to be honest. "Pubs overwhelm me, to be completely frank. I'd rather avoid them if possible."
Luc raised an eyebrow, then grinned. "Fair enough. Though you realize you'll still have to attend on special occasions, right? Work celebrations, that sort of thing."
"I suppose I'll manage when necessary," Jay said with a slight smile.
"We'll ease you into it gradually. Start with quiet wine bars, work our way up to actual chaos."
Jay laughed. "I'll keep that in mind."
After Luc left, Jay finished his remaining tasks and prepared to leave. The office felt peaceful in the early evening light, most desks empty except for the occasional colleague working late. Ian's office light was still on, but he decided not to disturb him.
Since the team had returned to full strength and the quarterly reports were done, Jay no longer needed to stay late every day. The consistent overtime of December had become unnecessary, leaving him with free afternoons. Strangely, he missed those quiet hours when the office emptied and he could focus completely on his work while Ian handled urgent matters in the adjacent office.
Jay collected his coat and headed home, stepping into the crisp January air. The city felt different in winter. People moved with purpose, bundled against the cold but undeterred by it.
Back at his apartment, Jay hung up his coat and surveyed the space. The small Christmas tree still stood in the corner, though he'd planned to pack it away. It felt reassuring, a reminder of how much had changed in the past months.
He made dinner and ate while reviewing the day's events. The routine of office life had become so natural that he sometimes forgot how foreign it had once seemed.
As evening deepened, Jay got restless. He cleaned up the dinner dishes, then finally dismantled the Christmas tree, packing away the ornaments for next year. The task felt both melancholy and hopeful, marking the end of one chapter and the beginning of another.
He sat by the window with a cup of tea, watching the city lights emerge in the darkness. Usually, he enjoyed these quiet moments in his apartment, the solitude providing a necessary counterbalance to the day's social interactions. Tonight, however, the silence felt different—not peaceful, but empty.
Jay wondered if Ian was still at the office. It was past eight o'clock, late even by Ian's standards, but not unprecedented. The man seemed to find comfort in work, using it as both challenge and refuge. Jay understood the appeal, having experienced it himself during the busiest weeks of December.
The thought of Ian working alone in the office stirred something in Jay's chest—not pity, but a kind of sympathetic understanding. They shared a preference for solitude, but perhaps that preference masked a long buried need for connection.
Jay considered returning to the office, ostensibly to collect something he'd forgotten, but dismissed the idea as transparent. Ian valued his privacy, and Jay respected that.
Instead, he thought about gym memberships. The idea had never appealed to him before—he'd always preferred leisurely activities like reading or walking. But knowing that Ian enjoyed physical exercise made the prospect more interesting. Not because he wanted to impress Ian, but because he was curious about experiences that brought Ian fulfilment.
Jay opened his laptop and researched fitness centres in his neighbourhood. The variety was overwhelming—yoga studios, boxing gyms, traditional fitness centres with pools and classes. He bookmarked several options, planning to visit them over the weekend.
The evening stretched ahead of him. Jay sat deeper into his chair, accepting the unusual restlessness as part of his continuing evolution. Six months ago, he would have treasured this solitude without question. Now, he wondered what else might be possible, what other activities he might enjoy if he remained open to them.
Scene 5
Preparations
The week before the gala, Jay made his rounds to collect the various elements of his formal ensemble. The tailor greeted him politely, clearly pleased with the final result.
"Perfect fit," the man said as Jay examined himself in the fitting room mirror. "You have good proportions for formal wear."
Jay turned slowly, assessing the reflection. The tuxedo looked good on him. The black wool draped smoothly across his shoulders, the trousers fell in clean lines, and the overall effect was both elegant and understated. He looked like he just might belong at the Grand Hotel.
"The shoes as well," the tailor continued, presenting a pair of patent leather oxfords. "Premium leather, hand-stitched. They'll be comfortable for an entire evening."
Jay tried them on, walking across the fitting room floor. The shoes felt substantial but not heavy, with the quality that would last for years.It wasn't cheap, but looking at the complete ensemble, he felt the investment was justified.
"I'm satisfied," Jay said, changing back into his regular clothes. "Thank you for the excellent work."
"You'll want to practice that a few times before the event," the shop assistant advised, demonstrating the bow tie technique. "Most people underestimate how tricky it can be under pressure."
Walking back to his apartment, Jay realized he needed to address another detail: his hair. While he kept it short and neat, the gala demanded a more refined appearance. He didn't want to embarrass himself or, by extension, his colleagues with a casual look at such a prestigious event.
Finding a hairdresser was more challenging than expected. Jay called several salons near his apartment, only to discover they were completely booked. The third salon he contacted explained that January was peak season for formal events, with many people scheduling appointments after the holidays.
"I'm sorry, but we're fully booked through next Friday," the receptionist said. "You might try calling some of the higher-end establishments. They sometimes have last-minute availability."
Jay's anxiety grew as he worked through his list. He'd assumed a simple haircut would be easy to arrange, but his procrastination had created an unnecessary obstacle. Finally, a salon in the upscale shopping district had an opening. Jay winced at the price but accepted. The amount would have been unthinkable a month ago, but his bonus had changed his perspective on necessary expenses. Looking presentable at the gala was worth the money.
Thursday afternoon, Jay collected his new suits from the boutique. The tailor had outdone himself—each piece fit impeccably, turning Jay's appearance from merely acceptable to elegant. The gray suit matched out his eyes, the navy complemented his pale complexion, and the pinstripe added sophistication to his overall look.
"These will serve you well. Quality pieces that will last for years with proper care," the tailor said.
Jay thanked him and headed to the nearby department store to complete his wardrobe upgrade. He selected several shirts in fine cotton, with better cuts and details than his previous purchases. The prices matched the quality—each shirt cost more than he'd previously spent on an entire outfit—but the improvement was undeniable.
Returning home, Jay faced an unexpected problem. His small studio apartment had exactly one closet, and it was now overwhelmed by his expanded wardrobe. The new suits, shirts, and tuxedo required more space than he had available.
The old clothes would have to go. Jay sorted through his existing wardrobe, identifying pieces that were worn beyond repair or simply outclassed by his new acquisitions. Several shirts with frayed cuffs joined the donation pile, along with sweaters that had seen better days and casual wear that no longer fit his image.
The hardest decision involved his first suit—a basic gray piece he'd bought for job interviews years ago. The fabric was inferior to his new purchases, the cut unflattering in retrospect, but it held sentimental value. Jay held it up, remembering his nervousness during those early interviews, the hope and uncertainty that had accompanied each job application.
After a moment's hesitation, he folded it and placed it on the donation pile. His small space couldn't accommodate keeping clothes for purely sentimental reasons.
The reorganized closet looked dramatically different. Where once hung a collection of serviceable but uninspiring garments, now stood a selection of elegant pieces. It looked almost comical in his modest studio apartment—these expensive clothes seemed to belong in a larger space, with better lighting and more sophisticated furniture.
Jay purchased a set of cedar sachets to keep his clothes fresh, placing them strategically throughout the closet. The small apartment sometimes retained cooking odours or general staleness, and he refused to let his investment in quality clothing be compromised by poor storage conditions.
As he organized his new wardrobe, Jay thought about other aspects of his appearance. He had never worn cologne, partly from lack of interest but mostly because of cost. Now, with his improved financial situation, the option seemed worth considering.
He researched fragrances online, quickly becoming discouraged by the complexity of the topic. Base notes, top notes, seasonal appropriateness, projection, longevity—the vocabulary alone was intimidating. Jay imagined himself smelling like a department store fragrance counter. Better to smell neutral than to risk  olfactory offence, especially at his first major company event. The last thing Jay wanted was to arrive at the gala smelling like an overzealous teenager.
Jay stepped back and surveyed his apartment with fresh eyes. The studio was small—a single bed, a compact living area, a kitchenette with a small dining table that served his needs. The space was efficiently organized, everything in its proper place.
He had always found the apartment cosy. The size matched his lifestyle and budget. Now, with his closet full of expensive clothes and his bank account containing an unprecedented amount of money, he could afford something larger. The thought had crossed his mind several times over the past weeks.
But Jay dismissed the idea each time it arose. His current apartment felt secure and manageable. Moving would require faith in his continued employment and success—faith he wasn't quite ready to embrace. The studio represented stability and caution, qualities that had served him well throughout his life.
Besides, he enjoyed the space. The large windows provided good natural light and the neighbourhood was beautiful and safe. Jay sat on the sofa with a cup of tea, pleased with his preparations for the gala. The tuxedo was perfect and the hairdresser appointment was scheduled. He felt ready to represent Turner & Associates with dignity.
Chapter 10: The high finance
Scene 1
The Grand Gala
The night of the gala arrived with unseasonable warmth for January. Jay spent an hour preparing, adjusting his bow tie multiple times and checking his appearance from every angle. The salon had worked wonders with his hair, creating a polished style that enhanced his features without looking overdone.
Still, staring at his reflection, Jay felt ridiculous. The formal wear was perfect, his grooming impeccable, but he still couldn't shake the sensation of playing dress-up.
The taxi ride to the Grand Hotel gave him time to compose himself. Through the window, he watched the city—people heading to dinner, couples walking hand in hand. It felt comforting after the surreal experience of getting ready for such an elegant event.
The Grand Hotel's entrance was ablaze with lights, red carpet extending from the street to the lobby doors. Photographers flanked the walkway, capturing arrivals for society pages and business publications. Jay paid the driver and stepped onto the pavement, immediately feeling intimidated by the spectacle.
Inside, the ballroom was breathtaking. Crystal chandeliers cast warm light over hundreds of guests in formal attire. Women wore stunning gowns in rich colors. Men in perfectly tailored tuxedos stood in groups, discussing business.
Jay searched frantically for familiar faces. The sheer scale of the event was staggering—at least three times larger than the quarterly gathering he'd attended months earlier. This wasn't just a company party, it was a showcase of the industry's elite.
"Jay!" Luc's voice cut through the ambient noise. "Over here!"
Jay spotted his colleague near the bar, looking comfortable and relaxed in his formal wear. He made his way through the crowd, relieved to find an anchor point.
"You look fantastic," Luc said, adjusting his own bow tie. "The tuxedo suits you perfectly."
"Thank you," Jay said, tugging at his cuffs. "I feel like a penguin." 
"A very handsome penguin," Luc said with a grin.
"I feel completely out of place."
"Everyone does at these things. The trick is pretending otherwise until you believe it."
Jay tried to follow this advice, but the grandeur of the event made it difficult. Waiters circulated with champagne, while the conversations around him involved mergers, acquisitions, and nine-figure deals discussed as casually as weather reports.
Across the room, Ian stood with a group of distinguished-looking men, his posture perfect and expression attentive. Even from a distance, he commanded attention, radiating confidence that came from years of success in this world.
Their eyes met briefly, and Jay felt a flutter in his stomach. Ian was momentarily speechless. The young man looked stunning, the tuxedo's lines emphasizing his slender frame. There was something endearing about Jay’s bearing, slightly uncertain but determined to rise to the occasion. The tailor had done exceptional work, and whoever had styled Jay's hair deserved recognition. Even Jay's obvious nervousness only added to his appeal, creating an almost irresistible combination of sophistication and vulnerability.
"Earth to Ian," Leo's voice cut through his thoughts. "You're staring."
Ian turned to find his friend watching him with undisguised amusement. 
"I wasn't staring," Ian said coolly. "I was observing the guests."
"Right. One guest in particular." Leo followed Ian's previous direction and spotted Jay. "Oh, he does look good. Almost too good for this crowd of sharks. Half the people here are already noticing him. He's attractive and clearly out of his element. Someone's going to make a move."
Ian felt something twist in his stomach. "That's not my concern."
"Isn't it?" Leo's smile widened. "You should probably go protect your poor assistant before someone sweeps him off his feet."
"Don't be ridiculous."
"I'm being practical. He's your responsibility tonight, isn't he? Part of your team?"
Ian glanced back toward Jay. The young man was handling himself well, but Ian could see the tension in his shoulders.
"He can take care of himself."
"Of course he can. But wouldn't it be terrible if some smooth-talking investment banker decided to show him around? You know how persuasive those types can be."
Ian's jaw tightened. "You're being absurd."
"Am I? Because I see at least three potential suitors eyeing him right now." Leo gestured discretely toward different groups. "The tall one by the bar has been watching him since he arrived. The silver-haired gentleman near the windows keeps glancing over. And don't get me started on that young partner from Greymount—he's practically circling like a predator."
Ian followed Leo's indications and felt his irritation spike. His friend wasn't wrong—Jay had attracted attention, and not all of it felt benign.
"Stop talking."
Leo's delighted laugh made several nearby guests turn their heads. "You're jealous. This is wonderful."
"I'm not jealous. I'm concerned about appropriate behaviour."
"Sure you are. Which is why you should go rescue the poor puppy before someone else does."
Ian shot Leo a withering look. "Don't call him that."
"Why not? He does have that adorable lost look. Very appealing to the protective instincts."
"You're insufferable."
"I'm helpful. Go claim your territory before it's too late."
Ian opened his mouth to deliver a cutting response, then stopped. Across the room, Jay was indeed being approached by the silver-haired man Leo had mentioned—a partner from a competing firm known for his aggressive recruitment tactics.
"Fine," Ian said abruptly. "I'll go ensure he's comfortable."
"Of course you will," Leo said, his expression triumphant. "How noble of you."
Ian straightened his shoulders and moved across the room, telling himself he was simply being a responsible supervisor. The fact that his pulse had quickened at the sight of Jay in formal wear was entirely irrelevant.
"Excuse me," Ian said, appearing beside Jay with grace. "I hope you're both enjoying the evening."
"Ian," Jay said, relief evident in his voice. "Yes, it's magnificent."
"Magnificent indeed," the man agreed.
"If you'll excuse us, I need to introduce my colleagues to some of our other clients."
Without waiting for a response, Ian guided Jay and Luc toward the buffet area where Max and Amy were already stationed, plates filled with elegant appetizers.
"Thank goodness," Jay murmured as they walked. "I had no idea what to say to him."
"You were doing fine," Ian said, though privately he appreciated the excuse to intervene. "Some guests can be... intense."
"That's one word for it," Luc added.
They reached the buffet where Max greeted them cheerfully. "There you are! I was beginning to think you'd abandoned us for more glamorous company."
"Never," Ian said, surprising himself with how much he meant it. "How are you finding the evening?"
"Excellent food, decent wine, and enough networking opportunities to last a lifetime," Amy replied. "Though I have to say, Jay, you look great."
Jay flushed at the compliment. "Thank you. I feel like I'm playing dress-up."
"We all are," Max said. "That's half the fun."
Ian remained with the group instead of mingling with the industry leaders as he typically would. Usually, these events were strategic opportunities for building relationships and identifying new business prospects. Tonight, however, he felt more interested in ensuring his team's comfort than advancing his own agenda.
"This is unprecedented," Leo said, appearing with a fresh drink and an expression of delighted surprise. "Ian Hagen, master networker, holding court with his own staff instead of wooing potential clients."
"The evening is young," Ian replied, though he made no move to leave the group.
"Indeed it is," Leo agreed, his smile widening. "Indeed it is."
Jay stood slightly behind Ian, unconsciously using him as a shield against the rest of the room. Ian's broad shoulders and confident bearing created a pocket of calm in the chaos, making the evening feel more manageable.
Scene 2
The incident
Ian was inevitably drawn into conversations with key clients and the strategic networking that made these events worthwhile. Jay watched him move through the crowd, commanding attention without seeming to seek it.
"Come on," Max said, appearing at Jay's elbow. "Let me introduce you to some people."
Before Jay could protest, he got swept into a group of distinguished guests. Max made introductions with enthusiasm, presenting Jay as "one of our most promising analysts" to a rotating cast of clients and business partners.
"Jay, meet Meg Randon from Pacific Investments," Max said, guiding him toward an elegant woman in a burgundy gown. "Meg, Jay has been instrumental in our recent acquisition."
The conversations moved from one introduction to the next. Jay tried to keep up, giving rehearsed responses about company performance. The guests seemed interested in his insights, which was both flattering and terrifying.
"Here, you need a drink," said a man, pressing a champagne flute into Jay's hand. "Can't discuss business without proper lubrication."
Jay accepted the glass, taking small sips as the conversations continued. Another guest immediately brought another one when he finished, then another handed him a different cocktail. The alcohol helped ease his nerves, making the intimidating crowd seem more manageable.
"You're much younger than I expected," commented a woman with pearls and a charming smile. "Ian certainly knows how to pick them."
Jay wasn't sure how to respond to that, so he simply smiled and took another sip of his drink. The room felt warmer now, the conversations louder and more animated, everything slightly out of focus.
"Such a refreshing change," another client whose name Jay had already forgotten, commented, watching Jay with uncomfortable intensity. "Most young people in this business are so aggressive, so hungry. You have a gentler disposition."
Jay managed a polite smile, though the man's tone made him uneasy.
"Absolutely charming," the same client continued, moving closer. "You know, you remind me of someone I used to know. Same delicate features, same innocent expression."
The man's hand landed on Jay's shoulder. Jay stepped back, but the circle of people around him made retreat impossible.
"Such pretty eyes," the client continued, his voice dropping to an intimate murmur. "I bet you get a lot of attention with looks like that."
Jay's chest tightened. The man's hand moved to his back, fingers trailing along his spine, making his skin crawl. Other members of the group continued talking, apparently oblivious to the inappropriate contact.
"You're very young, aren't you?" the client asked, his breath warm against Jay's ear. "Fresh out of university, I'd guess. Still learning the ropes."
Jay tried to respond, but his voice caught. The man's hand had moved to his waist, thumb stroking the fabric of his jacket. The touch felt predatory, reducing Jay to nothing more than an object for this powerful man's entertainment.
"I should—" Jay began, but the client interrupted.
"No need to be shy, beautiful. In this business, connections matter. A young man like you could benefit from the right kind of guidance."
The implications hung heavy in the air. Jay felt his composure cracking under the weight of humiliation and powerlessness. He was trapped again, prey for someone who saw his youth and perceived weakness as invitation. The alcohol that had earlier provided confidence now made him feel dizzy. 
"I should probably find my colleagues."
"Oh, don't rush off. We're just getting acquainted. You know, I have several opportunities coming up that might suit someone with your... particular talents. Perhaps we could discuss them privately sometime."
"I appreciate the offer, but I'm very happy in my current position."
"Are you? How refreshing. Such loyalty is rare in someone so young and attractive." The man's other hand touched Jay's arm, fingers trailing along the fabric. "Though I wonder if Ian fully appreciates what he has. Such a waste if he doesn't."
Jay could feel tears welling up. The man's hands, his suggestive tone, the way he looked at Jay like he was something to be consumed—it all felt horribly familiar.
"Excuse me," Jay whispered, pulling away abruptly. "I need to use the restroom."
He fled, weaving through the crowd toward the hotel's facilities. His hands shook as he pushed through the bathroom door, relieved to find it empty.
Jay stared at his reflection in the mirror, trying to compose himself. He was flushed, his breathing unsteady. He splashed cold water on his face, hoping to calm his racing pulse.
Shame washed over him—the same helpless feeling he'd experienced at his first job. All his progress, all his growing confidence, seemed to evaporate in the face of this casual violation.
Jay locked himself in a stall, pressing his back against the door while trying to control his breathing. "You're being ridiculous," he told himself. "Nothing actually happened." But the attempts at rationalization did nothing. It still felt like a violation, even if nothing explicitly inappropriate had occurred.
Jay stayed in the stall for several minutes, composing himself enough to face the world again. When he finally emerged, he nearly collided with Ian in the hallway.
"There you are. I've been looking for you."
Jay opened his mouth to speak, but Ian's expression stopped him. He looked furious, his jaw clenched and eyes blazing with barely controlled anger.
"I'm sorry," Jay said quickly, shrinking back. "I didn't mean to disappear. I just needed a moment."
Ian's expression immediately softened. "You have nothing to apologize for. Are you alright?"
"Yes, I'm fine. Just needed some air."
Ian looked at Jay's face—the red-rimmed eyes, the trembling hands. "No, you're not fine. What happened?"
"Nothing happened. Really, I'm just tired."
"Jay. You don't have to tell me anything, but please don't pretend you're fine when you're obviously not."
"I'm sorry. I'm probably overreacting. It's just that these events can be overwhelming."
"They can be uncomfortable, yes. But they're not supposed to be traumatizing." Ian's tone held an edge of steel. "Did someone upset you?"
"It was probably nothing. Just a misunderstanding."
Ian's expression darkened again, but he kept his voice level. "Would you like to leave? I can have my driver take you home."
"That's not necessary. I can get a taxi."
"I insist. And I'll come with you, if you don't mind. Someone here has thoroughly irritated me tonight, and I need a reason to leave before I do something unprofessional."
Jay looked surprised. "You want to leave too?"
"Desperately. I was one inappropriate comment away from punching someone in the face in front of half the financial district." Ian's slight smile took the edge off his words. "You'd actually be doing me a favor."
The admission made Jay feel less pathetic. "I find that hard to believe."
"You'd be surprised. I have a very short fuse when it comes to certain types of behaviour. Come on, let's go"
 "If you're sure it's not too much trouble."
"None at all. Let me call my driver."
Without drawing attention, Ian guided Jay through a staff door at the rear of the hotel, where a black car stood ready for them. Their discreet departure blended in with the flow of hotel staff coming and going.
Scene 3
Soothing the fears
The car's interior was luxurious but not ostentatious. Jay sank into the cushions, finally allowing himself to relax.
"You know," Ian said as they pulled into traffic, "I once attended a similar event where a client spent the entire evening trying to convince me to invest in his nephew's cryptocurrency venture. Apparently, it was going to revolutionize the pet grooming industry."
Jay looked at him in surprise. "Really?"
"Completely serious. He cornered me by the seafood station for forty minutes, explaining how blockchain technology would transform dog bathing. I finally escaped by claiming I was allergic to shellfish and needed immediate medical attention."
Despite everything, Jay smiled. "Did it work?"
"Beautifully. Though I had to avoid the seafood station for the rest of the evening, which was unfortunate because they had excellent crab cakes."
Ian continued with similar stories—absurd client requests, social faux pas at formal events, the occasional spectacular failure of decorum. His dry humour and timing gradually dissolved the tension in Jay's shoulders.
"Here we are," Ian said as the car stopped in front of Jay's building.
Jay looked at the modest apartment complex, suddenly acutely aware of how different it was from Ian's world. "Thank you for the ride. And for... everything."
"It was my pleasure. I hope you feel better tomorrow."
Jay hesitated at the car door. "Would you like to come up for tea? I know it's late, but..."
Ian paused, clearly weighing the invitation. "If you're sure it wouldn't be too much trouble."
"No trouble at all."
Jay led the way through the building's lobby to the stairwell, acutely conscious of the contrast between this and the elegant hotel they'd just left. "I'm sorry there's no elevator. It's four flights up."
"I don't mind stairs," Ian said, following without complaint.
Jay watched him climb with ease despite his formal attire. He was clearly in excellent physical condition, making the four flights seem effortless.
"This is it," Jay said, unlocking his apartment door. "It's not much, but it's home."
Ian entered the small but tidy space, taking in the simple furniture and careful organization. "It's very comfortable. You've made it welcoming."
Jay busied himself in the kitchen, glad to have something to do with his hands. "I hope you don't mind basic tea. I'm not much of a host."
"Basic tea sounds perfect."
Ian sat on the small couch, somehow making the apartment feel more sophisticated instead of highlighting its limitations. When Jay brought the tea, Ian accepted it with the same grace he'd shown at the elegant gala.
"How long have you lived here?" Ian asked, making conversation.
"About six years. I found it right after I moved to the city." Jay curled up in the armchair across from him. "It's not glamorous, but the rent is reasonable and the neighbourhood is safe."
"Comfort matters more than luxury in most cases. Was having a place to yourself important to you?"
"Yes. I know it's more expensive than having roommates, but I value the privacy. My previous financial situation didn't really allow for anything better than this studio."
"Privacy is worth the premium. Especially after long days at work."
"Exactly. I could probably afford something closer to the office now, maybe even a proper one-bedroom, but I'm used to this area. There are nice parks nearby, good cafes. It feels like home."
"That's more valuable than square footage. A lengthy commute can be draining, but if you're comfortable here..."
"I actually enjoy the metro ride. Gives me time to think, or read. Though I suppose you don't use public transport much."
"More than you might think. Sometimes it's faster than dealing with traffic. Speaking of adjustments, how did you find tonight's event? I know these corporate functions can be intimidating."
"It was... intense. My previous jobs never had anything like that, so I'm still learning how to handle them. The networking aspect especially."
"You handled yourself very well. Better than most people at their first major gala."
"Really?" Jay looked surprised. "I felt completely out of my depth. All those important people, discussing deals worth millions..."
"That's perfectly normal. The key is listening more than speaking, which you did naturally. Most people try too hard to impress and end up saying something they regret."
"I was terrified of saying the wrong thing to the wrong person."
"A healthy fear. But you were polite and interested in what others had to say. That's rarer than you might think in our industry."
Jay felt a warmth spread through his chest at the praise. "Thank you. That means a lot coming from you."
"How did you find the scale of it? I imagine it was quite different from smaller company events."
"Completely different. The quarterly meeting felt manageable, but tonight..." Jay shook his head. "The ballroom was enormous, and everyone seemed to know exactly who they should be talking to and why."
"That comes with experience. And frankly, half of those people are just as uncertain as you were—they're simply better at hiding it."
Jay laughed softly. "That's oddly comforting."
"The real secret is that most networking is just extended small talk with business cards. You're naturally good at putting people at ease, which is more valuable than knowing every industry insider."
"I hadn't thought of it that way."
Ian drank his tea, letting the quiet stretch out before setting the cup down and looking at Jay. "Are you feeling better about the evening? I know it ended on a difficult note."
Jay's expression sobered slightly. "Much better, thank you. I was worried I'd embarrassed myself, or worse, reflected poorly on the company."
"Not at all. You represented Turner & Associates admirably. And if anyone made you uncomfortable, that's entirely their failing, not yours."
"I appreciate you saying that."
"Of course." Ian said, then added noticing Jay's fatigue. "I should probably head home. Thank you for the tea, and for giving me an excuse to leave early."
"Thank you for making sure I got home safely. And for the stories. They helped more than you know."
"Jay, if anyone makes you uncomfortable at future events, please don't hesitate to find me immediately. These functions can be challenging, but you shouldn't have to endure harassment."
Jay nodded, touched by the concern. "I will. Thank you."
After Ian left, Jay sat in his apartment, processing the evening's events. The incident still bothered him, but Ian's kindness had turned what could have been a traumatic memory into something more manageable. He'd seen a different side of Ian tonight—warmer, even playful in his storytelling. Jay felt like he might have found not just a mentor, but someone who cared about his well-being.
Meanwhile, Ian sat in his car outside Jay's building, wrestling with his own thoughts. He'd overstepped tonight, but seeing Jay distressed had overridden every other consideration. The fury he'd felt still simmered beneath his composed exterior.
He'd handled it appropriately, he told himself. He'd helped a colleague through a difficult situation. Nothing more. The fact that Jay looked beautiful in his formal wear, that his vulnerability had stirred protectiveness in Ian's chest, that sitting in his modest apartment had felt more comfortable than any elegant venue—none of that mattered. Ian finally instructed his driver to take him home, but the image of Jay's smile lingered long after the evening ended.
Scene 4
Aftermath
Jay arrived at the office early Monday morning, as had become his habit. It felt comforting after the weekend's emotional upheaval. He'd spent Sunday processing the gala's events, alternating between embarrassment about his reaction and gratitude for Ian's kindness.
Ian walked in at five minutes to eight.
"Good morning, Ian. Your nine o'clock meeting has been moved to nine-thirty, and the reports are ready for your review."
"Thank you, Jay. I trust you had a pleasant weekend?"
"Yes, very restful. I hope yours was as well."
"It was. Thank you for asking."
The exchange was perfectly normal and unremarkable. Jay felt both relief and an odd sense of disappointment. He'd wondered if Saturday's events might change their dynamic somehow, but Ian's composed demeanour made it clear that nothing was different. He had simply been kind to a distressed colleague—nothing more, nothing less.
Jay was thankful for Ian's discretion. The last thing he wanted was awkwardness or pity affecting their working relationship. Still, part of him had hoped for some acknowledgement of the connection he'd felt during their conversation in his apartment.
As the morning progressed, the office filled with people. But instead of diving into work, everyone seemed focused on dissecting the gala's events. Jay heard fragments of conversations from every direction—praise for the venue, critiques of the food, speculation about various business deals discussed.
"Did you see Pat's dress?" Ela was saying to Amy as Jay passed their desks. "That shade of emerald was absolutely stunning on her."
"The food was incredible," Max added, joining their conversation. "Though I think I ate enough appetizers to feed a small country."
"The real mystery is why Ian left so early. He always stays until the bitter end at these things," Ela said, perching on the edge of Zoe's desk.
"Maybe he had another engagement," Zoe replied. The comment held no real conviction. Everyone knew how unlikely that was, but she wasn’t one to speculate.
"Or maybe he was just tired of schmoozing. Even Ian has his limits," Max added.
Jay concentrated on his screen, hoping his face wouldn't betray anything. Clearly, Ian had been discreet about their departure—no one seemed to have noticed Jay's early exit or connected it to Ian's.
Kim appeared at Jay's desk with a grin. "So, how did you survive your first major gala? You looked terrified when I saw you earlier in the evening."
"I drank too much," Jay said, sticking to the simple explanation he'd prepared. "Spent half the evening in the bathroom."
The admission prompted a round of laughter from nearby colleagues.
"Welcome to the club. We've all been there. Remember Max's last gala?" Luc said.
"Hey now. That champagne was stronger than advertised," Max protested, but he was smiling.
"You had to be practically carried out. Leo found you asleep in the coat check," Amy reminded him.
"At least I didn't spill red wine on the mayor's wife. That was Kim's contribution to last year's festivities," Ela added.
"It was an accident! Though I still think she deserved it after that comment about tech workers," Kim responded.
Jay listened to the stories with growing relief. His own embarrassment seemed minor compared to the spectacular mishaps being shared. Everyone had mortifying moments at these events—it was apparently part of the experience.
"At least these galas only happen once a year," Luc said, appearing at Jay's desk with a fresh cup of coffee. "Though I have to admit, the networking opportunities are valuable."
 "True," Jay agreed. "I met several interesting people before... well, before I overindulged."  
"The trick is pacing yourself. Start with wine, switch to water between drinks, avoid the stronger cocktails entirely. Though I suppose learning these lessons the hard way is part of the experience."  
Jay nodded, accepting the advice while privately knowing alcohol hadn't been the real problem. The memory of the incident still made his stomach twist, but the normalcy of the office setting helped push those thoughts into the background.
"The important thing is that we all survived to tell the tale. And the gossip from these things keeps us entertained for months," Ela said sagely.
"Speaking of gossip," Kim said, lowering his voice conspiratorially, "did anyone else notice that Cruver spent the entire evening trying to corner people by the bar? He was like a predator stalking prey."
Jay’s hands stilled on his keyboard. Cruver. He hadn’t been able to recall the name before, but now that it was mentioned, he was almost certain that was the man.
"Cruver's always been handsy. Someone should report him eventually," Amy said with distaste.
"To whom? His own firm? They'd just cover it up," Luc asked.
"Maybe, but his behavior is getting worse. Someone's going to call him out publicly sooner or later."
The conversation drifted to other topics, but Jay thought about Cruver's aggressive behaviour. Had other people noticed? Had he been equally inappropriate with others?
Throughout the morning, Ian acted as if nothing had happened. The only difference Jay noticed was an occasional glance in his direction—subtle looks that lasted perhaps a second longer than normal.
Jay told himself he was imagining things. Ian was simply ensuring his assistant was managing his workload properly. Nothing more complicated than that.
By lunch, the gala's aftermath had already turned into office legend. Stories would be retold and embellished over the coming weeks, but the immediate excitement was fading. Jay was glad that his own experience had been absorbed into the general chaos without attracting attention.
By the end of the day, Jay thought about how normal everything felt. The weekend's emotional turmoil might have been a dream, except for the lingering warmth he felt when remembering Ian's kindness. Ian had seen him at his most vulnerable and responded with compassion.
Jay was content to let the incident fade into memory, another workplace challenge resolved. He had no way of knowing that Ian's thoughts were far from calm.
In his office, Ian made several discrete phone calls throughout the day. By evening, he'd confirmed Cruver's identity and begun researching his business relationships. Turner & Associates did business with his company, but that didn't make him untouchable.
He would do everything in his power to ensure Cruver paid for his behaviour. He had made a mistake targeting Jay, and Ian intended to make sure he regretted it.
The plan would take time to implement properly. Ian couldn't act impulsively without risking his own standing. But Cruver would learn that some actions had severe consequences, even if they initially seemed trivial.
As Jay prepared to leave for the day, Ian watched him organize his desk in the same way he always did. He seemed to have recovered from Saturday's distress, but Ian couldn't forget the terror in his eyes. The image had lodged deeply, surfacing whenever he tried to focus on work.
He knew he was overreacting. The situation was under control, Jay was safe now. His own response troubled him—the anger, the protectiveness. It wasn’t simple concern anymore. The thought of anyone taking advantage of Jay left his pulse spiking.
Jay deserved to work in an environment free from predatory behaviour. Ian would make sure that happened.
Scene 5
Choice
January turned into February with the relentless pace of new year business. The office was overwhelmed with the endless stream of reports that marked the beginning of another fiscal cycle. Jay was busy managing Ian's increasingly complex schedule while handling the administrative tasks that kept the department running smoothly.
HR flooded everyone's inboxes with reminders about upcoming deadlines. Performance reviews needed to be scheduled, annual leave requests submitted, and various compliance training modules completed. The gala felt like a distant memory, which suited Jay perfectly. He'd successfully avoided thinking about Cruver's inappropriate behavior, though Ian's protective response that evening remained a warm memory he revisited more often than he cared to admit.
"Jay," Ian said on one morning at the beginning of February, "could you schedule a performance review for us? It's been six months since you started."
Jay looked up from his computer, surprised by how quickly the time had passed. "Of course. When would be convenient?"
"Tomorrow afternoon, if possible. Two o'clock."
Jay added the appointment to Ian's calendar, feeling nervous. Performance reviews at his previous job had been perfunctory affairs, brief meetings where his supervisor gave generic feedback before moving on. He suspected Ian's approach would be more comprehensive.
The next afternoon, Jay knocked on Ian's office door at two o'clock, holding a notepad and wearing his most attentive expression.
"Please, sit," Ian said, gesturing to the chair across from his desk. "I want to discuss your progress over the past six months."
Jay sat on the chair, trying to read Ian's mood. He looked serious.
"First, I want to acknowledge that you've exceeded expectations in every area. Your organizational skills and ability to anticipate needs have been exemplary. You've made my job much easier."
Jay felt warmth spread through his chest. "Thank you. I've tried to be scrupulous."
"You've been more than that. You've been intuitive. That's a rare quality in this industry. Your client interaction skills have also improved dramatically."
"I appreciate the feedback."
"Which brings me to my main point. I believe you're ready for advancement. I'd like to offer you a position as a regular team member. It would mean a salary increase and expanded responsibilities."
Jay was stunned. "A promotion?"
"Yes. You'd be working directly on analysis projects and strategic planning. It's a natural progression for someone with your capabilities."
Jay's mind raced. A promotion meant recognition, advancement, everything he'd dreamed of when he first started. But it also meant leaving his current role, no longer working directly for Ian.
"I... I don't know what to say."
"You don't need to decide immediately. But I wanted you to know that the opportunity exists. Luc followed the same path. He started as my assistant and moved up to regular team member within six months."
"But Luc had much better credentials than I do."
"Credentials matter less than performance. And your performance has been exceptional. You seem hesitant. May I ask why?"
Jay struggled to find words that wouldn't reveal too much. "I'm not sure I'm ready for that level of responsibility. I enjoy my current work, and I'm still learning so much in this role."
His real reasons were more complicated. Jay wanted to continue working directly for Ian, wanted to remain the person Ian relied on for daily support. The thought of being reassigned to work with other team members, of losing the close collaboration they'd developed, filled him with unexpected dread.
Ian watched Jay's internal struggle. Privately, he was overjoyed by Jay's hesitation. Working with Jay had become one of the most enjoyable aspects of his life. The younger man's dedication made every day more pleasant.
But Ian's integrity overrode his personal preferences. "I understand your caution, but I'd encourage you to consider the opportunity. Career advancement is important, especially at your age."
Jay's discomfort was evident in his posture. "Could I think about it?"
"Of course. Take all the time you need. And Jay? This is just a proposal. If you decide you prefer your current role, I'll be more than happy to retain your services. You're invaluable in this position."
Jay felt relief wash over him. "Thank you. I really do appreciate the offer."
"Consider it carefully. But know that either choice will be respected."
When Jay returned to his desk, he found it difficult to concentrate. The promotion was objectively the right choice—better pay, clearer advancement opportunities. But something in his chest rebelled against the idea of leaving Ian's direct service.
"Hey," Luc said, appearing at his desk. "How did the review go?"
"Good. He offered me a promotion to regular team member."
Luc's eyebrows shot up. "That's fantastic! Congratulations."
"I haven't accepted yet."
"What? Why not?"
Jay fumbled for an explanation. "I'm not sure I'm ready. I like my current work."
"Jay, that's career advancement. You can't stay an assistant forever."
"I know, I just... I want to think about it."
By the end of the week, word had spread through the office. Max congratulated him over lunch, Amy shared advice about transitioning to analytical work.
"So when do you start in your new role?" Zoe asked during a coffee break.
"I haven't decided yet," Jay admitted.
The team exchanged glances. It was clear they found his hesitation puzzling, but no one pressed for details.
That evening, Jay sat in his apartment, weighing his options. The logical choice was obvious—accept the promotion, advance his career, prove himself capable of growth. But his heart pulled him in a different direction.
He thought about Ian, about the joy of being the person Ian turned to when everything else went wrong. The role gave him a sense of purpose beyond what career advancement could offer.
More than that, he'd observed Ian's isolation within the office. He shouldered tremendous responsibility, made difficult decisions, and bore the weight of the department's success with minimal support. Jay wanted to be that support, to continue making Ian's life easier. He didn't want to advance away from Ian, he wanted to advance alongside him. And if that meant remaining an assistant, so be it.
Monday morning, Jay knocked on Ian's office door. "I've made my decision about the promotion."
Ian looked up from his computer. "And?"
"I'd like to stay in my current role, if that's acceptable."
"May I ask why?"
Jay had prepared for this question. "I enjoy the work. I feel like I'm still learning, and I want to master this role completely before moving on to something else."
It wasn't the complete truth, but it wasn't a lie either. Ian watched him in silence for a long moment.
"I respect that decision. But I want you to know that the opportunity will remain open. If you change your mind in the future, we can revisit the conversation."
"Thank you. I appreciate that."
As Jay returned to his desk, he felt a sense of rightness about his choice. It might not be the most ambitious path, but it felt authentic to who he was and what he wanted.
Ian watched Jay leave, fighting to suppress his relief. Having Jay continue as his assistant was a selfish preference, but one that made his workdays immeasurably better.
For Jay, the decision marked another milestone in his journey at Turner & Associates. His skills had been acknowledged, he'd been offered advancement, and he'd made a choice based on his own values. It felt like growth, even if it meant staying in the same role.
Surface Tension
Chapter 11: February rush
Scene 1
Snow and scent
February began with a big drop in temperature and a massive snowfall that blanketed the city. Jay watched from his apartment window as the flakes continued to fall, thick and relentless, coating everything in pristine white. The beauty was undeniable, but he knew the commute would be brutal.
His prediction was correct. The metro crawled through the tunnels at half speed, announcements crackling over the intercom about weather-related delays. Jay fidgeted impatiently in his seat, checking his phone repeatedly. He normally arrived at the office by seven-thirty, giving himself a comfortable buffer before the workday began. Today he wouldn't make it until ten minutes before eight.
Still early by most standards, but later than he'd like. The deviation made him uncomfortable.
The Northlight Financial Center emerged from the snow like a gleaming beacon glowing against the gray morning sky. Jay hurried through the lobby, his old coat doing little to shield him from the cold. He was still shaking as he brushed the snow from his shoulders and approached the elevator bank. The doors to the middle elevator slid open just as he pressed the button, revealing Ian inside.
"Good morning," Ian said, glancing up from his phone.
Jay stepped inside and and waited for the doors to close, feeling self-conscious. "Good morning. The weather caused some delays on the metro."
"I imagine it did. The roads weren't much better."
The elevator began its ascent, and Jay was acutely aware of the confined space, smaller than he'd ever noticed before. Ian stood near the back corner, perfectly composed.
A subtle scent reached Jay, fresh and slightly spicy, rich without being overpowering. He'd never noticed Ian's cologne before, or perhaps he'd simply never paid attention. Now that he had, it seemed to fill the small space, making him hyperaware of Ian standing just a few centimeters away.
Jay looked at the floor numbers on the digital display, trying to ignore the way his pulse had quickened for no logical reason. This wasn't the first time they'd shared an elevator, though usually it was at the end of long days when they rode down together after finishing late work. But his  morning, the moment felt different.
When the doors opened at their floor, Jay waited for Ian to exit first, then followed him through the office doors.
"Have a good morning, Jay."
"You too."
Jay walked to his desk, hanging his coat in the closet. He powered up his computer and reviewed Ian's schedule. The morning felt off-kilter, though he couldn't pinpoint why.
When he went to make his coffee, he was so distracted he nearly selected the wrong program, only realizing at the last moment he was about to brew an espresso instead of morning latte. Had he always been so aware of Ian? The scent, the sound of his voice in the small elevator space?
The questions felt foolish, and Jay pushed them aside as the day's workload demanded his attention. February had brought an avalanche of new client proposals that needed immediate review. He threw himself into the tasks, letting them take over his thoughts.
The hours passed quickly, consumed by phone calls, document preparation, and the endless stream of emails. Since the weather was anything but inviting and the workload had reached its winter peak, the team abandoned their lunchtime outings. Instead, they ordered takeout and ate in the office kitchen. Jay didn’t join them, eating at his desk while composing messages between bites. He barely looked up from his screen all day.
By seven o'clock, most of the office had cleared out, but Ian remained at his desk, working through a complex analysis. Jay organized his own materials but made no move to leave.
"Jay," Ian said, appearing at his desk. "You don't need to stay late. I can handle the rest of this."
"I don't mind. There's still work to finish."
"I appreciate the dedication, but don't feel obligated to match my schedule."
"I want to help."
Ian returned to his office, and Jay continued working until Ian finally emerged at eight o'clock, briefcase in hand.
They rode the elevator down together. Jay noticed that scent again and had to resist the urge to breathe more deeply. The realization made him feel ridiculous—he was being weird about something as simple as cologne. He wondered what it was called, though asking would be presumptuous.
But it was pleasant. Comforting, echoing other moments when he had been close enough to Ian. When they had decorated the Christmas tree together, Ian reaching past him to hang ornaments. When Ian shook his hand, wishing him a happy New Year while they shared a glass of whiskey.
The elevator stopped at the ground floor, and Jay stepped out.
"Have a good evening, Ian."
"You too. Get some rest."
The elevator took Ian down to the underground garage where his car waited. Jay pushed through the lobby doors and stepped into the white world outside.
The snow had continued falling throughout the day, accumulating in thick drifts that softened every hard edge of the cityscape. The harsh sounds of traffic were muffled, creating an almost mystical tranquility. The streetlights created pools of golden illumination, making each flake visible as it drifted through the air. The city looked magical, like something from a postcard.
Jay remembered being seven years old, racing through snow while his mother called for him to come inside before he caught cold. Would he ever feel that pure joy again, that uncomplicated delight in winter weather? Or had the city permanently changed his relationship with snow, turning it into nothing more than an inconvenience that delayed trains and made commuting difficult?
The subway ran more smoothly on the return trip. When he reached his apartment, Jay still had energy to review materials for tomorrow's meetings. February was shaping up to be an intense month, with demands that would require his full attention.
He reheated his dinner and spread the papers across his small table, eating while reading through the reports. He worked for almost two hours. By the time he finished, his eyes ached from staring at numbers. As he got ready for bed, the memory of Ian’s cologne lingered faintly in his mind. All he could do was hope that tomorrow would at least bring a normal metro schedule.

Scene 2
New coat
Friday's temperatures seemed to bite through every layer of clothing. Jay pulled his old coat tighter as he hurried from the metro station, but the thin fabric did little to shield him from the arctic wind cutting between the buildings. The coat had served him sufficiently during previous winters, but those had been milder and with shorter commutes. 
The metro had been delayed again, adding another twenty minutes to his commute. Jay stamped his feet in the elevator, trying to restore circulation as he rode up to the thirty-fourth floor.
The office provided blessed relief from the cold, but the atmosphere was tense with deadline pressure. Reports needed completion and the workday felt compressed and frantic.
"Anyone interested in that new sushi place for lunch?" Kim asked during the morning break.
"Are you insane?" Luc replied without looking up from his computer. "It's freezing out there."
"Plus we don't have time," Amy added. "These projections won't finish themselves."
Max appeared with a stack of takeout menus. "Lotus Kitchen delivers quickly, and their portions are decent. Should we just order in again?"
The team agreed with weary resignation. The nearby Lotus Kitchen had become their default choice because they delivered within thirty minutes and the portions were large enough to sustain them through long afternoons.
Jay ate his wok noodles at his desk while reviewing a tricky spreadsheet. The office felt like a bunker where they had all taken shelter from the hostile world outside.
As afternoon faded into evening, it became clear that the traditional Friday drinks would be another casualty of the weather.
"Drinks are canceled," Ela announced to no one in particular. "I'm going straight home before my fingers freeze off."
"Same," Luc agreed. "Maybe next week if this arctic nightmare ends."
Jay stayed until eight-thirty, helping Ian finish a complex client request. When they finally called it a day, the building felt almost empty, their footsteps echoing in the marble lobby.
Outside, the cold was unrelenting. Jay hunched into his inadequate coat and hurried toward the metro, each breath forming clouds that dissipated instantly in the harsh wind.
The evening train was delayed, leaving Jay standing on the platform in temperatures that seemed to seep through his bones. By the time he reached his apartment, he was shivering uncontrollably, his old coat having been utterly useless against the brutal weather.
His apartment felt like a sanctuary. The heating worked reliably, and the insulation blocked out the worst of the wind that rattled the windows. But the cold had sunk deep into his bones, and even the warm air couldn't immediately dispel it.
He went straight to the kitchen and put milk on the stove, stirring in honey as it warmed. The sweet, creamy drink helped chase away some of the chill, and he cupped the mug in both hands, letting the warmth spread through his fingers.
Saturday morning brought no relief from the cold, but Jay had a mission. His old coat simply wouldn't survive another week of this weather, and he refused to spend the rest of winter shivering through his commutes.
He took the metro to the shopping district, targeting the more upscale department stores where quality might actually match price. The first few shops yielded nothing suitable—either too expensive even by his new standards, or lacking the warmth he desperately needed.
In the fourth store he visited, he found a wool overcoat that immediately caught his attention. The fabric was thick and well-constructed, with a weight that promised real warmth. The cut was classic, appropriate for his work environment.
The price tag made him pause. It felt extravagant, even with his improved salary. His frugal instincts, developed through years of budgeting, rebelled against the expense.
But as he tried it on, feeling how the sleeves fell at exactly the right length, he forced himself to remember that he could afford this now. His position at Turner & Associates paid well, and winter clothing was a legitimate necessity.
"It looks very nice," the sales associate said. "That's one of our best winter coats. The wool is treated for water resistance, and the lining is designed for temperatures well below freezing."
The matching scarf added another amount to the total, but it completed the ensemble and provided additional warmth for his neck and face. Jay paid with his card, trying not to wince at the cost.
Walking home in his new coat, he felt warm. The wind that had been cutting through his old jacket now barely registered. The investment was painful but necessary.
Sunday was gray and gloomy, making the apartment feel cramped and dreary. Outside his window, the wind howled between buildings, sending snow spiraling in patterns that might have been beautiful under different circumstances. But the heavy overcast sky drained color from everything, making even the fresh snow look gray and uninviting.
Jay spread his work materials across his dining table, determined to make progress on the projects that had been pushed aside during the week's chaos. The workload seemed heavier than ever, with everything demanding attention and detailed analysis.
He worked through the morning, breaking only for a quick breakfast. The early darkness outside created an oppressive atmosphere, making it feel like evening by mid-afternoon. The sound of wind and blowing snow provided a constant backdrop that made concentration difficult.
Lunch was a brief interruption—soup heated on the stove while he continued reviewing documents. The warmth helped counter the psychological chill of the gray day outside.
By evening, Jay had made progress, but his eyes were tired from reading financial reports under artificial light. The weekend had been productive but joyless, another casualty of the relentless weather. 
Winter was challenging . The cold was more severe, the commutes more difficult, and the workload more intense than anything he had previously experienced.
As he prepared for bed, Jay looked out his window at the snow-covered street below. The streetlights cast circles of light that illuminated the falling flakes, creating the same magical effect he had noticed earlier in the week. But tonight, the beauty felt alien and hostile.
Scene 3
Company card
February continued with workload that made even the most experienced team members question their career choices. Jay learned that this was the season when Turner & Associates truly earned its reputation—the first quarter demanded everything from everyone, and the office turned into a war room. Jay started arriving early each morning, just in case the metro was delayed again. Despite his long days, the work on his desk only seemed to multiply.
The reflection in the elevator's polished steel had shown him someone who belonged here. The tailored gray suit he wore today was one of three he'd purchased recently, paired with a white shirt that perfectly fit his frame. The wool overcoat completed the look.
"Welcome to the battlefield," Amy said one morning, dropping a stack of files on Jay's desk. "Hope you got enough sleep in January."
The office atmosphere tensed noticeably. The sharp but pleasant concentration disappeared, replaced by a nervous bustle, punctuated by the sound of printers working overtime.
He soon learned that this frenzy had a name—strategy season. The phrase was repeated often enough for him to understand it was important, though its exact meaning remained elusive. Apparently, each client required a final summary document by mid-March, along with a detailed, labyrinthine analysis that could make or break the year’s results. 
Jay nodded along whenever someone mentioned it, piecing together fragments from overheard conversations. He knew it mattered, even if he couldn’t quite explain how.
"Jay, do you have the analysis ready?" Ian called from his office.
"On your desk," Jay replied, not looking up from his screen. "I also pulled the comparative data."
Ian paused. "I didn't ask for comparative data."
"The numbers make more sense in context."
Ian smiled to himself. "Thank you."
Each day was busy, one spreadsheet after another. Jay stayed as late as Ian did, which meant most evenings stretched well past eight. Ian had stopped suggesting he leave earlier, a small victory that Jay treasured.
"Another late night?" Luc asked, passing Jay's desk with his coat already on.
"Seems that way."
"You know you don't have to prove anything anymore, right?"
"I'm not trying to prove anything. I just want to do the work well."
Luc watched him for a moment, then shrugged. "Fair enough. Don't burn out."
By Thursday of the second week of February, the strain was showing on everyone. Kim had snapped over a minor formatting issue. Even Max, typically unflappable, had muttered about early retirement while staring at his computer screen.
"Jay." Ian called from his office. "When you have a moment."
Jay gathered his tablet and the summary he was working on, stepping into the sanctuary of Ian's office.
"New client request came in," Ian said without looking up. "We'll need to restructure the Mayfield portfolio entirely. Can you block out the afternoon?"
"Of course." Jay made a note on his tablet. "Should I reschedule your three o'clock with the pension fund representatives?"
"Push it to tomorrow. This takes priority." Ian finally looked up, and Jay caught tension around his eyes. "It's going to be a long day."
"I'll let the team know we might need them to stay late again."
"They're going to mutiny. They’re running on fumes. Make sure they at least eat something."
Jay had been thinking the same thing. Walking past the desks earlier, he'd noticed the slumped shoulders, the way conversations had dwindled only to necessary exchanges. The pizza and noodle orders were becoming the new norm, but morale was clearly suffering.
"Actually," Jay said, "I had a thought about that."
"Oh?"
"People are tired of the takeout options. What if we ordered from somewhere special tonight? Something that feels less like survival food."
Ian leaned back in his chair, considering. "That's not a terrible idea. Do you have somewhere in mind?"
"There's a new place in the Harbour District. They do elevated comfort food. It's expensive, but it shows we value their time."
Ian reached into his desk drawer and pulled out a company credit card, sliding it across the mahogany surface. "Use this for tonight. And going forward, actually. For situations like this."
Jay stared at the card. "Are you sure?"
"You should handle this kind of thing. I should have given you this weeks ago, but I assumed you'd take the promotion and transfer to the regular team."
The card felt unexpectedly heavy in Jay's palm.
"Use your discretion. Within reason, of course, but don't worry about the expense if it helps productivity. I should have thought of this myself."
Jay pocketed the card. "I'll take care of it."
Jay spent the next twenty minutes browsing the menu, keeping in mind team's preferences he learned over the months of daily lunches.
The delivery arrived at seven, filling the kitchen with aromas that made everyone stop what they were doing. Jay had ordered generously: herb-crusted salmon, braised short ribs, roasted chicken with vegetables, and an array of sides that could satisfy various dietary preferences. The dessert selection included individual chocolate tarts and lemon possets that looked like they belonged in a magazine.
"This is incredible," Zoe said, loading her plate. "Where did you find this place?"
"I've been keeping a list of restaurants that deliver to the financial district," Jay admitted. "Research for situations like this."
Amy raised an eyebrow. "You research restaurants for work?"
"I research everything for work."
The kitchen filled with laughter. Even Ian appeared, surveying the spread with what might have been surprise.
"This looks excellent," he said, accepting a plate from Jay. "Thank you for organizing it."
Jay watched Ian choose from the options, selecting the salmon and steamed vegetables—a deviation from his strict meal planning, but not a dramatic one. The sight of Ian joining the team in the kitchen, standing among them while they ate and talked, created a warmth that had nothing to do with the hot food.
"We should do this more often," Kim said around a bite of short ribs. "Not the working late part, but the good food part."
"I'll make sure we're prepared for the next busy stretch," Jay said.
Work was temporarily forgotten in favor of comparing favorite restaurants and debating the merits of various cuisines. Jay was drawn into discussions about the city's food scene, contributing insights he'd gathered from his exploration of the neighborhood.
When they finally returned to their desks, the atmosphere lightened. The work remained demanding, but the sense of being in it together had strengthened. Jay made a mental note to research team management strategies during his weekends. He wanted to understand how to support Ian more effectively in situations like this. For now, he was content with the knowledge that he'd made a small difference in his team's difficult day.
As the office gradually emptied, Jay remained at his desk, finishing the client summary that would be needed first thing tomorrow. Ian worked in his office, the glow of his screen visible through the partially open door.
Scene 4
Planning
The pace of February left Jay with little mental capacity for anything beyond immediate work demands. Emails accumulated in his inbox faster than he could process them, and HR's stream of company announcements became background noise he barely registered.
When another message arrived with "Annual Company Retreat Update #3" in the subject line, Jay almost deleted it reflexively before remembering he should probably stay informed about major company events.
The email contained cheerful updates about the May retreat, complete with photos of the beachfront resort where they would spend three days. Pristine white sand stretched along turquoise water, while the hotel's amenities list read like something from a travel magazine—spa services, championship golf course, multiple restaurants, and evening entertainment that promised to be unforgettable.
Jay had submitted his application back in January when the first announcement arrived, filling out the basic form without giving it much thought. The retreat had seemed distant then, just another calendar entry to acknowledge and forget. But with HR's regular updates, the event kept surfacing in his consciousness like a recurring reminder of something he wasn't prepared to contemplate.
During the afternoon coffee break, he decided to ask his colleagues about their experiences with company retreats.
"I just read the retreat update. The place looks incredibly expensive."
"Oh, it is. Turner & Associates doesn't do anything halfway. You should see the budget they allocate for these things," Kim said.
Jay tried to imagine himself in such surroundings. "What exactly happens at these events?"
"Last year we went sailing. Instructors, fancy boats, the whole experience. The year before that was skiing in January. They actually flew us to a mountain lodge."
"And everyone goes?"
"Pretty much. It's not technically mandatory, but it's heavily encouraged. Plus we get Friday off, so it's basically a long weekend."
Amy joined the conversation. "The activities are actually fun. I know it sounds overwhelming, but they plan everything really well. Good food, interesting presentations, and enough free time to actually relax."
"What kind of activities are we talking about?"
"Beach volleyball, kayaking, wine tasting, cooking classes. They have a few options so you can pick what interests you. Trust me, after the winter we've had, three days of sunshine will feel like paradise."
Jay tried to picture himself participating in beach volleyball with senior executives and department heads. The image didn't come easily.
Luc wandered over, drawn by the conversation. "Are you worried about the retreat? It's not like the gala where you have to network and schmooze. It's much more casual."
"I'm just trying to wrap my head around the whole thing."
"The resort is ridiculous. But hey, if the company wants to spend money on pampering us, I'm not complaining."
"You'll love it," Ela assured him. "First company retreat is always special."
Jay managed a smile and returned to his desk, where another HR email waited with details about the April quarterly party. This announcement felt manageable—an evening event at a downtown venue, cocktails and dinner, nothing he hadn't handled before. After surviving the gala and various functions, a quarterly party seemed almost mundane.
"More corporate socializing," Ela commented, reading the same email. "At least this one won't have clients trying to corner us in hallways." 
The reference to Cruver made Jay momentarily uncomfortable, but the feeling passed quickly. The quarterly party would be mostly internal staff, which eliminated most of his anxiety about the event.
Another HR email arrived later that week, this one about vacation planning. Jay opened it while eating his desk lunch, more wok noodles that had lost any appeal weeks ago.
HR requested that everyone submit their preferred vacation dates by the end of March, but when Jay checked the team calendar, Ian's time off requests weren't listed yet.
As Ian's assistant, he wasn't sure whether he should coordinate his time off to coincide with Ian's or arrange it for when Ian wouldn't be in the office. The question felt important enough to warrant clarification, but not urgent enough to interrupt Ian's current crisis management. 
Jay decided to wait until Ian submitted his own plans, then match his timing accordingly. The decision could wait until their workload calmed down, whenever that might be. He marked the reminder for follow-up and returned to more pressing tasks.
