Jay had no specific summer plans anyway. His previous vacations had been spent in his apartment, reading books he'd been meaning to finish and catching up on television series. Occasionally he would visit a museum or take walks through different neighborhoods, but nothing that required advance planning or major expenses.
The money he didn’t spend on travel went directly into his emergency savings. Security mattered more than adventure, especially given his previous employment disasters. He liked knowing he had more than one safety valve in place—different accounts, different contingencies. One of them was even literal: a discreet envelope of cash tucked away where no one would think to look, his last line of defense if everything else failed.
But the company retreat was different. It was mandatory in all but name, a three-day commitment to being social and engaged with colleagues in an environment designed for relaxation but likely to be exhausting.
Jay tried to imagine himself at the beachfront resort, participating in group activities and evening entertainment, making conversation over elaborate dinners. The image felt foreign, like trying to picture himself in someone else's life.
The team was still at their desks on Friday evening, working through another late session fueled by takeout containers. The winter had fallen into a pattern of brutal cold and heavy workloads that seemed designed to test everyone's endurance.
"I'm starting to forget what sunlight looks like," Max complained, reviewing a complex investment analysis under the harsh fluorescent lights.
"May can't come fast enough," Amy agreed. "Three days at a beach resort is sounding better every day."
Jay continued organizing files while listening to his colleagues discuss the retreat with anticipation. Their enthusiasm was reassuring, even if he couldn't quite share it.
The retreat remained an abstract concept, something to worry about when the immediate pressures subsided. For now, February's demands consumed his thoughts, leaving little room for contemplating extravagant resorts.
Jay finished his tasks and prepared to face another frigid commute home, thankful for his new coat and the warm apartment that awaited him. Summer planning could wait until summer felt possible.
Scene 5
Evening work
The office had emptied gradually throughout the evening, colleagues departing as their individual workloads permitted, calling out farewells that echoed in the gradually emptying space. Kim left first, followed by Ela, Luc and Zoe, then Max with a joke about "bankers' hours", and last Amy with a cheerful wave. The sounds of departure marked the transition from daytime to the quieter  evening hours when real work could be accomplished.
Ian remained at his desk, reviewing materials that demanded his complete attention. Complex calculations required uninterrupted focus, something the bustling daytime office rarely provided. He preferred these evening sessions when he could think without constant interruptions.
Jay never announced his intention to stay late or asked whether his assistance was needed. He simply remained, transitioning seamlessly from daytime tasks to evening support.
The emails continued arriving even after business hours—urgent requests from clients, updates from overseas markets, messages that demanded immediate attention despite the late hour. Jay monitored Ian's inbox, flagging critical communications and sorting less urgent matters for morning review. His organizational system had evolved over the months, no longer requiring oversight.
When documents needed printing, Jay handled them without being asked. Contracts requiring signatures appeared on Ian's desk with relevant sections highlighted. The small post-it notes written in careful handwriting had become Jay's trademark, each containing exactly the information needed—reference numbers, modification details, deadline reminders.
"The Greenwell portfolio needs restructuring," Ian said, more to himself than to Jay.
"The last review flagged concerns about the tech sector allocation," Jay replied without looking up from his computer. "I pulled the performance data this morning. It's in the folder on your desk."
Ian retrieved the folder, finding exactly the information he needed. Jay had anticipated the requirement before Ian had fully articulated it, a development that had become increasingly common as the months passed.
"Thank you. Anything urgent in the evening emails?"
"Everest Capital wants to schedule a call for Monday morning. I've booked them at ten. Gelundis Fiduciary sent the documents you requested. I've printed them and placed them on your desk."
Jay's responses were always like this, succinct and comprehensive. Jay understood the flow of Ian's work, the priorities that mattered, the details that could wait. The learning curve had been steep initially, but now Jay functioned like an extension of Ian's own organizational system.
Ian noticed Jay's eyes drift to his empty coffee cup, saw the slight furrow of concern that appeared when Ian worked too long without a break. Jay never presumed to offer refreshments or suggest rest, but his worry was evident in his expression. But Ian made a point of refilling his own coffee. Jay was his assistant, not his servant, and Ian refused to blur that distinction regardless of Jay's obvious willingness to help with anything requested.
For years, working late had been a solitary grind, necessary but joyless, sustained by determination. The office became a tomb after hours. Now, Jay changed the atmosphere. He provided something Ian hadn't realized he was missing—silent, supportive companionship. He moved through the office quietly, never interrupting Ian's concentration but always being available when assistance was needed. He delivered documents during Ian's natural pause points and seemed to sense when Ian needed space to think. Jay never complained about the long hours, never seemed impatient to leave, never made Ian feel guilty for the demanding schedule. 
But the guilt remained. Jay's dedication seemed boundless, his willingness to stay overtime and handle demanding tasks both impressive and troubling. Ian wondered whether he was taking advantage of Jay's eagerness to please, whether the young man felt pressured to exceed normal expectations which wasn't fair or sustainable.
Yet Ian couldn't bring himself to send Jay home earlier. The Friday evening stretched, and Ian extended their work sessions simply to postpone the return to his empty penthouse.
"Ian. And Jay." Leo's voice interrupted Ian's thoughts as his friend appeared in the office doorway. His visits had become less frequent since the new year chaos began—perhaps his mysterious schedule had intensified as well.
Jay looked up from his computer, acknowledging Leo with a polite nod. Without a word, he rose from his desk and walked toward the kitchen area, clearly intending to give them privacy for their conversation.
Ian watched Jay's retreat with amusement. Over the months, Jay had developed an obvious wariness toward Leo that manifested in blatant avoidance. Jay remained courteous, but he made no effort to encourage casual interaction and seemed to take every opportunity to remove himself from Leo's vicinity.
"Your assistant is remarkably discreet," Leo observed, sitting on the chair across from Ian's desk.
"He is."
"And devoted."
Ian's expression hardened. "If you have something specific to discuss, please get to the point."
"You have to admit, the arrangement is working splendidly. You look more relaxed than I've seen you in years."
"The workload requires extended hours. Jay understands that."
"Does he? Or does he simply enjoy spending time with his boss? Either way, at least you're not working yourself to death in solitary misery anymore. When work becomes indistinguishable from leisure, one can hardly call it a hardship."
"That's enough," Ian said sharply. "You're reading entirely too much into it."
"I'm just pleased to see you looking less exhausted than you did six months ago."
Ian wanted to argue, to deny Leo's implications, but he was unable to formulate a convincing rebuttal. It was true that work had become more enjoyable since Jay's arrival. Even during those early months when suspicion coloured every interaction, the dynamic had felt invigorating.
His initial reservations about Jay now seemed not just unfounded but actively foolish. Jay's shyness, his obvious intimidation by the office's grandeur and his colleagues' credentials, his almost painful dedication to performing every task perfectly—all of it was disarming, making suspicion impossible to maintain.
Ian had watched obsessively during those first weeks, cataloguing every interaction and analysing every response. But Jay's awkwardness was too genuine to manufacture, his gratitude too heartfelt to be fake. The young man's reactions to praise, his visible relief when tasks were completed successfully—none of it could be performed that convincingly.
"He's competent," Ian said, unwilling to elaborate further.
"He's more than competent. He's exactly what you needed. Just remember to occasionally acknowledge that fact. Even perfect assistants require recognition."
After Leo left, Jay returned from the kitchen, sitting back at his desk without comment or question about the conversation he had deliberately avoided.
Ian continued working, acutely aware of the way Jay turned what should have been tedious late-evening drudgery into tender contentment. Jay was, by any reasonable measure, a gem—rare, valuable, and deserving of careful handling. The question was whether Ian was providing the appreciation he warranted, or whether he was simply taking advantage of Jay's generous nature.
Jay had become an integral part of Ian's daily life, which Ian was only starting to fully recognize. The realization was both comforting and concerning. Ian did his best to keep emotional distance, but Jay was undermining those defences without Ian quite understanding how or when it had happened.
Chapter 12: Stormy weather
Scene 1
Stormy morning
The storm hit the city with fury that made it feel personal. Freezing rain pelted the windows of Jay's apartment at five-thirty in the morning, and by six, the power had went out completely. He dressed by the light of his phone, checking transit updates that grew increasingly dire with each refresh.
"Metro suspended on all lines due to power outages. Estimated restoration time unknown."
Jay had left his apartment twenty minutes earlier than usual, anticipating delays, but he hadn't counted on the sheer violence of the weather. The streets were rivers of slush and ice, and the few buses running were packed beyond capacity. By the time he reached the financial district, the expensive wool coat was already damp and heavy, the supposed waterproofing no match for the winter storm. His shoes squelched with every step, and he was forty-five minutes late.
He'd sent Ian a text: "Power outage in my area. Metro suspended. Will be late but coming in as soon as possible."
The response had been immediate: "Don't worry about it. Be safe."
But Jay did worry about it. In almost seven months at Turner & Associates, he'd never been late. Not once. The sick feeling in his stomach had nothing to do with the cold.
Standing outside the Northlight Financial Center, drenched and shivering, Jay spotted a boutique across the street with its lights already on. The elegant script on the window read "Hathaway's Menswear" and under normal circumstances, he would have walked past without a second glance. The place sold sweaters for thousands to men who thought nothing of such expenses.
But these weren't normal circumstances.
The bell chimed softly as he entered, water dripping from his coat onto the pristine floor. A well-dressed clerk appeared immediately, taking in Jay's bedraggled state with composure.
"I need a complete change of clothes. Everything. I’m a size 46 jacket, medium shirt, 80 waist, and size 42 shoes.”
The clerk's expression didn't change. "Certainly, sir. Business attire?"
"Yes. And I'm in a hurry."
Within minutes, the man had assembled a selection: cream trousers that looked like they'd been cut from silk, a white shirt with subtle texture, a charcoal blazer that felt impossibly soft, leather shoes that gleamed despite the store's dim lighting, and undergarments still in their packaging.
"Will you be trying these on?"
Jay glanced at his phone. Nearly nine o'clock. "No time. I'll take them."
The total made his chest tighten. More than he'd spent on clothing in the past year combined. But he handed over his card anyway, too rushed and miserable to properly process the financial damage.
The Turner & Associates lobby felt like a sanctuary after the chaos outside. Jay's shoes squelched against the marble as he approached the elevators, acutely aware of the water trail he was leaving behind. The elevator ride to the thirty-fourth floor felt endless.
The office was half-full, with various team members shaking off coats and complaining about the commute. Ela looked up from her desk, her perfect hair slightly mussed.
"This weather is insane," she said. "I saw three accidents between my house and here."
Jay managed a weak smile and headed straight for Ian's office. The door was open, and Ian sat at his desk looking as immaculate as ever, not a hair out of place. Of course. He probably had a driver who delivered him directly to the underground garage.
"I'm sorry I'm late," Jay said from the doorway.
Ian looked up with barely disguised concern. "You look like you swam here."
"I might have. I need to go change my clothes."
"Go. There are towels in the executive bathroom." Jay caught the underlying worry in his voice. "Take your time."
The executive bathroom was a revelation Jay had never fully appreciated. Thick, hotel-quality towels hung from heated racks, and the space felt more like a spa than an office facility. Jay stripped out of his soaked clothing, toweling off his hair and trying to warm his chilled skin.
The new clothes fit perfectly, which was either remarkable luck or a testament to the clerk's expertise. The trousers moved with him like they'd been tailored, and the shirt felt like wearing a cloud. The shoes were comfortable despite being unworn, and everything coordinated with elegance that Jay had never achieved in his life.
He caught his reflection in the mirror and barely recognized himself. The clothes flattered him, highlighting his frame. The cut of the trousers made his legs look longer. He looked expensive. Polished.
It was too much. Too obvious. But his regular clothes hung dripping from the towel rack, and he had no other options.
When he returned to his desk, a steaming mug waited for him. Ian emerged from his office, holding his own cup.
"Ginger tea," Ian said. "From my personal supply. It's good for warming up."
"Thank you."
Ian's voice sounded slightly strained. "The storm is supposed to continue through the afternoon. If you need to leave early—"
"I'll be fine." Jay sipped the tea, surprised by its complexity. Not the simple ginger tea he'd expected, but something layered with honey and spices that warmed him from the inside out. "This is excellent."
"My housekeeper makes it." Ian seemed to be looking everywhere except directly at Jay. "She insists it prevents illness."
Jay sat at his desk, opening his laptop. Checking emails and reviewing Ian's schedule felt grounding after the morning's chaos. He was aware of how the new clothes moved with him, how the fabric felt against his skin, but he pushed the awareness aside. Work was work, regardless of what he wore.
From his office, Ian stole glances at his assistant, then forced himself to look away. Jay had always been attractive, but these clothes revealed what had been hidden beneath his wardrobe, and his still-damp hair gave him a tousled look that was unexpectedly appealing.
Ian gripped his coffee cup tighter and focused on his computer screen. This was exactly the reaction he'd trained himself to avoid. But Jay looked... God, he looked incredible. And completely unaware of it, which somehow made it worse.
Ian closed his eyes briefly, then opened them and forced himself to concentrate on work. The storm outside continued to rage, but the real tempest was happening inside his chest, where feelings he'd been suppressing threatened to overwhelm his defenses.
At his desk, Jay worked, occasionally glancing toward Ian's office when he thought no one was looking. The ginger tea warmed him and gradually, the morning's disasters faded into memory.
He had no idea that Ian was fighting the strongest urge he'd felt in years—the desire to cross the space between them, to touch Jay's hair, to discover if those new clothes felt as good as they looked.
Scene 2
Discomfort
Jay was three hours into his regular workday when Luc appeared at his desk with a peculiar expression on his face.
"Okay, I have to ask. When did you decide to switch careers to menswear modeling?"
Jay's fingers paused over his keyboard. "What?"
"This whole look." Luc gestured at Jay's outfit. "Very successful young executive with mysterious past."
"It's just clothes. Emergency replacements after this morning's disaster."
"Emergency replacements that probably cost more than my car."
"Close. About two paychecks worth. If it wasn't so embarrassing, I'd return them."
"Return them? Are you insane? You absolutely cannot return them. They're worth every penny. You look... You look so good I'm suddenly questioning my sexuality."
Jay managed a weak smile, but his chest tightened with discomfort. "That's a disturbing thought for both of us."
"I'm serious though. You should keep these for special occasions. Dates. You'd sweep anyone off their feet."
Jay's smile felt brittle. "Right. Special occasions."
He thought about his empty social calendar, his studio apartment where he spent most evenings alone. The clothes were beautiful, expensive, and completely wasted on someone who had no intention of impressing anyone. His love life was a deliberately barren landscape, and he intended to keep it that way.
Loneliness could be sharp sometimes, cutting through the quiet of his apartment. But it was still infinitely preferable to the discomfort of relationships, the vulnerability they demanded, the ways they could go wrong.
"Anyway," Luc continued, oblivious to Jay's internal spiral, "emergency or not, you look fantastic. Don't let it go to waste."
After Luc left, Jay was hyper-aware of every movement, every glance from colleagues, every reflection in the elevator doors. The clothes felt like a costume now, drawing attention he didn't want and couldn't handle. He pulled at the sleeves, trying to make it feel less fitted, less obvious. 
Amy walked past his desk and did a double-take. "New wardrobe?"
"Emergency purchase," Jay said quickly.
"Well, it looks great."
Each compliment felt like a weight added to his shoulders. He wasn't used to being noticed, didn't want to be noticed. The expensive fabric felt like a spotlight, highlighting everything he'd rather keep hidden. He knew his coworkers meant well, but being on display made his skin crawl.
The self-consciousness continued for the rest of the day. Jay threw himself into work with extra concentration, hoping to distract himself from the way people looked at him differently.
Ian, meanwhile, found concentration impossible. Every time Jay moved past his office, every glimpse of those perfectly fitted trousers sent his mind spiraling into territories he'd sworn off. He stared at the curve of Jay's neck when he leaned over files, the way the fabric moved across his shoulders when he reached for something.
By four o'clock, Ian had reread the same report three times without absorbing a single word. He closed his laptop with more force than necessary and rubbed his temples, trying to banish the increasingly vivid images that kept surfacing despite his best efforts.
This was exactly what he'd feared the most when Leo first suggested hiring Jay. Not the corporate espionage—that had been a convenient excuse. This. The way attraction could undermine years of self-control, how it could make even the most disciplined person lose perspective.
Jay wasn't Sam. Jay was earnest, hardworking, and completely uninterested in him. And that made it worse, because it meant Ian's feelings weren't just inappropriate—they were unwanted.
Jay was relieved when five arrived. He gathered his things quickly, eager to escape the day's accumulated discomfort. The storm had passed, leaving the city washed clean but still bitter cold.
Outside, he flagged a taxi without hesitation. If he'd taken a taxi this morning instead of relying on the public transport, none of this would have happened. The ride would have been inconvenient but manageable. Instead, his penny-pinching had cost him thousands in emergency clothing.
The taxi pulled up to his building, and Jay paid the fare without mentally calculating the metro alternative. Some lessons were expensive.
In his apartment, he hung the designer clothes with excessive care, spacing them properly in his closet. They looked absurd next to his regular wardrobe—like artefacts from someone else's life. He found a large zip-lock bag and sealed them inside, double-checking the closure. If these were going to represent two months of his salary, he'd make damn sure nothing happened to them.
He made a mental list: keep a spare suit at the office, always take taxis in bad weather, never again underestimate the city's capacity for chaos. The emergency fund he'd been building could absorb this hit, but it still stung.
As he heated leftover soup for dinner, Jay turned over the day's strange dichotomy in his mind. He'd looked better than he ever had in his life, and it had made him miserable. The compliments felt like spotlights, the attention like judgement. He preferred the invisibility, the safety of blending in.
At the other end of the city, Ian stood in his pristine kitchen, staring at a glass of wine he'd poured but hadn't touched. The image of Jay in kept surfacing, no matter how many times he pushed it down. The way the trousers had followed the line of his body, the flush in his cheeks from the cold and tea.
Ian had spent years building walls that had kept him safe and successful. But watching Jay work today had shaken something loose. The fantasies that followed were persistent, scenarios of slowly removing those beautiful clothes, discovering the body beneath, learning how his skin might feel and how he might respond to being touched.
He drained the wine in one swallow and headed for his home gym. Perhaps exhaustion could accomplish what discipline hadn't.
In his small apartment, Jay changed into something comfortable and tried to forget about designer fabrics and unwanted attention. Tomorrow he'd return to anonymity, and the expensive clothes would remain safely sealed in their plastic bag, waiting for a special occasion that would never come.
Neither man slept well that night, though for very different reasons.
Scene 3
Fever
Jay woke the next morning with a scratchy throat and a headache that felt like someone had wrapped his skull in cotton. He dismissed it as lingering stress from the previous day's financial disaster, downing extra coffee and forcing himself through his morning tasks.
By afternoon, the headache had evolved into something more sinister. His body ached, and he shivered despite the office's consistent temperature. When he stood to deliver files to Ian's office, the world tilted slightly.
"Are you feeling alright?" Ian asked, looking up from his desk. "You look pale."
"I'm fine," Jay said automatically, though his voice sounded rough. "Just tired."
Ian's expression remained sceptical, but he didn't press. Jay returned to his desk and pushed through the remaining hours with sheer determination, ignoring the way his vision seemed to blur around the edges. He didn’t stay late that day. As soon as the workday ended, he returned home, ate his reheated dinner with a dose of paracetamol, and went to bed early, hoping whatever he was coming down with wasn’t anything serious.
The next morning brought no ambiguity. Jay woke with a fever that made his apartment feel like an arctic wasteland, his throat feeling like sandpaper, and every muscle in his body screaming protest when he tried to sit up. The sound of his alarm felt distant, as if it were coming from underwater. He reached for his phone with trembling fingers, squinting at the bright screen that made his head pound worse. 
He should be getting ready for work, should be preparing for another day of managing Ian's schedule and sorting through the endless stream of reports. Instead, he could barely lift his head from the pillow without the room spinning.
The simple act of getting to his work phone required tremendous effort. He waited until seven-thirty and dialed Ian's number with shaking fingers, dreading the conversation.
"Jay? What's wrong?"
"I'm not feeling well. I won't be in today. I'm sorry for the short notice." The admission felt like failure.
"You sound terrible. Have you seen a doctor?"
"Not yet. It's probably just flu."
"Call your doctor immediately. Don't try to push through this. Take whatever time you need to recover properly."
"I can work from home if you need anything urgent."
"Absolutely not." Ian's tone brooked no argument. "Rest. The work will wait."
Jay wanted to protest, to insist he could handle whatever needed handling, but fever made coherent thought impossible. "I'll check my email—"
"No, you won't. That's an order."
After hanging up, Jay collapsed back onto his pillow, frustrated by his own weakness. The timing couldn't have been worse—the crushing workload demanded all hands on deck, and here he was, laid low by his own carelessness. Getting drenched in the storm had been stupid enough, but he'd been pushing himself too hard for weeks, while his immune system surrendered.
Finding medical care was overwhelming. He had a general practitioner, but getting an appointment required leaving home. The thought of sitting in a waiting room filled with other sick people made him feel worse.
After twenty minutes of dealing with automated phone systems while his fever spiked higher, he managed to secure a telehealth appointment for that afternoon. The doctor would call him at two o'clock, which meant he had hours to endure the mounting symptoms alone.
The doctor called precisely on time. "Describe your symptoms, please."
"Fever, chills, sore throat, body aches. Everything hurts."
"How long have you been experiencing these symptoms?"
"I woke up with them this morning, but I felt tired yesterday. Thought it was just the workload."
"Any nausea or difficulty breathing?"
"Some nausea. Breathing is okay, just tired."
The doctor asked several more questions about his symptoms and medical history before delivering the diagnosis. "This sounds like influenza. I'm prescribing antiviral medication and something for the fever. You'll need to stay home until you're fever-free. Given the severity of your symptoms, expect to be out of work for at least a week, possibly longer."
A week. Jay tried to process the information through his fever-fogged brain. Ian would have to manage without him during one of their busiest periods.
"I'm sending the prescriptions to your preferred pharmacy. The antiviral will help reduce the duration if you start it within the first forty-eight hours."
Jay gave her the information for the pharmacy near his apartment, then realized with growing panic that he had no way to get there. The thought of getting dressed and standing in line at the pharmacy felt impossible in his current state.
After the call ended, he spent thirty minutes researching delivery options. Most pharmacies offered same-day delivery for an additional fee, but the earliest available slot was six o'clock that evening.
He ordered the delivery service with shaking hands, entering his card information twice because the numbers kept blurring together. The extra charges for express delivery felt steep, but he had no alternative.
The wait until evening stretched endlessly. Jay alternated between burning fever and violent chills, wrapping himself in every blanket he owned only to throw them off minutes later when the heat became unbearable. He managed to drink water and nibble crackers, but solid food felt impossible with his inflamed throat.
The delivery arrived at six-thirty, and Jay answered the door in his bathrobe, accepting the pharmacy bag with hands that trembled so badly the delivery person looked concerned.
"You okay, man? You look really rough."
"Just the flu," Jay managed, closing the door and leaning against it for support.
The medications provided some relief, but recovery was slow and miserable. Jay lost track of days, existing in a haze of fever dreams interrupted by coughing fits. He drifted in and out of sleep, sometimes forgetting where he was, once waking convinced he was late for an important meeting that existed only in his delirious imagination. 
The weather outside matched his internal state. February was ending with a wet, gray dissolution as the accumulated snow melted into dirty slush that clogged the gutters and made the streets treacherous. From his window, Jay watched pedestrians cross the mess with careful steps, their faces hidden behind scarves and hoods.
He felt similarly liquefied, as if the fever had melted his composure and left him as shapeless and gray as the slush outside. Tasks like taking medication or ordering food required enormous effort. Showering was impossible, he could barely stand long enough to brush his teeth.
The apartment grew stuffy and cluttered as takeout containers accumulated on every surface—soup from the place nearby, bland rice dishes he could barely swallow, tea that went cold before he remembered to drink it.
By Sunday, the fever had broken, but the exhaustion remained crushing. Jay could think more clearly, which only made him more aware of the disaster his apartment had become. Empty containers covered the coffee table and kitchen counter, used tissues overflowed from the wastebasket, and dirty clothes lay where he had dropped them during fevered attempts to regulate his body temperature. The mess felt overwhelming, another symptom of his solitary existence. No one had checked on him, no one had brought soup or offered to run errands.
Ian had sent one text on Friday asking about his condition, and Jay had responded that he was following doctor's orders to rest and will take the next week off. No further communication had followed, which was appropriate given their relationship, but it contributed to the isolation that seemed to permeate everything. His work phone sat on the table, and he'd meant to keep it charged, but in his fevered state, he'd failed to plug it in properly. By Monday, it was dead.
Scene 4
Worry
Meanwhile, Ian discovered the true extent of his dependence on Jay. Tasks that Jay handled seamlessly now required Ian's direct attention, pulling him away from higher-level responsibilities.
But it was more than just inconvenience. The office felt empty without Jay at the desk outside his door. Ian kept glancing up from his work, expecting to see Jay's head bent over his computer.
"You're brooding," Leo observed, appearing in Ian's doorway Monday afternoon.
"I'm working."
"You're staring at your computer like it personally offended you. Missing your assistant?"
Ian's jaw tightened. "He's been out sick since Thursday."
"Have you called to check on him?"
"It's not appropriate."
"Since when do you care about appropriate?"
Ian turned back to his screen, but he couldn't focus. Leo was right, though Ian would never admit it. He'd been fighting the urge to call Jay every few hours, to drive to his apartment and check on him personally. The knowledge that Jay was alone in the city, with no family nearby, only made it worse.
By Wednesday, Ian's restraint was wearing thin. He'd called Jay's number twice, getting voicemail both times. The rational part of his mind knew that people got sick, that Jay was probably fine and just needed rest. But the irrational part imagined worst-case scenarios.
What if Jay's fever had spiked dangerously? What if he'd collapsed in his apartment with no one to help? What if—
Ian forced himself to stop. They weren't that close. Jay was his assistant, nothing more. Showing up at his apartment would be an overreach that would make them both uncomfortable. But knowing that didn't make the worry go away.
Thursday brought no word from Jay. Ian checked his phone constantly, jumping every time it buzzed. He'd composed and deleted a dozen text messages, each one crossing lines he wasn't sure he had the right to cross.
By Friday, Ian was barely functional. He'd called Jay's number twice more, each time going straight to voicemail.
He tried to focus on reports, on client calls, on the mundane tasks that Jay filtered and organized. But his attention kept drifting to the empty desk outside his office, to the unanswered phone calls, to the uncomfortable realization that somewhere along the way, Jay had become more than just competent support. Jay had become necessary. The office without him felt wrong.
The waiting felt like torture, and the concern that gnawed at him had very little to do with missed deadlines or disrupted schedules. It had everything to do with the way Jay's absence had revealed how much space he'd carved out in Ian's carefully ordered world.
Ian's phone buzzed against his desk. Jay's name appeared on the screen, and Ian's heart rate spiked embarrassingly.
"I'm feeling better and should be back Monday. Sorry for the extended absence."
Ian stared at the message, relief flooding through him. He wanted to type a dozen questions. Are you sure you're better? Do you need anything? Have you been eating?
Instead, he typed: "Take the weekend to recover fully. See you Monday."
It was appropriate and completely inadequate for what he wanted to say. Ian set his phone down and closed his eyes, finally allowing himself to breathe properly.
Jay set his phone aside and returned to the soup he'd been forcing himself to eat. Monday would come whether he was ready or not, and he needed to be strong enough to face the accumulated work waiting for him.
The past week felt like a dream now that his temperature had finally normalized. Days were filled with aching muscles and throat pain, broken only by the arrival of soup deliveries and the struggle to swallow medication. He started to feel better around Wednesday, but he'd still spent most of the time in bed, too weak to do anything productive.
Friday was the first day he'd felt fully human again, which meant confronting the disaster his apartment had become. Empty takeout containers covered every flat surface, laundry was scattered across the bedroom floor, and the sink was full of dishes he'd been too exhausted to wash. The air felt stale and sickly, and Jay realized he haven't opened the windows in over a week.
He tackled the cleanup, working in short bursts with rest breaks between tasks. Throwing away the accumulated trash took two trips to the building's dumpster. Doing the dishes required sitting on a kitchen stool to manage the dizziness. 
He found his company phone buried under a pile of used tissues on the nightstand, completely dead. The sight of the black screen triggered a flutter of anxiety. What if Ian had needed something urgent? What if there had been crisis situations requiring his attention? He plugged the phone into its charger and waited for it to power on, mentally preparing for a flood of missed calls and urgent messages. When the screen finally illuminated, he found four missed calls from Ian and texts from Luc and Amy wishing him fast recovery. He immediately sent a message to Ian and was relieved to get a quick reply—clearly, nothing too urgent had happened. He also let his colleagues know he would be back on Monday.
The much needed shower refreshed him both physically and mentally. Hot water washed away the staleness of sick days, and clean clothes felt like putting on a new identity. Jay looked at himself in the bathroom mirror and was startled by how pale and thin he looked. A week of barely eating had carved hollows under his eyes.
By evening, the apartment was habitable again, though far from pristine. Jay had managed to change his sheets, and the fresh air from the open windows had cleared out the worst of the stagnant atmosphere. Sleeping in clean bedding after days of fevered restlessness was soothing.
On Saturday he had enough energy for a shopping expedition. Jay wrapped himself in his winter coat and ventured to the grocery store, moving slowly through the aisles and  supporting himself on the shopping cart. He bought fresh fruit, vegetables, and ingredients for simple meals, foods that would help rebuild his strength after a week of living on soup and crackers.
The outside world felt overwhelming after days of isolation. Colors seemed too bright, sounds too loud, and the brief shopping trip left him exhausted. But it was proof that his body was healing.
On Sunday morning Jay stood in his kitchen and prepared a proper meal, scrambled eggs with fresh herbs, toast made from good bread, and freshly squeezed orange juice that tasted like sunshine after days of cold tea.
The breakfast tasted amazing after endless containers of mediocre takeout soup. Each bite held flavor and substance that reminded him why real food mattered, why taking care of himself was worth the effort even when no one else would notice.
Jay finished his meal and began planning the week ahead. Laundry needed completing, his work clothes required pressing, and he should prepare for whatever had accumulated during his absence. The illness had passed, his strength was returning, and normal life waited to resume.
He cleaned his dishes, putting his kitchen back in proper order. His ability to handle domestic tasks was evidence that he was truly better and not just managing symptoms. By evening, his apartment mirrored his recovery—clean, organized, ready for whatever came next.
Scene 5
Recovery
Jay stood in front of his bathroom mirror on Monday morning, adjusting the tie. He'd dressed in one of his elegant suits he'd purchased in January—conservative gray that made him look unremarkable. 
He looked pale in the morning light, his face thinner than it had been the previous week. The fever had burned through him completely, but he was functional and ready to return to work.
He arrived at Northlight Financial Center at eight o'clock—a deliberate choice to ease back into his workday.
The elevator ride to the thirty-fourth floor felt unusually long, his legs unsteady from a week of fever-induced inactivity. He'd managed to keep down solid food for two days running, which felt like a victory worth celebrating.
Ian's office was already illuminated when Jay reached his desk, and he could see Ian's silhouette through the glass. The sight brought unexpected comfort—some things, at least, remained constant.
Ian had been in his office since seven-thirty, checking his watch with increasing frequency as eight o'clock approached. When Jay failed to appear at an early hour, a knot of worry had formed in Ian's stomach. The relief he felt seeing Jay emerge from the elevator was massive.
Jay had barely sat on his chair when Ian appeared in his doorway with an expression Jay couldn't quite read.
"Good morning," Jay said. "I'll have your schedule updated within the hour."
"You're back," Ian said.
"Yes." Jay's voice still had a slight rasp, but it was worlds better than the sandpaper croak of the previous week. "I'm sorry for being out so long."
"You look..." Ian paused, clearly editing his words. "How are you feeling?"
"Much better. Thank you for your patience with the sick leave."
"Of course." Ian watched him intensely. Jay knew he didn't look great, but Ian's scrutiny felt like it went deeper than surface observations. "I'll be right back," Ian said abruptly, disappearing into his office.
Jay opened his laptop and began checking emails. A week's worth of messages awaited his attention, but Ian had handled the most urgent matters in his absence.
Ian returned with a large bag that looked suspicious. "Care package," he said, setting it on Jay's desk. "Standard procedure for assistants returning from illness."
Jay opened the package to find an impressive array of items: the same ginger tea that had warmed him during the storm, bottles of vitamins and supplements, packets of dried fruits and nuts, protein drinks, throat lozenges, and what looked like enough cold medicine to stock a pharmacy.
"This is... a lot," Jay said. "Thank you."
"My housekeeper insisted." 
"Standard procedure?"
"Of course."
Jay doubted that Ian had ever given a care package to any previous assistant, but he wasn't about to call attention to the inconsistency. "I appreciate it. Really."
"Make sure you use everything. Full recovery is important."
"I'll make sure to use everything," Jay agreed, though privately he wondered where he'd store it all in his small apartment.
"Good." Ian hesitated, then added, "Don't stay late this week. And don't come in early. Full recovery means proper rest."
"I understand."
As Ian returned to his office and Jay went about his business, he felt a warmth that had nothing to do with the ginger tea he was already brewing. The care package was evidence that his well-being mattered to someone.
The office was deep in the grip of March's demands, with everyone moving at the accelerated pace. Jay eased back into his tasks gradually, prioritizing the important assignments and letting less urgent matters wait.
The rest of the week passed on self-care. Ian insisted Jay maintain regular hours, no early arrivals or late departures until he was fully recovered. Jay didn't argue, recognizing that his previous approach to work-life balance had been unsustainable. Jay arrived at eight and left at five, taking taxis instead of the public transport, ignoring the daily assault on his budget. Despite his scepticism about their effectiveness, he swallowed the vitamins Ian had provided, drank the ginger tea, and went to bed at reasonable hours instead of staying up late to tackle additional work.
It felt strange, prioritizing his health over productivity. But Ian monitored his schedule, and Jay appreciated the gesture.
The work itself was manageable. Jay focused on Ian's most immediate needs. Ian seemed content with this arrangement, never pressing for more than Jay could comfortably handle.
"You're looking better," Amy mentioned on Wednesday, stopping by Jay's desk.
"Thank you. I feel better."
"Good. You scared us disappearing for a week like that."
Jay managed a smile. "I'll try to avoid it in the future."
But despite the recovery, Jay was subdued. The illness had stripped away more than just physical strength, it had reminded him of his fundamental isolation in the city. A week of fever-induced solitude had driven home the reality that he had no safety net beyond his own resources.
The care package from Ian had been a bright spot, but it also highlighted what was missing. Courteous gestures couldn’t fill the void left by the absence of real connections.
Ian noticed Jay's mood and told himself it was simply the lingering effects of illness. People took time to bounce back from the flu, especially when they'd been pushing themselves too hard beforehand. Spring would arrive eventually, bringing longer days and warmer weather, and Jay would recover his spirit.
But privately, Ian worried. Jay's pale complexion and sluggish movement indicated he was fighting to regain his strength. The way he rationed his energy spoke to a deeper exhaustion than simple physical strain.
"Are you sleeping better?" Ian asked on Friday afternoon as Jay prepared to leave.
"Yes, thank you. The supplements are helping."
"Good. Don't push yourself too hard."
Jay nodded and gathered his things. "I'll see you on Monday."
Ian watched him go, fighting the urge to say more. There were questions he wanted to ask, but they would cross lines he'd drawn for good reason. Jay was his assistant, not his friend, and certainly not anything more than that. The concern felt inappropriate, but Ian couldn't shake the feeling that Jay needed more support than he was receiving.
Jay had established a new order—sustainable, healthy, but somehow diminished. He performed his duties with typical competence, but the eager dedication that had once driven him to arrive early and stay late was gone.
Ian told himself this was probably better. More balanced. Healthier. But he missed the Jay who had stayed late by choice, who had brought an atmosphere to the office that Ian was only now realizing he'd come to depend on.
Chapter 13: Ides of March
Scene 1
Lunch discord
March came with the promise of spring that never materialized. The temperatures had crept up enough to melt most of the snow, but what remained was a persistent chill accompanied by dampness that seemed to seep through every layer of clothing. Gray skies stretched endlessly overhead, broken only by intermittent drizzle that made every outdoor excursion feel like a calculated risk.
The team had begun to cautiously revive their daily walks to nearby restaurants for lunch, venturing out whenever the weather and workload permitted. The outings had been on hold for weeks, because of heavy snow and mounting deadlines, and while neither had improved much, the conditions were tolerable enough, and everyone was tired of takeout eaten at their desks.
Jay still had lingering congestion and his voice held traces of hoarseness. His first week back, he avoided the lunch expeditions entirely, choosing instead sandwiches from the bakery he had discovered during those December days when he and Luc had eaten in the office kitchen.
The sandwiches were substantial—thick slices of fresh bread filled with quality meats and vegetables that made them feel like proper meals. Jay ate at his workspace, reluctant to test his recovering immune system against March's unpredictable weather.
"You sure you don't want to join us?" Luc asked on Wednesday, pulling on his jacket. "We're just going around the corner."
"I think I'll stick with staying dry today," Jay replied, unwrapping his turkey and swiss. "The cold air might trigger another coughing fit. Maybe next week when I'm feeling completely back to normal."
By his second week, Jay felt ready to rejoin the team's lunch outings, but he quickly discovered that the winter's isolation had affected more than just his health. The carefree atmosphere that had characterized their group outings before his illness had developed fractures that exposed everyone's frayed nerves.
"Where are we thinking today?" Amy asked on Monday, checking the weather app on her phone with visible frustration.
"Let's keep it simple. That pasta place nearby. Quick walk, good food, we're back within an hour," Luc said.
Kim looked up from his computer with obvious irritation. "We've been there five times in the past two weeks. There's a new fusion place that opened on Fifth Street that's supposed to be excellent."
"Fifth Street is a ten-minute walk each way in this weather," Luc countered. "Look outside—it's starting to drizzle again."
Amy stood at the window, watching drops streak down the glass. "Maybe we should just order in today. I really don't want to get soaked."
"We've been ordering takeout constantly," Kim replied. "I'm tired of eating soggy noodles from containers. Can't we make an effort to go somewhere decent for once?"
Jay watched the exchange with growing discomfort. The conversation held an edge of irritation that hadn't been present in their previous lunch discussions. Everyone seemed to be operating with shortened patience and elevated frustration levels.
"The pasta place is fine with me," Jay offered diplomatically.
They ended up at the nearest option, but Kim grumbled throughout the meal about the limited menu and predictable atmosphere. The pasta was well-prepared, but Kim's dissatisfaction cast a shadow over the entire lunch that left everyone feeling vaguely annoyed.
On Tuesday skies were clearer, and Kim immediately pushed for his fusion restaurant choice. Luc agreed reluctantly, muttering about the walk and the likelihood that they'd end up rushing back through puddles.
The fusion place turned out to serve inventive dishes in an atmosphere that was both trendy and comfortable, but the longer walk meant they had to eat quickly to return on time. Luc complained about the pace throughout the meal and continued his criticism during the walk back, noting every wet patch they had to cross and every gust of cold wind that cut through their jackets.
"The food was good," Amy said, clearly trying to defuse the tension. "Maybe we can come back when the weather improves."
"If the weather ever improves," Luc replied darkly.
Wednesday brought heavy rain that made outdoor dining impossible. They ordered from the Lotus Kitchen again, but the delivery arrived forty-five minutes late with lukewarm food that had clearly sat in the delivery vehicle too long. The noodles were congealed, the spring rolls soggy, and everyone ate in frustrated silence while watching the rain pound against the office windows.
Jay was caught between his colleagues' competing preferences and deteriorating moods. He tried to remain amiable, agreeing with whoever spoke first and avoiding any comments that might add to the tension. He worried about seeming difficult or high-maintenance by expressing preferences that might complicate the group's already strained decision-making process.
The atmosphere was fragile. Perhaps it was the accumulated stress of the harsh winter and heavy workload, or maybe the persistent gloominess of March's weather was affecting everyone's mood. Whatever the cause, the friendships that had sustained them through previous challenges now seemed strained by the mundane challenge of choosing where to eat lunch.
Through it all, Ian remained completely unaffected by the team's dining dilemmas. He ate his portioned meals at exactly one each day, sitting in the office kitchen with containers that were expertly prepared. The contents varied—sometimes grilled chicken with vegetables, occasionally salmon with quinoa, always balanced and deliberately planned.
Ian never participated in the lunch discussions or seemed bothered by the rain, the cold, or the team's fluctuating moods. His habits remained unchanged regardless of external circumstances, a consistency that Jay found comforting amid the general atmospheric tension.
March was shaping up to be one of the most challenging months Jay had experienced at Turner & Associates. His health was slowly improving, but the combination of persistent weather, workplace stress, and the team's unusual discord created an environment that felt more difficult.
Jay's energy levels remained inconsistent, good days alternating with afternoons when his stamina flagged without warning. The lingering effects of his illness made the gloomy weather feel more oppressive, as if the external atmosphere was amplifying his internal sense of depletion.
At least April was only a few weeks away, and with it the promise of better weather and the quarterly party that might help restore some of the team's camaraderie. For now, Jay focused on diplomatic neutrality and looked forward to the day when choosing a lunch destination would once again be a simple matter of preference.
Scene 2
Failure
The phone call came at three-fifteen on a Thursday, interrupting Ian's review of cash flow forecasts. The lawyer's voice was measured but held urgency that immediately commanded attention.
"We needed that compliance document by noon today, Ian. The deadline was absolute."
"Which document?"
"Subscription agreement addendum for Lagerstrom Holdings. I sent the reminder last week with all the specifics."
Ian pulled up his calendar, scanning for any mention of the deadline. Nothing appeared in his scheduled tasks or flagged emails. "Let me check my communications and call you back."
After ending the call, Ian searched his email inbox systematically. The Lagerstrom Holdings reminder wasn't in his priority folder or his pending actions list. He expanded his search to include all messages from the past two weeks and found it buried in his general inbox, marked as non-urgent.
Ian stared at the email, reading the requirements. The message should have been flagged immediately as high priority, scheduled on his calendar, and followed up with reminder notifications. Instead, it had been categorized as unimportant correspondence and forgotten.
He knew he’d have to bring it to Jay’s attention. Under normal circumstances, it would be a straightforward correction—point out the mistake, explain the proper procedure, and move on. But with Jay still looking pale from his recent illness, Ian was wary of making him dwell on it. The message had to be delivered without softening the facts, yet without making it seem like a disaster.
"Jay, could I speak with you for a moment?" Ian appeared at Jay's desk later that afternoon.
"Of course." Jay looked up from the client reports he had been reviewing, immediately sensing tension in Ian's posture.
They walked to Ian's office, and Ian closed the door before sitting behind his desk. Jay sat down, trying to read Ian's expression for clues about what might have gone wrong.
"There was an issue with Lagerstrom Holdings filing," Ian began. "The deadline was yesterday, and we missed it."
Jay felt his stomach drop.
"The law firm sent notification about the requirement last Tuesday. It was flagged as non-urgent."
The memory hit Jay like cold water. The legal email with its ambiguous subject and dense content, filed away as something that could wait for attention during less hectic periods.
"I'm sorry. I misunderstood the importance."
"Legal communications can be deliberately unclear. The subject lines rarely indicate urgency, and the language is often unnecessarily complex. It's a common challenge, even for experts."
Jay appreciated Ian's attempt to soften the criticism, but the fact remained—he had made a serious error that had real consequences. "What happens now?"
"We'll file a late submission with penalty fees. The client relationship isn't damaged, but it's an unnecessary expense and administrative complication. Going forward, when you're uncertain about a message's importance, the default should always be to flag it for my review."
"I understand."
"Mistakes happen, Jay. The important thing is learning from them and implementing systems to prevent repetition."
Jay managed to nod, but inside he felt like he was crumbling. Ian's patience and constructive approach only highlighted the trust he had violated and the standards he had failed to meet.
"I know. I'll be more careful."
"I'm confident you will be. This was an oversight, not a pattern."
"Is there anything else?" Jay asked in a  strained voice. Ian's reassurance seemed to make him feel worse, as though kindness was harder to bear than criticism.
"No, that covers it."
Jay returned to his desk with rigid movements. Ian watched him sit on his chair and resume working, looking like he was fighting back tears.
By five, Jay was relieved his recent illness had set the expectation that he’d leave at a normal hour. The thought of remaining in the office while struggling to maintain composure felt unbearable. He packed his things  and headed for the metro.
He walked to the station with his head down, replaying the conversation and trying to understand how he had made such a fundamental error. The legal email had seemed unimportant when he'd scanned it, but Ian was right—he should have read it more carefully, should have erred on the side of caution.
It wasn't his first mistake since starting the job. There had been moments when his inexperience showed despite his best efforts. Ian had always been patient with his errors, offering guidance without making him feel incompetent. But this felt different, more serious.
The metro was crowded with commuters, but Jay barely noticed the press of bodies around him. He stared at his reflection in the dark window, seeing someone who looked tired and defeated.
His apartment felt like a refuge when he finally reached it, like a sanctuary where he could safely fall apart. He went straight to his bed and pulled the covers over his head, seeking darkness and isolation from the world that had once again proven too challenging for his capabilities.
The past few weeks had been testing him to a degree he wasn't sure he could handle. The workload, the endless winter, office tensions, persistent illness, and now a failure that called into question everything he thought he had achieved.
Maybe he wasn't cut out for this work after all. Maybe Max's original assessment had been correct, and Jay was nothing more than a joke hire who had temporarily fooled everyone, including himself, into believing he belonged in such a prestigious environment.
The thought of facing Ian tomorrow, of sitting at his desk and trying to act normal while knowing he had disappointed the one person whose respect mattered most, was unbearable. How could he maintain composure when everything pointed to his inadequacy?
Eventually, hunger forced him from bed. Jay reheated his dinner and ate it slouched on the couch, too disconcerted to care about table manners. The food was tasteless, just fuel to sustain him through another evening of self-doubt. He left the dishes in the sink for the night, unable to muster his sense of order. 
The shower helped slightly, the hot water washing away some of the day's tension if not its underlying problems. Jay prepared his clothes for tomorrow with extra care, checking every detail to ensure nothing could go wrong with his appearance. He couldn't give Ian any reason to question his commitment.
Scene 3
Hot chocolate with rum
Jay arrived at the office fifteen minutes early, hoping the extra time would help him compose himself before facing his colleagues. He wore a smile that felt like a mask stretched too tight across his face. Every greeting required conscious effort, every interaction a performance designed to convince people he wasn't falling apart internally.
The effort was unsuccessful.
"Good morning, Jay," Amy said brightly as she passed his desk.
"Good morning!" Jay replied with forced enthusiasm. The smile he had rehearsed felt like a grimace, and Amy's brief look of confusion confirmed his failure to appear normal. Each forced moment of cheerfulness only highlighted his underlying distress.
Ian called him into his office at nine-thirty for their daily briefing, his manner unchanged from any other morning. He reviewed the day's schedule and asked Jay to prepare materials for an afternoon meeting.
Jay found Ian's consistency both comforting and unsettling. Part of him had expected different treatment—perhaps coolness or increased oversight—but Ian behaved exactly as he had the day before the mistake. The normalcy felt like a gift, proof that Ian still trusted him despite yesterday's failure.
But it also left Jay uncertain about where he stood. He wanted reassurance, wanted someone to tell him that mistakes were survivable and that his position was secure. Did Ian's unchanged behavior mean the mistake was truly forgiven, or was he simply maintaining decorum while privately reassessing Jay's capabilities?
Ian, meanwhile, struggled with his own internal conflict. From his office, he could see Jay's rigid posture and forced interactions, could practically feel the anxiety radiating from his desk. Every instinct told Ian to offer comfort, to find ways to demonstrate that the incident was insignificant.
His management training argued for different approach. Drawing more attention to the error might make Jay feel worse, might signal that the mistake was more serious than Ian had indicated. Better to demonstrate through behavior that their relationship remained stable.
Ian knew his concern went beyond appropriate managerial interest in team morale. He was worried about Jay specifically, personally, and it had nothing to do with work and everything to do with the young man's obvious distress.
Lunch provided no relief for Jay. The weather had finally begun to soften, with temperatures climbing and rain subsiding. The team was less grumpy about venturing outside, and they chose a restaurant that actually served appealing food.
But Jay could barely taste his meal. The conversation around him felt distant and unimportant, and his contributions were stilted. He questioned every comment, second-guessing responses that would have been natural weeks ago.
"Everything alright?" Ela asked during a lull in conversation.
"Fine," Jay said quickly. "I slept poorly. Must be the change of weather."
The lie was obvious to everyone at the table, but no one pressed for details.
Back at the office, Jay tried to lose himself in administrative tasks, but concentration remained elusive. Every email required multiple readings, every decision felt fraught with potential for additional mistakes.
"Jay?" Luc appeared at his desk around three o'clock, clearly concerned. "Can I talk to you for a minute?"
They walked to a quiet corner of the break room, away from the afternoon bustle.
"Is everything okay? You look like your pet just died."
Despite himself, Jay smiled at the dark humor. "I'm fine."
"No, you're really not. What's going on?"
Jay's composure finally cracked. "I made a serious mistake yesterday. Missed an important deadline because I misclassified an email. Ian was very patient about it, but..."
"But you're beating yourself up. Jay, these things happen. Trust me, whatever you did wasn't that serious in the grand scheme of things."
"It cost the company money. It was a compliance issue."
"So? Missed deadlines are part of business. We pay fees and move on. It's not career-ending."
Jay wanted to believe him, but the mistake felt too major to dismiss. 
"I let Ian down. He's been so patient with my learning curve, and I repaid that trust by screwing up something important." 
"Jay, if Ian fired everyone who made filing mistakes, this office would be empty. Trust me, you're not the first person to misread a legal notice."
At five, Luc appeared at Jay's desk again. "Come on. Let me buy you a drink. You need to get out of your head."
"I should probably just go home."
"Absolutely not. You're going to sit in your apartment and spiral into a depression about one mistake. That's not happening on my watch."
They walked together through the gradually warming streets, and Jay discovered that Luc lived remarkably close to the office.
"Fifteen-minute walk," Luc explained. "One metro stop if the weather's really terrible. I love this neighborhood, even though the rent is insane. The convenience is worth it."
He led Jay down a narrow side street to a small cafe tucked between a bookstore and a vintage clothing shop. The interior was warm and dimly lit, with atmosphere that invited long conversations.
"Two hot chocolates," Luc told the server. "With rum. And extra whipped cream."
"I don't drink during the week," Jay protested.
"Trust me. Nothing lifts the mood like chocolate, alcohol, and dairy products combined. This place makes the best hot chocolate in the city. Thick enough to stand a spoon in."
The hot chocolate arrived in enormous mugs, topped with clouds of whipped cream and dusted with cinnamon. It was absurdly rich and warming, the rum adding just enough heat to chase away the lingering chill of winter and worry.
"When I first started at Turner & Associates, I sent a confidential client report to the wrong email address. Complete security breach. The client was furious, compliance had to get involved, and I thought I was going to be fired on the spot."
"What happened?"
"Ian handled it masterfully. Took full responsibility, smoothed things over with the client, and then sat me down to review email protocols. My hands were shaking for a week afterward, but he never made me feel like an idiot. A missed deadline is nothing compared to that."
The warmth from the drink was spreading through Jay's chest, and he felt his shoulders relax slightly.
"I just don't want to disappoint him," Jay admitted.
"You won't. Ian values competence and dedication, both of which you have in abundance. One mistake doesn't erase months of great work."
The cafe's gentle atmosphere worked its magic on Jay's frayed nerves. The hot chocolate was exactly what he hadn't known he needed, comforting and shared with someone who understood the pressures of their work environment.
"Thank you," Jay said as they prepared to leave. "This really helped."
"Anytime. We all need someone to remind us that mistakes aren't the end of the world."
The walk back to the metro felt better than yesterday. Jay's mood remained dampened, but the tight knot of anxiety in his chest had loosened. Luc's perspective had provided exactly the reassurance that Jay had been unable to manufacture.
By the time Jay reached his apartment, he felt better. The mistake still troubled him, and tomorrow would still require more vigilance, but perhaps he could face it without the crushing weight of shame that had dominated his thoughts since yesterday afternoon.
Scene 4
Justice
By the end of the week, Jay stopped being so tense and his smile no longer required conscious effort to maintain. The immediate crisis of the missed deadline had passed, leaving behind a manageable residue of embarrassment and heightened caution. The crushing despair that had threatened to consume him seemed overdramatic in retrospect.
He had implemented a new system for email management—a final review of the non-urgent folder at the end of the day to catch any misfiled communications before they could cause problems. The additional step felt like insurance against future mistakes, a small price to pay for peace of mind.
"Looking better," Amy observed during their Friday afternoon coffee break. "Whatever was bothering you earlier seems to be resolving."
"Just had a rough patch. These things pass," Jay replied, and he meant it. Mistakes happened because he was human, not because he was inept. The important thing was learning from errors and implementing systems to prevent repetition. Luc's perspective had helped him understand that career included setbacks as well as successes.
Ian watched this progression with relief. Jay's recovery was proceeding exactly as it should—initial distress followed by practical adjustment and renewed determination. Ian’s choice to continue normal treatment instead of hovering with reassurance turned out to be right.
Luc's intervention had been supportive and perfectly timed. Ian wondered that perhaps Luc deserved recognition for his handling of a sensitive situation. A raise, maybe. 
He caught himself mid-thought and frowned. That was excessive. Luc had simply shown basic collegiality toward a distressed teammate. The situation had resolved naturally through peer-level intervention, nothing more.
He was giving the matter too much attention, creating significance where none existed. Jay was a valued employee who had made a mistake, received guidance, and moved forward. The end. But even as Ian told himself these things, he knew they were excuses. 
Thankfully, he didn't have much time to examine his feelings too deeply. His thoughts had been occupied lately by an entirely different matter, one that had nothing to do with filing systems and everything to do with a man named Ric Cruver, who had cornered Jay at the company gala three months ago.
Orrey Capital Partners maintained accounts worth tens of millions annually to Turner & Associates. They weren't handled by Ian's team directly, but fell under the firm's broader portfolio of institutional clients. Terminating such relationships required justification and senior management approval.
Ian had spent the past three months building that justification.
It had started with research—reviewing Orrey's public filings, examining their transaction patterns. Most companies had irregularities if you knew where to look, and Ian possessed both the expertise and motivation to look very thoroughly indeed.
The first discovery had been relatively minor—dubious accounting practices that didn't quite cross legal lines. Interesting but insufficient grounds for termination. Ian had continued digging. 
The months of investigation had yielded exactly what Ian had expected to find. The company presented a respectable facade that concealed ethical compromises typical of firms that employed predatory individuals in client-facing roles.
Offshore transfers through shell companies. Regulatory filings submitted with suspicious timing. Board members with connections to recently sanctioned entities. Individually, these elements might be explained as aggressive but legal business practices. Together, they created a pattern pointing to deeper issues.
The corruption Ian had uncovered was the final nail in the coffin. Orrey had apparently facilitated payments to overseas officials through a complex series of investment vehicles that provided plausible cover for what were essentially bribes. The transactions were sophisticated enough to avoid obvious detection, but transparent enough that any serious investigation would expose their true nature.
Ian had been careful to build his case through legitimate channels. Anonymous tips to regulatory agencies. Conversations with former Orrey employees who were willing to discuss their experiences. Public records requests that revealed inconsistencies in official statements.
Three weeks ago, a preliminary investigation had been opened. The inquiry was still confidential, but Ian had sources who kept him informed about such developments.
Yesterday, a former Orrey accountant had agreed to cooperate with investigators, providing documentation of internal practices that violated several regulations. The investigation was about to become very public and very serious.
Ian reviewed the memo he had prepared for Turner Senior one final time. The language was dry, focusing on valid business risks.
"Recent regulatory developments concerning Orrey Capital Partners require immediate attention. A formal investigation into the company's practices has been initiated, with particular focus on offshore transaction structures and reporting irregularities. Given the scope of the investigation and potential reputational risks to Turner & Associates, I recommend reviewing our client relationship."
The memo made no mention of Ric Cruver specifically, though Ian had no doubt that a man who would harass people at a corporate event was exactly the type to work for a company with ethical problems. Birds of a feather, as the saying went.
Ian had uncovered corruption through entirely legitimate means. The fact that his investigation had been motivated by personal reasons didn't invalidate the evidence or make his recommendations inappropriate. Orrey Capital Partners deserved whatever consequences their business practices brought them.
And if Cruver ended up unemployed when Orrey inevitably collapsed under regulatory scrutiny, well, that was simply karma operating as it should.
Ian signed the memo and placed it in his outgoing correspondence. Turner Senior would receive it Monday morning, along with supporting documentation that painted a clear picture of a client relationship that had become a liability.
Once submitted, the process would move quickly. Turner Senior trusted Ian's judgment on risk assessment, and the documented evidence was compelling enough to support immediate action. Within a week, Orrey would receive formal notification that Turner & Associates was terminating their business relationship. Cruver would lose his primary justification for attending Turner & Associates events, eliminating any future opportunity for him to come in contact with Jay. 
Jay continued reviewing emails at his desk, completely unaware that Ian had spent three months systematically destroying the life of the man who had made him uncomfortable at a corporate party. Ian intended to keep it that way. Jay didn't need to know about Cruver's impending downfall, just as he didn't need to know that Ian had been unable to forget the sight of Jay's terrified eyes that January evening. Ian returned to his work, confident that one problem was soon to be permanently resolved.
Scene 5
Orrey Fallout
Weather turned mild on the last Monday of March, and Jay's spirits had lifted as he walked to work without needing to brace against bitter wind. His new coat, which had been essential for survival during February's brutal cold, now felt almost too warm in the spring air. The lingering traces of his winter illness had finally disappeared. The sting of his mistake had faded.
The office atmosphere mirrored the seasonal change. Lunch plans were made with enthusiasm, and even Luc cracked jokes that didn't have undertones of weather-related misery.
Around ten-thirty, whispers began circulating through the entire floor. Jay caught fragments of conversations—mentions of investigations, regulatory problems, client relationships under review. The gossip network that connected Turner & Associates was subtle, and news was spreading quickly about something big.
"Jay," Luc appeared at his desk with a coffee cup. "Coffee break. I need to ask you about something."
They went to the kitchen and sat on chairs near the windows, while the coffee machine worked in the background as they talked.
"So," Luc began, "what do you know about Orrey Capital Partners?"
"Should I know something about them?"
"Come on, you manage Ian's email. There's no way you haven't seen communications about what's happening with them."
Even if Jay had seen such emails—which he hadn't, beyond the standard correspondence—he wouldn't have shared details with anyone. Ian's communications were confidential, and Jay treated them with the same discretion he would expect from a doctor or lawyer.
"I haven't seen anything unusual," Jay said honestly. "Why?"
"Word is spreading that Turner & Associates just terminated their relationship. Apparently there's some kind of investigation. Major scandal brewing."
Client terminations were rare events, and clearly serious enough to generate office-wide discussion when they occurred.
"That's only the third time in company history," Luc continued. "Last time was before either of us worked here. It's a big deal."
Amy conspicuously entered the kitchen and joined the conversation. "Official investigation. Major compliance violations, possible criminal charges. Turner Senior decided to cut ties immediately so we don't risk association."
"That's... a lot," Jay muttered.
As the day progressed, the Orrey situation dominated conversation. By lunch, everyone knew that a formal investigation has been opened. The team even changed their choice of restaurant for the day, opting for a small place with dim lighting and music just loud enough to cover hushed voices. Plates of butter chicken sat cooling as conversation circled endlessly around the same points: who had known what, who was likely to be implicated, who might already be cooperating with investigators.
Max claimed to know some of the Orrey partners by name. Luc kept prompting him for more gossip. Every revelation earned a chorus of murmured disbelief or dark amusement, as though the group couldn’t decide whether to be appalled or impressed.
Jay mostly listened. By the time they returned to the office, the Orrey scandal had grown in scope and consequence, reshaped by every retelling. Later that afternoon, details were emerging about offshore transactions and regulatory violations that painted a picture of systematic corruption.
Jay recalled Ric Cruver and felt a surge of vindictive pleasure. The man who had cornered him at the gala worked for a company now facing federal investigation. There was poetic justice in that development.
On Tuesday Ian called for an official team meeting where he formally announced Turner & Associates' decision to terminate the Orrey relationship.
"Effective immediately, we will no longer provide services to Orrey Capital Partners," Ian explained to the assembled staff. "The ongoing investigation presents reputational risks that outweigh any financial benefits of maintaining the relationship."
"What kind of investigation?" Max asked.
"Securities fraud, money laundering, violations of offshore reporting requirements. The scope is impressive."
Jay felt visible relief wash over him. Never having to see Cruver again, never having to worry about encountering him at corporate events, felt like an unexpected gift from the universe. His removal eliminated a source of background anxiety he was only now recognizing. Spring really was bringing good news.
Since Ian's team hadn't directly handled the Orrey account, the termination wouldn't create additional workload for them. But Ian, as CFO, was deeply involved in the transition process, and Jay noticed an immediate increase in legal emails and regulatory correspondence crossing his desk.
Jay flagged every Orrey-related message as urgent. Whatever bureaucratic complications arose from severing ties with corrupt clients, he was determined to ensure Ian had immediate access to all relevant information.
By Friday, major newspapers were reporting on the investigation. They ran a detailed analysis of Orrey's business practices and explored the broader implications for investment management oversight. Turner & Associates was mentioned favourably for their prompt action in distancing themselves from the scandal.
"We dodged a bullet," Ela observed during the afternoon break. "Being associated with this kind of mess would have been a nightmare."
Jay saw Ian read the newspaper coverage. Turner & Associates' reputation remained intact, their decisive action viewed as prudent risk management. But Ian still followed the news with concern.
Ian remembered his own scandal years earlier—the tabloid headlines, the invasive coverage, the way reporters had dissected every detail of his personal life when his relationship with Sam Connelly had exploded into public view. The ruthlessness with which his private affairs had been turned into entertainment for people who didn't know him.
Ian thought he had deserved the consequences. His poor judgement had created the mess entirely. The media attention had been brutal but expected—a natural result of his own foolishness.
Orrey's senior staff would face similar scrutiny, but Ian felt no sympathy for their situation. Unlike his own case, caused by naivety and lack of experience, Orrey's problems stemmed from deliberate corruption and ethical violations. They deserved whatever consequences their actions brought.
But the memory served as a sharp reminder of why personal feelings had no place in the workplace. Ian watched Jay organizing the latest batch of Orrey correspondence, clearly pleased that Cruver's employer was facing investigation and disgrace.
Jay looked better. The combination of improving weather, recovered health, and seeing his harasser's company destroyed had lifted his spirits. Ian was glad to see Jay's confidence returning, but the sight also reinforced his determination to maintain distance.
He could not afford to develop feelings for Jay, no matter how much he enjoyed the young man's company or how protective he felt about his well-being. The Orrey situation had worked out well, but it also demonstrated how quickly personal feelings could drive his decisions. Strictly business, he reminded himself. That was the only safe path forward.
Chapter 14: Spring
Scene 1
Quaterly reports
Bonus?
The beginning of April meant the crushing reality of quarterly reporting deadlines.
