Jay had experienced two previous quarters since joining Turner & Associates, but his newbie status had insulated him from fully understanding what the process demanded. He had observed his colleagues working longer hours and drinking more coffee, but hadn't grasped the true scope of the ordeal. In October, he had been employed for only two months, handing peripheral tasks without grasping the magnitude of the chaos, while the senior staff managed the critical work. January's year-end crunch had been spread over longer periods, with annual reports following more forgiving timelines than their quarterly counterparts.
The warning signs had been evident throughout the previous week. Overtime became standard, takeout containers once again accumulated in bins, and conversations grew increasingly terse as stress mounted. But Jay had been too absorbed in the Orrey scandal to fully register the building tension. Cruver's downfall had provided distraction from the approaching deadline storm.
Now, with April's first Monday stretching ahead like a battlefield, Jay understood what his colleagues meant when they spoke about quarterly reports with the same tone others reserved for natural disasters.
The office atmosphere was electric with stress. Conversations were reduced to essential communications. Lunch breaks disappeared entirely, replaced by hastily consumed sandwiches eaten at desks while reviewing spreadsheets. The office banter died away, leaving only the sound of keyboards, printers, and the occasional frustrated curse when numbers didn't reconcile properly.
Jay resumed his early arrivals without being asked, arriving at seven to find Ian already at his desk with coffee that indicated he had been there for some time. The change felt necessary—there was simply too much work to accomplish during normal business hours.
"Good morning," Jay said quietly, not wanting to disturb Ian's concentration.
Ian looked up with visible relief. "Thank you for coming in early."
"I figured you could use the support."
"I can. This is going to be an intense week."
Jay organized materials, flagged urgent communications, and handled administrative tasks that would otherwise take Ian's time during critical analysis periods. Everything felt secondary to the quarterly reports that consumed the office's collective focus.
The rest of the team had arrived by eight, each person bearing the determination of soldiers preparing for siege.
Jay spent the morning shuttling between departments, delivering documents, coordinating meetings, and handling the tasks that kept the reporting process functioning. His role had expanded beyond supporting Ian to serving as a general assistant for anyone who needed help maintaining their sanity during the crunch.
Ten PM arrived without anyone acknowledging the hour. Ian remained hunched over his computer, and Jay continued managing correspondence, prepared for this level of sustained effort by his previous experience with late nights.
When Jay finally left the office at eleven-thirty, Ian was still working. The pattern continued Tuesday through Thursday—Jay arriving at seven to find Ian already present, leaving at ten or later while Ian remained behind to refine reports that would be scrutinized by clients, regulators, and senior management.
Jay handled responsibilities he had never imagined fell within his job description. Coordinating with IT when servers crashed under the load of simultaneous report generation. Liaising with building security when access cards malfunctioned during a late-night work session. Managing food deliveries for a team too busy to leave their desks for meals.
By Friday, Jay understood why his colleagues had looked like zombies during previous quarters. Sleep became a scarce commodity. He would return to his apartment near midnight, shower quickly, and collapse into bed for five hours before the alarm demanded another day of relentless focus.
The weekend brought no respite. The entire team assembled in the office on Saturday morning, working through analyses that couldn't wait for Monday's continuation. Jay had never worked weekends before, but the experience felt like a rite of passage—proof that he truly belonged to the team that pushed through impossible deadlines together.
"First weekend crunch?" Max asked as they walked to the elevator.
"Yeah. I had no idea it would be this intense."
"The quarterly bonuses exist for a reason. These hours aren't sustainable without proper compensation."
Jay had wondered about the quarterly bonus system, thinking it seemed generous when he first learned about it. Now he understood it was hazard pay for surviving periods when work consumed every waking hour and personal life ceased to exist.
Sunday was marginally less frantic, but only because the most critical analyses were nearing completion. Jay spent the day formatting reports and ensuring all supporting documentation was properly organized. Ian moved through the office like a man possessed, reviewing every detail.
By Sunday evening, Jay felt a bone-deep exhaustion he had never experienced before. The physical fatigue was manageable, but the mental strain of maintaining perfect accuracy while operating on minimal sleep left him feeling hollowed out.
"We're almost through it," Luc said, appearing at Jay's desk with yet another cup of coffee. "Tuesday morning everything gets submitted, and we can return to merely overwhelming workloads instead of impossible ones."
"How do you do this every quarter?"
"Practice. And the knowledge that it's temporary. Plus the bonus makes the suffering worthwhile."
Jay glanced toward Ian's office, where the light burned as it had for days. Ian's dedication was absolute, his focus unshakeable even when exhaustion was clearly taking its toll. Jay had stayed late and arrived early, but Ian had been there before him every morning and remained after he left every night.
The realization that Ian was pushing himself even harder than the rest of them filled Jay with concern and admiration in equal measure. This was leadership—not just directing others through difficult periods, but bearing the heaviest burden personally.
Jay returned to his formatting tasks, determined to handle every possible detail so Ian could focus on the complex analysis work that only he could complete. If Ian was going to sacrifice sleep and personal time to meet their deadlines, Jay would ensure that sacrifice wasn't wasted on mundane tasks he could manage himself.
Tuesday finally arrived, and  Jay watched Ian submit the final file at six PM, then slump back in his chair exhausted. With it came the completion of reports that represented three months of complex financial management distilled into documents that would be scrutinized, analysed, and filed away until the next quarterly deadline demanded similar sacrifice.
Scene 2
Retreat arrangement
The insane workload of the first quarter was finally easing. The office atmosphere lightened, though everyone remained busy enough to still feel the strain.
Another quarterly party was approaching, and Jay was calm about it. The company events no longer felt like elaborate tests of his social adequacy. Eight months of experience had taught him what to expect—a sophisticated gathering with some clients, but nothing like the intimidating gala that had left him shaken in January. His colleagues, even those from other departments, had always treated him with respect.
The May retreat, however, remained a source of persistent anxiety. HR's continued updates painted a picture of three days of opulence that sounded simultaneously appealing and terrifying. Jay had cautiously asked his colleagues what to expect, hoping for reassurance.
"It's actually fun. Not like those corporate retreats where you have to do trust falls." Ela said.
Their enthusiasm did little to ease Jay's concerns about spending an entire weekend in such an intimidating environment with his colleagues.
The latest email from HR brought an unexpected twist: in the spirit of teamwork and mentorship, senior employees were encouraged to pair with junior ones for the trip. Everyone would be sharing a room—something that rarely happened at these events, where private accommodations were the norm. The change was presented as a way to promote better integration, though it seemed to catch most by surprise.
Jay watched as his team began to sort themselves out. Ela immediately claimed Kim, with whom she regularly socialized outside work, even if she was technically only a year ahead of him. Amy, one of the most senior employees, paired with Zoe, who had joined just over three years ago, before Luc. 
“You should stay with Max,” Luc said to Jay. “I can find someone in another department.”
“That works for me. Even out the numbers.” Max added cheerfully. 
Jay felt gratitude for his colleagues' consideration, but before he could respond, Leo appeared in their workspace with a grin that immediately put everyone on alert.
"Perfect timing," Leo announced. "I've been thinking about the retreat room assignments, and I have a brilliant idea."
Ian emerged from his office, clearly recognizing the tone. "Leo, what are you planning?"
"Team integration," Leo continued, undeterred. "The CFO should set an example by sharing a room with his assistant. Perfect even numbers, no one gets stuck with strangers, and it demonstrates that we're all equals here."
The silence that followed was deafening. Jay felt heat rise in his cheeks as he processed Leo's suggestion, immediately certain that Ian would find the idea appalling.
"That's completely inappropriate," Ian said, his voice tight with barely controlled anger.
Jay's stomach dropped. Of course Ian wouldn't want to share a room with him. "He's right," Jay said quickly. "It would be completely unthinkable for the CFO to stay in a room with someone of my calibre. Or with anyone at all, for that matter."
The hurt in Jay's voice was unmistakable, and Ian's expression fell. Leo's grin widened.
"Actually," Leo said smoothly, "all the C-suite managers are participating. I'm rooming with my junior staffer, and we've encouraged the same throughout the company. It's about breaking down hierarchies, not reinforcing them."
"Max would be the obvious choice for Ian," Amy said uncertainly.
"But that's hardly in the spirit of integration," Leo replied. "Max is already senior staff. Pairing with junior employees like Luc promotes better teamwork practices. Don't you think, Max?"
Max's jovial laugh filled the awkward silence. "Brilliant idea, Leo. Nothing builds team spirit like sharing a bathroom for three days."
"The rooms are quite spacious," Leo added. "Employee comfort is paramount. Two beds, sitting area, private balcony. No one will be roughing it."
Ian's jaw was clenched so tightly Jay worried he might crack a tooth. "I don't have any concerns beyond the well-being of my subordinates. I don't want to make anyone uncomfortable."
"Then it's decided," Leo said brightly. "I'll make the arrangements with HR immediately. This is going to be fun."
He departed with obvious glee, leaving behind a group of colleagues trying to process what had just happened.
"Jay," Luc said quietly, "if this makes you uncomfortable, I'll talk to Ian. Leo can be put in his place."
"I'm not uncomfortable," Jay said, though his voice lacked conviction. "I just don't think I'm quite worthy of sharing a room with Ian."
Luc laughed. "Ian's not a medieval lord, Jay. We're all coworkers of the same standing. He can be intimidating, but—"
"He's not intimidating," Jay interrupted. "He's stern, but he's very comfortable to be around."
"Alright then," Luc said with a smile. "You'll have a great time."
Jay nodded, trying to convince himself that was true. At least he knew Ian was trustworthy and kind. Whatever his reservations about the arrangement, Jay was certain he wouldn't suffer any grievances.
Twenty minutes later, Ian cornered Leo in the break room.
"What the hell do you think you're doing?"
Leo's expression remained cheerful. "Helping my dearest friend find happiness, even if it means I have to suffer extreme violence in the process."
"You're going to suffer more than violence if you don't fix this."
"Think about it rationally," Leo said, switching to a more diplomatic tone. "An event like this is going to be overwhelming for Jay. Having you in his corner will actually make it easier for him. You'll be protecting him from discomfort."
"I'll be crossing every line."
"Imagine if Jay got paired with Max and had to listen to his crass comments half the night. Or ended up with some stranger from accounting."
Ian's anger faltered slightly. The thought of Jay feeling vulnerable or out of place at the retreat was distressing.
"You're manipulating me," Ian said.
"Absolutely. But I'm not wrong about Jay needing someone safe in his corner. And whether you want to admit it or not, you're the person he trusts most."
Ian stared at his friend, recognizing the truth in Leo's words despite his frustration. Jay had looked hurt when he'd protested the arrangement, as if Ian's objection had been personal.
"Three days, Leo. Three days of temptation I've been carefully avoiding."
"Three days of making sure one of your employees feels supported and comfortable. That's what good managers do."
Ian's resistance crumbled under the weight of his own protective instincts. "I'm going to make your life miserable for this."
"I'm counting on it," Leo said with a grin. "But you'll thank me eventually."
Ian doubted that, but as he returned to his office, he couldn't deny that the prospect of spending three days ensuring Jay's comfort felt less like a burden and more like a privilege he didn't deserve. The retreat was still weeks away, but already it loomed as a test of every defence Ian had built against his growing feelings.
Jay, meanwhile, returned to his work with cautious optimism. Whatever his initial anxiety about the arrangement, he trusted Ian more than anyone else at the company. If he had to manage three days of splendour and team-building activities, there was no one he'd rather have watching his back.
Scene 3
Spring has sprung
As April went on, spring came into full bloom. The city shed its winter coat, revealing patches of green that emerged from every crack and corner. Jay felt his energy returning with the season, and he tackled the backlog of life that had accumulated during his illness and the intense work period.
He set aside an older suit to keep at work—still presentable, if not as refined as his current rotation. It was the perfect choice for emergencies. He slipped it into a garment bag and brought it to the office, hanging it discreetly in the small coat closet near his desk. Better to be prepared than caught off guard again.
The rain-soaked suit had been rescued from the dry cleaners the week before, returned to its former condition without any trace of its ordeal. Jay examined it, running his fingers along the fabric. The relief was disproportionate to the cost of replacement, but he couldn't shake his ingrained frugality despite the increase in his bank account.
The lavish boutique outfit hung in his closet like a monument to poor decision-making. Every time he saw it, he winced at the memory of spending thousands on clothes he never wanted to buy. 
The begging of the month had brought a sizable quarterly bonus, pushing his financial situation into a comfortable territory. Yet the emergency shopping spree still rankled. He'd grown up counting every penny, and old habits died hard.
His apartment needed better preparation for future crises. The medicine cabinet overflowed with Ian's care package, but he'd been caught unprepared in other ways. He spent an afternoon organizing supplies: shelf-stable canned soups, crispbread, bottled water, ice packs, wet wipes, tissues. Everything placed strategically wherever his studio permitted.
The gym membership had been on his mental to-do list for months. He'd been thinking about Ian's obvious fitness, his perfect posture. Jay's own physical condition was embarrassing by comparison. 
He'd researched options in his neighborhood months ago but never followed through.  He opted for a modest facility in a nondescript building near his apartment. Their website promised beginner-friendly programs—exactly what he needed.
Turner & Associates provided memberships to upscale fitness centers in the financial district, but the thought of running into colleagues while sweating through a workout made him uncomfortable. This place felt manageable.
The gym occupied the ground floor, with painted concrete floors and equipment that looked worn but was well-maintained. The front desk clerk, a woman in her fifties, handed him a clipboard. "First time?"
"Yes. I saw you have a beginner program?"
"Absolutely. Rus will be your trainer today. He's great with people who are just starting out."
Rus turned out to be younger than Jay expected, with a patient demeanour. He was neither intimidatingly muscular nor dismissively casual—just encouraging.
"What are your goals?" Rus asked as they walked through the gym floor.
"I'm completely out of shape. I got sick recently and realized I need to take better care of myself."
"Good motivation. We'll start easy and build from there."
The first ten minutes on the treadmill were humbling. Jay's breathing became labored faster than he'd expected, and sweat soaked through his new gym shirt before he'd even broken into a proper jog. Rus noticed his struggle and guided him to a rowing machine.
"This is better for building endurance without impact. Try it for five minutes."
The rowing was easier on his lungs but brutal on muscles he'd forgotten he had. By the end of the hour, Jay felt simultaneously exhausted and energized. His legs shook as he walked to the locker room.
"How do you feel?" Rus asked as Jay gathered his things.
"Like I haven't moved my body in years."
"That's normal. Try to come back twice a week. Build the habit first. Intensity comes later."
Jay walked home slowly, but the soreness was not entirely unpleasant, giving him a sense of finally taking action instead of just thinking about it.
His phone rang that afternoon while he was cooking dinner. His mother's name appeared on the screen, and he answered with the slight wariness that family conversations always brought.
"Jay, sweetheart, how are you?"
"I'm good, Mom."
"Good, good. I'm so glad. Listen, I wanted to talk to you about something exciting. Ema's graduating from middle school this year."
Jay waited for more information, unsure why this required a phone call. His half-sister was fifteen, which made middle school graduation fairly predictable.
"She's been working so hard on this cheerleading act for the ceremony," his mother continued. "The whole squad is performing, and it's really important to her. She's been practicing for months."
The request was coming. Jay could hear it building in his mother's phrasing.
"The graduation is at the end of June, and we're making it a real family celebration. Your aunt and uncle are coming, and even your cousin Min. It's been so long since we've all been together."
Jay's stomach tightened. He hadn't been back to his hometown in over a year, not since the awkward Christmas visit before last. The idea of handling family dynamics felt exhausting.
"Ema specifically asked if you were coming," his mother continued. "She says you're the only one who never treats her like a little kid."
That was news to Jay. His interactions with Ema had been minimal and mostly centered around polite small talk during holiday visits. Maybe the Christmas present he’d sent had an impact.
"I'll have to check my schedule. Work's been really busy lately."
"Of course, honey. I know your job is important. But it would mean so much to her—to all of us—if you could make it. We hardly ever see you anymore."
The guilt came wrapped in affection, voiced with concern that made it impossible to ignore. His mother had a gift for that—never demanding, never harsh, just earnest enough to make refusal feel ungrateful. While Jay loved her, unease stirred beneath the warmth. Going home always felt a little like stepping into someone else’s life.
"I'll try, Mom. I really will. But I can't promise anything right now."
"That's all I ask. Just think about it, okay? It's been too long since you've been home."
After the call ended, Jay sat in his apartment, dinner preparations forgotten. Home. The word felt strange, disconnected from the small town where he'd grown up feeling like an outsider. His mother's house, filled with his step-father's family photos and his half-siblings' achievements, had never quite felt like his place.
He pushed the decision aside for now. The company retreat loomed in his immediate future, and that felt like enough stress to handle at the moment. June was still months away. By then, maybe he'd have a better sense of whether he could face going back to where he'd started.
Scene 4
Confidence
The night of the quarterly gathering crept up quickly, and Jay set about getting dressed without the apprehension he once felt. The navy suit felt like an old friend when Jay pulled it from his closet. The same outfit that had made him feel like a fraud at his first quarterly party now seemed perfect—elegant but conservative, well-fitted without being flashy. He selected a tie with subtle burgundy stripes, bolder than his normal choices but not enough to make him feel exposed.
The evening ahead didn't trigger the anxiety that had plagued him before. He understood these events and knew what to expect now. 
The venue was an upscale hotel, as always. Jay walked through the entrance without the awkward scanning he'd done at his first company event. He didn't need to search for familiar faces or worry about standing alone looking lost. He headed straight for the buffet, knowing exactly what he wanted. Experience had taught him that Max would materialize near the food within minutes of his own arrival, drawn by an infallible instinct for quality catering.
He was working on his second helping of an outstanding meat pie when Max appeared with Ela and Kim in tow.
"Started without us, I see," Max said, loading his plate with generous portions.
"The lamb is great," Jay replied, cutting another piece. "I wasn't about to let it get cold."
Kim sampled something that might have been duck confit. "They really outdid themselves this time. Last quarter's spread was great, but this restaurant is spectacular."
"The wine selection is impressive too," Ela added, returning from the bar with a glass of something that caught the light like liquid amber.
The conversation flowed around food and ambiance, comfortable topics that required no performance. Jay was relaxed, which would have been impossible months ago.
The rest of the team filtered in gradually. Jay tried the colorful cocktails without worrying about maintaining perfect composure, sampled appetizers without calculating whether he looked appropriately sophisticated doing so.
When Ian appeared, he moved through the room with grace, exchanging pleasantries with each group. Jay watched him approach their circle without the sharp awareness that had once made every interaction feel like a test.
"Evening, everyone. I trust you're all enjoying the hospitality."
"The catering budget was well spent," Amy replied. "Though I suspect we'll be hearing about it in the next expense review."
“Worth every penny,” Ian remarked as he took a bite.
He included Jay in the brief conversation that followed, nothing more or less than he offered the others. Jay felt neither snubbed nor singled out, just part of the group. When Ian moved on to entertain the senior partners, Jay realized how foolish his previous anxiety had been.
The evening dispersed into smaller conversations, people drifting between groups as mood and interest dictated. Jay remained near the buffet, no longer self-conscious about his appetite.
Luc returned after a circuit of the room to find Jay working on a tropical pudding topped with crystallized ginger.
"Still eating?" Luc asked with amusement. "You've been at this for an hour."
"The food is superb," Jay replied without shame, taking another spoonful. "It deserves to be appreciated."
"You look more at ease than during previous events," Luc observed.
"After the trauma of the gala, a small company party feels almost like home. I recognize nearly everyone in the room now. Maybe not on speaking terms with all of them, but I know the faces."
"That's progress," Luc said. "Though I'm not sure your relationship with the dessert table qualifies as friendly anymore. It's more like devotion."
Jay laughed. "I prefer to think of it as mutual attraction."
Near the end of the event, Leo approached carefully, as if he was testing uncertain waters. They hadn't spoken privately since his visit to Jay's apartment months ago, when the conversation had ended with tension and unresolved feelings.
"Jay. Enjoying yourself?"
"The food is outstanding. The company is tolerable."
"I thought you'd forgiven me," Leo said, though his tone made it clear he’d expected this reaction.
"I have. But I still don't trust you at all."
"Fair enough. I'm glad you made the best of the opportunity that was presented to you, regardless of what the underlying motives were."
"So am I. The opportunity turned out well. No thanks to your methods."
"Undoubtedly. But I'm still taking credit for the introduction."
Jay returned home that evening content. He'd survived the event without anxiety, without the desperate need to prove himself that had characterized his early months at Turner & Associates.
From across the room, Ian had watched Jay's confidence with relief. He remembered the deliberate coldness he'd shown at Jay's first quarterly party, the way he'd let his own emotional turmoil dictate his behavior. The memory made him feel ashamed.
Jay had looked lost that night, unsure of his place. Ian had recognized the vulnerability and responded by making it worse, using his own authority to reinforce Jay's sense of exclusion. He'd been shielding himself from feelings he didn't want to acknowledge, but Jay had paid the price for Ian's emotional confusion.
Tonight, Jay had needed no protection from the environment or the people in it. He'd moved through the evening freely, at ease with colleagues who had become friends. Watching him laugh at something Luc said, Ian felt Jay had found his place here. Ian had played a role in that success, but he'd also created unnecessary obstacles along the way.
Whatever complicated feelings Jay stirred in him, they were his burden to bear. Jay should never again be on the receiving end of Ian's struggles, never again made to feel unwelcome because of battles he hadn't chosen to fight.
The resolution sat heavy in Ian's chest as he made his final rounds of the evening, a promise to himself that felt both necessary and impossibly difficult to keep.
Scene 5
Coffee gesture
The quarterly party had come and gone, a pleasant enough evening, but unremarkable enough that Monday morning brought minimal discussion of who had attended and who had managed graceful early exits. 
April had delivered an unpleasant surprise from the weather gods. After a few days of spring warmth that had everyone shedding heavy coats and planning outdoor lunch destinations, the temperature had plummeted overnight, returning the city to winter's grip.
"I'm not going out in that," Luc declared, staring through the office windows at the gray sky that threatened rain or possibly snow. "Let's just order from the usual places."
"Agreed," Amy said.
Zoe also nodded. "I spent all morning looking forward to not having to wear my heavy coat anymore. I'm not changing my mind now."
But Kim was undeterred by the hostile weather. "There's a new place I want to try. Sweet and spicy with tropical influences. Could be interesting."
"Or terrible," Max grumbled, but he was already reaching for his coat. "But I suppose terrible food beats another day of noodles."
Jay became part of a smaller expedition—just the four of them braving the cold while half the team remained in the office's climate-controlled comfort. It felt different, like a specialized mission.
"Spicy with fruit," Kim read from his phone as they approached the restaurant. "The reviews are either raving or completely horrified. No middle ground." 
"That's a good sign," Max said dryly. "Nothing quite like polarizing opinions about food."
The restaurant was decorated with bright murals that seemed to mock the gray weather outside. The menu contained a bewildering array of dishes that combined flavors Jay would never have imagined together.
"Mango habanero chicken with coconut rice," Kim read enthusiastically. "Or pineapple curry beef with plantain chips."
"I'm getting a normal sandwich," Ela announced after scanning the options with obvious skepticism. "This is too experimental for a Monday."
Jay ordered something described as "glazed chicken with lychee and pickled dragonfruit," uncertain whether he was ordering lunch or volunteering for torture. The quarterly reports marathon had left him feeling more adventurous about small risks, as if surviving that ordeal had earned him permission to be slightly bolder in other areas.
Kim and Ela fell into animated conversation about flavor combinations and restaurant concepts. Max provided commentary that grew increasingly amused as their meal progressed.
"This is actually interesting," Jay admitted, surprised by how the unusual combinations somehow worked together.
Kim was delighted with his choices, while Ela remained unconvinced that experimental cuisine was an improvement over traditional seafood restaurants.
"It's fine for a one-time experience," she concluded, "but I'll stick with places that do one thing really well."
The walk back to the office reinforced Jay's gratitude for his expensive winter coat. The wind cut through the business district, making April feel more like February. His colleagues hunched into their outerwear while he remained comfortably insulated from the elements.
Halfway back, Jay noticed a new coffee shop that had opened recently in a space formerly occupied by a cellular phone store. The interior looked warm and inviting, with chalkboard menus advertising seasonal specials that seemed perfect for combating the unexpected chill.
"What if we brought coffee back for everyone? Something to help with team morale after this weather disappointment. I can use the company card."
Kim brightened immediately. "Good thinking."
Jay had been managing the department's discretionary expense account for months but had found few opportunities to use it for team benefits. Coffee seemed like an appropriate expenditure for boosting spirits during dreary weather.
Inside the coffee shop, they examined the seasonal offerings with the same adventurous spirit that had guided their lunch choices. Spring blend with lavender and honey. Cardamom spice latte with oat milk foam. Cherry blossom white chocolate mocha that seemed designed specifically for April's conflicted weather.
"Let's get everyone something different," Jay decided. "Make it an adventure for the whole team."
They placed orders for seven specialty drinks, each person receiving whatever struck the group as interesting or appropriate. Jay deliberated over Ian's selection longer than the others. Ian typically drank black coffee from a specific blend. Consistent, no surprises.
But Jay felt reckless. He ordered Ian a maple bourbon latte with cinnamon, knowing it was nothing like his preferences but hoping the gesture would outweigh any reservations.
The walk back to the office involved some coordination to prevent spills while avoiding wind that seemed determined to test their balance. They entered the building with the triumphant air of explorers returning from successful expedition.
"Coffee delivery service," Kim announced to the office at large, distributing the various drinks while explaining their selections.
Jay approached Ian's office with the maple bourbon latte, suddenly uncertain about his impulsive choice. Ian looked up from his computer with mild curiosity as Jay placed the cup on his desk.
"What's this?"
"Seasonal coffee from the new place down the street. Thought it might help with team morale given the weather."
Ian's expression was complicated—surprise mixed with something that might have been amusement or concern. "You didn't have to do that."
"I know. But everyone seemed to need something to lift their spirits."
Ian lifted the cup and took a sip, his face registering the unexpected flavors with diplomatic neutrality. "It's... interesting."
"You don't have to drink it if you don't like it. I know it's not your usual style."
"No, it's fine." Ian took another sip, seeming to consider the taste more thoughtfully. "Actually, it's quite good. Different, but good."
Jay felt relieved. "The company card has been sitting unused for months. Seemed like a good opportunity."
After Jay returned to his desk, Ian continued sipping the unfamiliar coffee. The maple sweetness was foreign to his palate, more dessert than beverage, but the gesture made it oddly enjoyable.
Jay had felt confident enough to make an independent decision about team morale, comfortable enough to use discretionary funds without seeking approval first. The coffee was evidence that Jay felt at home at Turner & Associates, that he saw himself as someone with agency to improve his colleagues' day.
The sweetness of the drink reminded Ian uncomfortably of Jay himself—kindness offered without calculation or expectation of reward. It was rare to encounter such generosity in their industry, where most gestures had hidden motives or strategic considerations.
Ian finished the entire cup despite his preference for savoury flavors, reluctant to waste something Jay had chosen specifically for him. The gesture had been small, but this thoughtfulness made long workdays more bearable.
He would have to be careful not to read too much significance into this, Ian reminded himself. It was a good development for Jay. Nothing more than that.
Chapter 15: Inferiority
Scene 1
Industry reading
The cold snap persisted throughout the week, generating daily debates about lunch destinations that had evolved from frustrated complaints into good-natured banter. The team's disposition had improved markedly since the brutal winter months—even when the weather disappointed, conversations held undertones of amusement.
"I'm staging a protest," Luc announced Wednesday morning, staring out the windows at yet another gray sky. "This is supposed to be spring. Where are the flowers? Where is the warmth? I demand answers from the meteorological authorities."
"File a complaint with nature. I'm sure they'll get right back to you," Kim said.
"At least it's getting warmer each day," Amy pointed out. "Yesterday was definitely less miserable than Monday."
She was right. Despite the continued overcast skies, the temperature had been climbing, making the walks to restaurants more tolerable even if they remained unpleasant. The promise of spring weather felt tantalizingly close.
The lunch expeditions varied in composition throughout the week. Some days half the team ventured out while others remained behind for delivery orders. Other days, everyone braved the weather with good-natured grumbling about April's fickleness.
But each day brought subtle improvement—a degree or two warmer, slightly less wind, occasional glimpses of sun that promised better weather ahead. By Thursday, even Luc had stopped lamenting the cold, though he continued to wear his winter coat with pointed emphasis on his suffering.
The approaching Easter weekend dominated conversation as the afternoon arrived. Four days off stretched ahead like a gift, the first extended break since the Christmas holidays.
"Family dinner at my parents' place," Max explained during their afternoon coffee break. "My mother has been planning the menu since February. She treats Easter like a culinary competition."
"Sounds lovely," Amy said. "We're driving to my aunt's farm. Fresh air, home cooking, absolutely no internet connection."
"I'm jealous," Zoe admitted. "I might hang out with some friends, but nothing definite. Probably just catch up on sleep and maybe see some movies."
"What about you, Luc?" Kim asked.
Luc's expression darkened comically. "Spending the weekend with my girlfriend's family. Three days of being interrogated about my career prospects and long-term intentions. I may not survive."
"It can't be that bad," Jay said.
"You don't know her father. He views every conversation as a job interview." Luc slumped in his chair dramatically. "I'd rather work through the holiday."
"No, you wouldn't. Nobody would rather work through Easter." Kim corrected.
"Fair point. But the family visit is still going to be torturous."
The group turned to Jay expectantly. "What about you? Any plans?"
"Nothing special. Rest, relaxation, maybe catch up on some industry reading I've been meaning to finish."
The reaction was immediate and unanimous.
"Industry reading? During a holiday weekend?" Max exclaimed.
"Jay, you're supposed to be resting. No work thoughts, no business materials, nothing related to Turner & Associates," Amy said.
Ela chimed in with equally dramatic warning. "You'll turn into Ian if you're not careful. He probably has financial statements bookmarked as leisure reading."
Jay laughed at their collective horror. "It's not really work reading. The industry publications  are interesting. Market analysis, regulatory developments, case studies of successful investment strategies—it's fascinating material."
"That's what Ian would say. Next you'll be telling us you enjoy reviewing compliance manuals for fun."  Luc observed darkly. 
"I don't enjoy compliance manuals. But understanding how markets function, learning about different investment approaches, reading about economic trends—that's actually engaging. It helps me understand the context of what we do here."
Kim shook his head in mock despair. "He's lost to us. Another casualty of corporate ambition."
"I'm not ambitious in that way. I just find it interesting to learn about the industry. It's not about getting ahead, it's about understanding."
"Promise us you'll do something purely recreational. Watch terrible television, read a novel, take long baths. Something completely unrelated to finance." Amy insisted. 
"I'll probably visit the library near my apartment. They have a good fiction section."
This seemed to satisfy his colleagues somewhat, though they continued to tease him about his idea of holiday relaxation. Jay didn't mind the gentle mockery—it felt affectionate, evidence of how comfortable he had become with the team.
By five, the office had begun its exodus for the long weekend. Jay remained at his desk longer than the others, organizing files and clearing his inbox for Tuesday's return. The administrative tasks that accumulated during busy periods required attention, and he preferred returning from breaks to clean systems without backlogs.
By five-forty-five, the office was nearly empty except for the cleaning staff. Jay gathered his things and prepared to leave, looking forward to four days without alarms or deadlines. But as he looked towards Ian's office, he could see him still absorbed in his tasks, surrounded by papers that indicated hours of additional work ahead. Ian showed no signs of preparing to leave, no indication that he recognized the approaching holiday weekend.
Jay paused in the hallway, wanting to say something but uncertain what words would be appropriate. He could suggest that Ian take the evening off, but that felt presumptuous. He could ask if Ian needed assistance, but that would obligate Jay to stay when he had already completed his own responsibilities. Instead, Jay approached Ian's doorway and knocked gently on the frame.
Ian looked up with mild surprise, as if he had forgotten other people existed in the building.
"I'm heading out. Just wanted to wish you a good spring break."
"Thank you. Enjoy your time off."
"Are you planning to leave soon?"
Ian glanced at the papers scattered across his desk. "I have a few things to finish up first."
Jay suspected that "a few things" actually meant several hours of complex tasks. The same deflection Ian employed whenever anyone said he might be working too much.
"Well, don't stay too late," Jay said, knowing the words were pointless but feeling compelled to say them anyway.
"I won't. Have a good weekend, Jay."
Jay left the office with Ian's response echoing in his mind, looking back as he headed for the elevator. Through the glass walls, he could see Ian already reabsorbed in his work, the papers and computer screen commanding his complete attention.
The sight made Jay feel something unfamiliar—less like admiration for Ian’s dedication and more like sadness. Everyone else was heading home to family dinners, countryside retreats, or simple relaxation. Ian remained alone in an empty office, choosing work over whatever personal life he might have waiting elsewhere.
As the elevator took Jay toward the lobby and the weekend beyond, he hoped that Ian would eventually recognize the value of rest and companionship. But he suspected that hope was probably futile.
Scene 2
Easter
Jay spent Easter weekend exactly as he had planned—alone in his apartment with a stack of economics texts spread across his dining table. While his colleagues enjoyed family gatherings and countryside retreats, he immersed himself in monetary policy analysis and emerging market trends.
The decision wasn't born from fascination with macroeconomic theory, though he had grown to appreciate the subject's complexity. It stemmed from a persistent anxiety about his credentials that no amount of workplace success seemed to alleviate.
His economics degree from a local university had none of the prestige that seemed to follow his colleagues. Just a middling education from a third-tier institution that had been affordable.
Jay had never heard anyone at Turner & Associates make disparaging comments about his academic background. Ian treated him with consistent respect, his colleagues included him in discussions without condescension, and his work was valued regardless of where he had learned to perform it. But the knowledge of his inferior credentials gnawed at him regardless.
Leo's original hiring rationale—that Jay was attractive enough to serve as decoration while performing basic administrative tasks—had wounded him precisely because it highlighted what he already suspected. He would never have secured such a prestigious position based purely on qualifications. The bitter truth was that his looks had opened a door that merit alone would have kept firmly closed.
The economics textbooks represented his attempt to bridge that gap through self-directed learning. He had recently ordered them online, deliberately choosing volumes more advanced than the ones he had kept from his college days, that spoke more directly to the demands of his current position. If he couldn't change his educational pedigree, he could at least expand his knowledge base beyond what his modest degree had provided.
Friday and Saturday passed in concentrated study, interrupted only by meals and brief walks to prevent the eye strain that came from hours of reading. Jay worked through chapters on international finance, took notes on concepts that challenged his understanding, and flagged sections for future review.
Sunday morning brought his single concession to holiday tradition—a breakfast that echoed childhood Easter meals with his grandmother. Hard-boiled eggs prepared exactly as she used to make them, served with specialty ham and lamb that lent a festive touch to his simple kitchen.
The memory of those breakfasts was among the few pleasant recollections from his early years. His grandmother had been a stern woman, made bitter by the burden of raising her unwed teenage daughter and the illegitimate grandson who came with her. Love had been scarce in that household, but basic care was provided with grudging reliability.
Jay understood now that his grandmother had been overwhelmed by responsibilities she hadn't chosen. A woman in her fifties suddenly caring for a baby while supporting her own daughter through the consequences of poor decisions. What he remembered as coldness was more likely exhaustion.
But Easter breakfasts had been different. The holiday had softened his grandmother's severity, creating space for small kindnesses that made the meal feel special. Jay recreated that atmosphere as best he could in his adult kitchen, enjoying the memory despite the complicated emotions it brought.
His mother called during the afternoon, her voice holding the warmth that always made Jay feel guilty about their limited contact.
"How are you spending Easter, sweetheart?" she asked.
"Quietly. Reading, relaxing. How's your celebration?"
She updated him about the holidays and his half-siblings' school achievements. Ari was excelling in mathematics, Ema had joined the debate team, Tim was already considering college applications.
Jay felt happiness for his siblings, who would never experience the uncertainty that had defined his own childhood. They had a father who was present and engaged, a stable household where children were not accidental burdens.
"I'm glad they are doing well, Mom," Jay said, meaning it completely.
"Are you taking care of yourself? You work so hard."
"I'm fine, Mom. Really. The job is good, the people are nice. I'm exactly where I want to be."
It was true, mostly. After the call ended, Jay thought about the contrast between his siblings' secure upbringing and his own fractured early years. But he felt no resentment toward his mother or her husband, no bitterness about the different treatment. His mother had been barely eighteen when he was born, unprepared for the responsibilities that followed. She deserved the happiness she had found with his stepfather and the family they had built together.
Jay's circumstances were his own to manage. He had been dealt a particular hand—modest background, limited resources, questionable academic credentials—and it was up to him to play it as skilfully as possible.
He took a walk through his neighbourhood in the late afternoon, happy the weather was warm enough to make outdoor exercise pleasant. The streets were quiet, most people apparently occupied with family gatherings or social activities. Jay preferred the solitude, using the time to process the morning's reading while enjoying the physical movement.
Back in his apartment, he returned to the economics texts with renewed focus. On Monday he started the second, more challenging book—a dense analysis of global financial systems that required frequent breaks and repeated reading of complex passages. Jay made notes, flagged concepts for further research, and pushed through sections that tested his comprehension limits.
By evening, he had completed one book entirely and made some progress through the second. The weekend felt productive, a feat that purely recreational activities wouldn't have matched.
The irony wasn't lost on him—just days earlier, he had felt sad watching Ian work alone in the empty office, viewing such dedication as somehow tragic. Now Jay realized he had spent his own holiday in almost identical fashion, choosing development over social activities.
But he felt no self-pity about the parallel. Quite the opposite—he felt fortunate beyond reasonable expectation. Tomorrow he would walk into Turner & Associates and collaborate with brilliant, accomplished people who treated him as an equal despite his inferior credentials. That opportunity was far more than someone with his background had any right to expect.
The least he could do was work continuously to deserve it. If self-directed education during holidays was the price of maintaining his position among people who belonged in that environment, it seemed like a bargain worth accepting.
Scene 3
Return to normalcy
The morning air was warm as Jay walked toward Northlight Financial Center at seven-twenty. He had shed his winter coat permanently, trading it for a light jacket. The earlier sunrise made the city feel more alive.
Turner & Associates' floor was empty when he arrived. Jay sat at his workstation and powered up his computer, scanning through emails that had accumulated over the holiday weekend. Nothing urgent waited for his attention—a comfort that would have been impossible during the brutal first quarter.
Ian arrived precisely at eight, looking more refreshed than he had in months.
"Good morning, Jay. How was your Easter?"
"Productive, thanks. How was yours?"
"Much the same. I managed to avoid checking emails for almost twenty-four hours. Consider it a personal victory."
Jay smiled. "That does sound like progress. At least you weren't here at six this morning."
"The early starts were getting ridiculous, even by my standards." Ian moved toward his office, then paused. "Did you do anything special for the holiday?"
"Read some economics texts, made myself a proper breakfast. Nothing elaborate." Jay kept his voice casual, not wanting to emphasize how solitary his weekend had been.
"Sounds perfectly reasonable. Better than the alternative of forced family gatherings."
Jay didn't press. He had learned to appreciate these brief moments of conversation without trying to extend them beyond their natural course.
By eight-thirty, the office filled with returning colleagues. Luc arrived looking distinctly less cheerful than normal, dropping into his chair with an audible sigh.
"Rough weekend?" Jay called from across the room.
"Girlfriend's family reunion. Her uncle monopolized every conversation with political rants, her grandmother criticized my table manners, and her cousin's children treated the place like a playground."
Amy emerged from the elevator wearing an expression of mild exasperation. "At least you had indoor plumbing. My daughters spent the entire weekend complaining about the lack of internet access at the cottage. Apparently, disconnecting from social media for three days constitutes cruel and unusual punishment."
"How old are they again?" Zoe asked.
"Fifteen and thirteen. Prime drama queen ages. They acted like I was punishing them personally by choosing a spot without reliable WiFi."
Max appeared cheerful, though he patted his midsection ruefully. "My mother outdid herself this year. Ham, roast, three different lamb dishes, and enough dessert to feed a small army. I may need to spend the next month living on salads."
Zoe pulled out her phone and scrolled through photos. "I went hiking with some university friends. We found this amazing trail system about two hours north of the city. The wildflowers are already blooming, and the views were incredible."
She showed the screen to anyone within range, displaying pictures of rolling hills dotted with early spring colours.
Kim and Ela arrived together, exchanging what could only be described as conspiratorial glances. When Max asked about their weekend, they both became unusually evasive.
"Oh, you know. Relaxing. Nothing special," Ela said, avoiding eye contact.
"Very relaxing," Kim agreed, then caught Ela's expression and stifled what sounded suspiciously like a laugh.
The exchange didn't go unnoticed by the rest of the team, but nobody pressed for details.
"What about you, Jay?" Amy asked. "Please tell me you did something more interesting than studying."
Jay hesitated, knowing his answer would provoke exactly the reaction he wanted to avoid. "I spent most of the weekend reading. Picked up some economics textbooks I'd been meaning to get through."
The response was immediate and unanimous.
"Jay, no," Luc groaned.
"Please tell me you're joking," Amy said.
"Economics textbooks? During a holiday weekend?" Max shook his head. 
Jay felt heat rise in his cheeks but tried to keep his tone light. "I enjoyed it, actually. Besides, it's useful knowledge for this job."
"You already do excellent work," Max said. "You don't need to study during your time off."
The conversation moved on to other topics, but Jay caught several concerned glances directed his way throughout the morning. He appreciated their worry, even if he couldn't explain why the weekend had felt exactly right to him.
The office resumed its previous pace. Projects moved forward without the frantic urgency that had characterized the first quarter. When noon arrived, the team gathered near the elevators to decide on their destination.
"Where are we going?" Max asked.
"Ela's been talking about that seafood place," Amy said.
Ela's face lit up. "The one with the incredible crab cakes? Yes! I've been craving them for weeks."
The suggestion met with general approval, and the group headed out into the warm afternoon air. Jay walked beside Kim, who was still exchanging secretive smiles with Ela.
The seafood restaurant was an excellent choice. The spring weather made outdoor seating comfortable, and the relaxed atmosphere encouraged conversation. Jay listened to his colleagues discuss work projects and the general improvements in everyone's mood now that the intense workload had eased.
He realized how much he had grown to value these moments. Six months ago, he would have been terrified of these gatherings, worried about saying the wrong thing or revealing his inadequacy. Now he contributed naturally to conversations.
The afternoon passed smoothly, with everyone handling their respective assignments at a sustainable pace. When five o'clock arrived, most of the team began packing. Jay, however, remained at his desk, pulling up formatting templates for reports that Ian would need the following day.
The office gradually emptied until only Jay and Ian remained. The workload didn't truly justify staying late, but the idea of Ian working alone evening after evening continued to bother Jay. He couldn't articulate why the image of his boss maintaining solitary vigil in the empty office seemed so troubling, but the feeling persisted.
He tackled minor tasks that had accumulated during the busy period. These could wait until later, but doing them now meant fewer distractions during regular hours.
Jay arranged his workstation thoughtfully, thinking how it might look to someone observing the space. His colleagues had personalized their areas with pictures, small decorative items, books. Zoe kept a small succulent plant, Kim displayed an action figure from some science fiction series, and Max had framed photographs of his family.
Jay's desk remained notably bare by comparison. He decided to bring one of the economics textbooks tomorrow—specifically the one he had finished over the weekend but found useful enough to keep as a reference. Perhaps some magazines as well, for the slower periods that might eventually arrive.
At eight o'clock, Ian emerged from his office carrying his briefcase and jacket.
"Ready to head out?" he asked.
Jay saved his work and gathered his belongings. They walked to the elevators together, the building now mostly empty except for security staff and cleaning crews.
"Thank you for staying late," Ian said as they waited for the elevator. "The extra preparation time helps with tomorrow's schedule."
"It's no trouble. Besides, the workload is finally manageable enough to catch up on things."
The elevator arrived, and they stepped inside. Jay pressed the lobby button while Ian selected the garage level.
"The pace is definitely more reasonable now. I appreciate everyone's patience during the difficult months."
"Everyone understands. Results like ours don't come without some sacrifice."
The elevator stopped at the lobby level, and Jay stepped out.
"Have a good evening," Ian said.
"You too."
The lobby doors closed, and Jay walked out into the spring air. The temperature had dropped enough to make his light jacket useful, but the evening remained pleasant as he walked toward the metro station.
The spring break had helped put the ordeal of the first quarter behind them. Jay felt a calm he wouldn’t have believed possible just weeks earlier, when every day brought new crises and impossible deadlines. The desperation of the winter months had finally lifted, leaving space for regular work that Jay had always preferred.
Scene 4
Comfortable warmth
The weather turned pleasant as April progressed, warm enough for lunch outings to consistently happen without the constant complaints about the cold. Jay seized every opportunity to steer the group toward Willow & Sage, which had become his personal mission to explore completely.
"Their spring menu is great," he mentioned during Tuesday's morning coffee break.
"Not the bistro again," Kim groaned.
"The tomato basil soup is amazing. And I'm working through their entire soup menu systematically,"  Jay replied, unrepentant.
"How many varieties do they have?" Amy asked.
"Fourteen rotating options. I've tried eight so far."
He no longer flinched at spending so much money on lunch, his budget comfortably accommodating what had once felt like extravagance. The bistro's rotating soup menu had become his personal challenge—he was determined to sample every variety they provided.
The lunch crowd no longer required complex negotiations about time and location. Jay was at the centre of these casual arrangements, his enthusiasm for the bistro powerful enough to overcome any reluctance about the prices.
At Willow & Sage, he worked his way through a bowl of roasted fennel bisque while listening to Ela's gossip. The spring air drifted through open windows, bringing the scent of flowering trees from the nearby park. He couldn't remember the last time he'd felt this consistently content.
The workload had returned to manageable levels. The team rarely stayed past six anymore, the crisis-driven overtime becoming an exception. Ian still stayed late most nights, a habit that seemed ingrained regardless of actual necessity.
Jay occasionally remained when his own work warranted it—maybe once or twice a week, when documents required an urgent review. During these late evenings, it was mostly just the two of them in the office, working diligently side by side. Conversation, when it happened, was minimal and strictly confined to the task at hand. 
Even after all these months, Ian remained reserved, and Jay had learned not to expect more. They established a comfortable, if dull, pace. Ian didn't discouraged these stays. He was secretly elated by Jay's soothing company that brought some joy to those otherwise solitary hours.
The Friday drinks tradition had resumed with the return of reasonable schedules. Jay's refusal had become expected, meeting Luc's good-natured persistence with dry humour.
"Come on, Jay. Tomorrow's supposed to be beautiful. We could even sit outside at Murphy's."
"I'm sure the weather will be just as beautiful from my apartment window."
"You're missing out on valuable team bonding," Luc insisted.
"I'm bonding with this grilled vegetable panini right now. It's very fulfilling."
That afternoon, instead of heading straight home, Jay took a detour to the small park he'd discovered during his first weeks at Turner & Associates. He'd never returned since that initial exploration, too caught up in the anxiety of fitting in to spare time for simple pleasures.
The spring bloom had made the space more charming. Trees displayed clouds of pink and white blossoms, and flower beds showed the first bright colours of the season. The park felt more tranquil than he remembered, insulated from the city's noise by landscaping and the gentle murmur of a small fountain.
An artisanal ice cream truck had parked near the main path, with hand-painted menu board advertising flavours Jay had never heard of. He ordered something called sea buckthorn with honey and cream, curiosity overcoming caution with unfamiliar foods.
The bench where he sat faced west, catching the afternoon sun through the new leaves. Jay ate slowly, savouring the unexpected combination of flavours while watching office workers cut through the park on their way to metro stations and bus stops.
The contentment was unfamiliar, this desire to sit and simply exist without purpose or agenda. He'd never been someone who sought out moments of leisure, too accustomed to the anxiety that made stillness feel like wasted time. But here, with the sun warm on his face and the strange, delicious ice cream melting on his tongue, he felt at peace.
The lack of anxiety was still new enough to notice, like the absence of a headache that had been ignored until it disappeared. He thought about joining the team for drinks next Friday, imagining their surprise when he actually showed up. The idea made him smile.
Tuesday afternoons had become gym days, scheduled around Rus's availability. The trainer turned out to be a pleasant, easygoing man in his thirties who managed Jay's pace expertly, clearly accustomed to guiding beginners.
"Let's add five minutes to the treadmill today," Rus said during Jay's third week of regular attendance. "You've been building good endurance."
Jay could manage the treadmill with less difficulty than before, breathing steadily even as the minutes passed.
"How are you feeling?" Rus asked after twenty-five minutes.
"Less like I'm going to die," Jay admitted, stepping down from the treadmill  "Though still moderately terrible."
"That's progress. Your stamina is definitely improving."
Jay nodded, the five additional minutes feeling like a step forward.
"I've put together a simple set of exercises you can do at home," Rus continued, handing Jay a printed sheet. "Nothing that requires equipment or much space. It should help with overall conditioning."
The plan looked manageable: stretching, squats, pushups, and basic core work. Jay grudgingly committed to fifteen minutes every other day, fitting it into his morning, though he suspected his enthusiasm for home exercise would be limited.
The gym felt less intimidating than he'd feared. The other members were focused on their own workouts, paying no attention to his efforts. Jay took pride in the visible improvements—he could spend longer on the treadmill now before running out of breath, manage more pushups before his arms gave out. It was enough to maintain his motivation.
In the evenings, he'd taken to reading management books, studying team dynamics and effective leadership, on top of the economics literature he was already working through. The theoretical frameworks helped him understand the mechanisms behind Ian's seemingly effortless authority.
Every chapter reinforced his admiration for Ian's approach. What had appeared straightforward from the outside revealed itself as complex orchestration, requiring constant attention to individual personalities, group dynamics, and organizational pressures. Ian made it look easy, but the books made clear how much skill and experience went into that appearance of ease.
Jay closed the volume and set it aside, looking out at the city lights through his apartment window. The spring evening was warm enough to leave the windows open, bringing the sounds of traffic into his quiet space. Jay was content to savour this season of unexpected peace.
Scene 5
Intrusion
Ian watched from his office doorway as Jay gathered his belongings at precisely five o'clock, the same pattern he had observed for the past several weeks. Every Tuesday without fail, Jay departed at closing time while other days he might stay late to tackle additional work.
Despite months of demonstrated reliability and competence, despite Jay's obvious dedication and honest character, Ian still paid him far more attention than necessity warranted. Not from suspicion anymore, but from fascination.
"Goodbye, Ian," Jay said as he was leaving.
"Have a good evening," Ian replied.
The office emptied gradually as the rest of the team left as well. Ian remained, reviewing performance reports. By six-thirty, the floor had fallen quiet. Ian emerged from his office, needing to retrieve files from the main storage area. But instead, he paused beside Jay's workstation, examining the small changes that had accumulated over recent weeks.
The space no longer maintained its original sterile state. Jay had added subtle personal touches that spoke to growing comfort with his environment. A spare suit hung in the small closet—a sensible precaution after the winter storm incident that had forced him to purchase emergency clothing. The desk drawers now contained basic medical supplies, evidence he had learned to prepare for unexpected circumstances.
Two items particularly caught Ian's attention: an economics textbook and several current issues of financial journals. The book was a respected academic text that Ian recognized from his own university years—intermediate level material that covered monetary policy, international finance, and market dynamics relevant to their daily work.
Ian picked up the textbook and immediately noticed the abundance of small coloured tabs protruding from its pages. Jay had marked passages extensively, creating a reference system. The sight triggered a memory of similar study habits from his own academic years, when textbooks became personal guides.
Ian knew he was crossing a line, but curiosity overcame his better judgement, and he opened the book to a random page. Jay's handwriting filled the margins—the same beautiful script that appeared on document annotations and project notes throughout their shared work. It created an unexpected sense of intimacy, as if Ian were reading private correspondence.
The notes revealed a structured approach to learning. Jay had not simply highlighted important passages but had engaged with the material, asking questions, drawing connections, and referencing additional sources. Comments like "check acquisition model—similar approach?" and "correlation with policy discussion from Monday's meeting" demonstrated that Jay was connecting theoretical concepts to their practical applications.
Ian sat on Jay's chair, drawn deeper into this accidental glimpse of how Jay's mind worked. The margins contained tiny revelations: moments where Jay had clearly struggled with complex concepts, breakthrough insights marked with enthusiastic exclamation points.
One dense section on international capital flows contained a series of increasingly confident notes. Jay had initially written "need to review this again—I don't get it," but subsequent annotations showed his growing understanding of the material. By the chapter's end, his notes had evolved into a detailed analysis.
The progression charmed him more than he cared to admit. Here was evidence of intellectual curiosity combined with effort—someone who refused to accept limitations imposed by his educational background and instead committed to continuous improvement.
Ian turned pages slowly, discovering more about Jay through his private academic conversations with himself. The young man emerged as someone who questioned assumptions, sought multiple perspectives, but at the same time remained humble. Comments like "am I understanding this correctly?" and "need to verify this interpretation" revealed someone more concerned with accuracy than appearing knowledgeable.
Some notes made Ian smile involuntarily. Jay had written "finally makes sense!" beside a complex explanation of derivative markets, and "why didn't they explain it this way initially?" after working through a challenging mathematical proof. The enthusiasm was endearing.
The journals contained similar annotations, though focused on current events. Jay had connected recent market movements to broader economic principles, noted discrepancies between different analysts' interpretations, and identified trends that related to their clients' investment strategies.
Ian had been reading for over an hour, completely absorbed in Jay's private educational journey. The invasion of privacy should have troubled him, but instead he felt a surge of affection he couldn't suppress.
These notes painted a picture of a person who approached learning with curiosity and without superficial ambition. The contrast with his own privileged educational background was stark—Ian had attended elite institutions, enjoyed access to the best tutors and materials.
Another lengthy note caught his attention: "The relationship between interest rate policy and employment figures is more complex than I originally understood. Need to reconsider some of my assumptions about decision-making. Maybe the delayed effects explain why Ian always seems to anticipate policy changes before they're announced?"
The mention of his own name made his heart skip a beat. Jay was trying to understand not just abstract economic principles, but the practical wisdom Ian applied in his daily decisions. It warmed him to know Jay’s thoughts had turned to him even in his private moments.
The building had grown completely quiet around him, the city lights beginning to dominate the view from the large windows. Ian closed the textbook, returning it to its exact position on Jay's desk. He remained in the chair for several more minutes, surrounded by the evidence of Jay's pursuit of knowledge.
Sitting in the empty office, Ian acknowledged what had been building for months despite his resistance. His attention to Jay's schedule, his awareness of Jay's reactions, his concern for Jay's well-being—none of it stemmed from obligation or suspicion.
It was becoming increasingly difficult to maintain the distance he had imposed between them, especially when every interaction revealed new reasons to be fond of the young man. Jay's dedication, modesty, consideration for others despite his own circumstances—all of it contributed to a growing attachment that Ian knew he should resist.
The feelings were dangerous and entirely inappropriate. Ian had learned that lesson the hard way, though he understood now that what he felt for Jay was fundamentally different from that earlier infatuation. Sam had been exciting—charming and magnetic, disarming Ian's caution. Jay was none of those things, but his warmth had somehow become essential. Whatever he felt for Jay, it had grown beyond his ability to rationalize it away.
Freefall
Chapter 16: Preparations
Scene 1
Client meeting
The morning sun streamed through Ian's office windows as he reviewed the final details for the Calder Wetherby Trust meeting. Outside, the city basked in a spring weather so perfect that it made even the most dedicated workaholic consider taking lunch outdoors.
"Jay, could I speak with you for a moment?" he called through his open office door.
Jay appeared within seconds. "Yes?"
"I have a meeting tomorrow with Calder Wetherby Trust. It's scheduled for one o'clock at their offices. I'd like you to accompany me."
Jay felt his stomach tighten. Client meetings represented territory he had barely explored during his time at the company. "What would you need me to do?"
"Take notes, primarily. Handle any documentation that comes up during the discussion. It's not complicated, but having someone there to track details allows me to focus on the conversation itself."
The request was reasonable—assistants accompanied executives to important meetings regularly. But Jay had grown comfortable with his behind-the-scenes roles. He rarely needed to participate in high-stakes client interactions.
"Is there anything specific I should prepare for?"
"Not particularly. Tomorrow's meeting is more relationship maintenance than active negotiation." Ian paused, seeming to consider Jay's obvious apprehension. "You'll do fine. Just bring your laptop and take notes on anything that seems important."
Jay nodded, already mentally reviewing his limited experience with client-facing responsibilities. The prospect made him nervous, but Ian's confidence was reassuring.
"We'll leave at noon to allow plenty of time. The meeting shouldn't run longer than an hour."
Ian rarely attended client meetings personally—that responsibility typically fell to Leo or the dedicated client relations team. Leo possessed the natural charm and social grace that made him perfectly suited for client schmoozing.
As CFO, Ian's role centred on internal operations and strategic oversight, and not the social management that such encounters required. But Ken Wetherby had specifically requested Ian, and when one of Turner & Associates' four largest clients made such requests, exceptions were made.
After Jay returned to his desk, Ian thought about the decision to include him. Officially, having an assistant present for note-taking was perfectly standard. Unofficially, Ian had grown accustomed to Jay's company and found the prospect of handling the meeting alone less appealing than it should have been.
The next morning brought cloudless skies and temperatures that made the walk to work pleasant. Jay arrived early, wearing what he considered his most formal suit—gray with a black tie. The combination was conservative to the point of severity, clearly chosen to project maximum professionalism. He took special care to ensure that every detail appeared flawless, from his precisely knotted tie to his polished shoes. The last thing he wanted was to embarrass Ian in front of such an important client.
At his desk, he spent extra time reviewing the Calder Wetherby file, but since it was one of the most important clients in their portfolio, he was already well-acquainted with their account history. 
At noon precisely, Ian appeared at his desk. "Ready?"
They descended to the garage level where Turner & Associates' company car waited. The driver held the rear door open as they got in. Jay had ridden in the company car before, but the vehicle still felt incongruous with his modest background. Leather seats and a partition separating them from the driver created an atmosphere of affluent privacy.
"The drive should take about thirty minutes. Traffic permitting," Ian said.
Jay watched the cityscape change as they moved from the financial district toward residential areas he had never visited. Glass towers and concrete gave way to tree-lined streets and architectural grandeur that belonged to a different era entirely. The buildings grew larger and more ornate, set back from the street by manicured lawns and elaborate landscaping. Even the sidewalks looked more expensive.
"I've never been to this part of the city," Jay admitted, unable to hide his fascination with the scenery.
"It's quite different from downtown. More space, obviously. Different pace of life."
The understatement was remarkable. Jay stared at mansion after mansion, each more impressive than the last. Wrought-iron gates, circular driveways, gardens that probably required entire teams for maintenance. The wealth on display was almost abstract in its magnitude.
Ian had grown up in this world and rarely thought about its impact on newcomers. Watching Jay's reaction reminded him of the first time he had brought Sam to similar areas, though Jay's response was more reticent than Sam's had been.
Where Sam had made jokes about excessive wealth and ostentatious display, Jay simply observed with appreciation for beauty. His wonder seemed untainted by resentment or cynicism. Ian looked away before Jay caught him staring. The comparison to Sam was unfair and unhelpful—Jay had never given him reason to doubt his sincerity. The awe in Jay’s expression was endearing, though Ian tried not to find it as charming as he did.
"We're making good time," Ian observed, glancing at his watch. He spoke to the driver through the intercom. "We have some extra time. Take us through Riverside Park  before heading to the office building."
The detour revealed a street lined with what could only be described as architectural masterpieces. Victorian mansions competed with modern estates, all surrounded by grounds that resembled public parks. Jay tried not to gawk, but the sight was spectacular.
One particular mansion caught his attention—a limestone structure with towers and bay windows that looked like something from a historical drama. The grounds included a golf course and a guest house larger than Jay's entire apartment building.
"Impressive," Jay said quietly.
Ian didn't comment, but Jay thought he detected the hint of a smile on his face.
The Calder Wetherby Trust occupied a building that seemed to have been transported from nineteen-century. Dark wood panelling, crystal chandeliers, and soft rugs created an atmosphere of established wealth that made Turner & Associates' modern style seem almost casual by comparison.
Jay's eyes widened slightly as they were escorted through the entrance hall, but he quickly composed himself and followed Ian's lead toward the executive offices.
Ken Wetherby greeted them in a reception area dominated by oil paintings of stern-looking men in formal attire. He was perhaps sixty, with silver hair and confident bearing that came from generations of inherited authority.
"Ian, excellent to see you," Ken said, extending his hand.
"Ken, thank you for accommodating our schedule. This is Jay Hall, my assistant."
The introduction was polite but perfunctory. Jay understood immediately that his role was to remain largely invisible while the important conversation took place.
They moved to Wetherby's office, where leather-bound books lined the walls and afternoon light filtered through leaded glass windows. Jay positioned himself at a small side table with his laptop while Ian and Wetherby sat on armchairs near a fireplace.
The meeting that followed was unlike anything Jay had expected. Instead of financial analysis or contract negotiations, the conversation meandered through topics that seemed almost social. Tennis, mutual acquaintances, a brief discussion about whiskey that led Wetherby to pour drinks despite the early afternoon hour.
Jay typed diligently, capturing what little business content emerged from the discussion. A  mention of "exploring opportunities in the emerging markets," and a casual comment about "moving forward with the infrastructure investment we discussed."
Each seemingly offhand remark involved sums that made Jay's head spin. Hundreds of millions addressed as casually as discussing weekend plans. When Ken mentioned expanding their portfolio by another two hundred millions, Jay's typing intensified slightly, but he showed no other reaction to figures that would have represented astronomical sums to most people.
After about one hour, Ian glanced at his watch and made polite excuses about not taking up more of Wetherby's time. The farewell was warm but brief, with promises to "do this again soon" and instructions to "give my regards to Leo."
"That was more politics than business," Ian explained as they walked back to the car. "But sometimes these relationship visits are more important than formal presentations. Wetherby likes to feel that his account receives personal attention from senior leadership."
Jay processed this information as they got back into the car. The meeting had been essentially a social call dressed up as business—an expensive way to maintain goodwill with a client who could afford to demand such treatment.

Scene 2
Tingling
"Since we're out during lunch hours, we might as well make the most of it. There's a restaurant nearby that's worth trying," Ian said.
"That sounds good."
The car delivered them to an establishment that epitomized the district's sophisticated atmosphere. The building was elegant, with the entrance marked by brass fixtures and flowering plants.
The server led them to a private dining room with views of a small garden courtyard. Jay felt the unease of being surrounded by extravagance beyond his reach, but Ian’s relaxed demeanour helped him ease into the experience.
"Order whatever appeals to you," Ian said, scanning the menu. "It's a business expense."
